And you should take a cannula of bronze, or iron, or of a vulture's feather, and place it over the wart or corn. Then pour a small amount of hot water into the cannula; hold your hand, and revolve it with pressure of your hand for a short time, so that the edge of the cannula is pressed into the base of the corn, and the hot water finds its way to the depth, to the base of the corn; and let the patient wait for a while on the scorching of the hot water for an hour.
Then leave him, for the corn is eradicated with its roots.
Do this with each of them, one by one, until you have dealt with all that are on the body.
Then treat them after they are eradicated, with that which generates flesh from ointments.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in Tremore.
When tremor occurs in someone from cold in a nerve, or from quartan fever, or otherwise: then it is necessary that you cauterize it with four cauteries, or five over the spinal vertebrae, and between each vertebra with a cautery, and in his chest with a cautery, and over his stomach with a cautery.
For tremor ceases with the cautery of olive oil, and the health of cold illness is hastened.
Concerning the method of cauterizing Albathir.
Chapter LVII.
Sometimes foul Albathir, that is, pustules, are expelled in the body, which are made from corrupted gross matter.
Therefore, when they appear, it is necessary first to cauterize every pustule on the head with a fine cautery made from myrtle wood, whose tip is heated with fire. Or with the root of long aristolochia. Or with a lenticular cautery.
And sometimes they are cauterized at the beginning of their expulsion in this way: and they do not increase; and the superfluity that makes them is broken, and it is healed from them.
However, it is necessary that it be done after evacuation with phlebotomy.
Concerning the method of cauterization in a flow of blood from an artery in a wound.
Often a flow of blood occurs from an artery that has been cut in a wound, which occurs externally, or in the incision of an abscess, or cautery of a limb, and similar things: and it is difficult to cut it off.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then quickly apply your index finger to the opening of the artery, and press it firmly until the blood is obstructed beneath your finger, and nothing escapes from it.
Then place small olive-shaped cauteries, and many large ones in number, into the fire, and blow over them until they become very hot.
Then take one of them, either small or large, according to the wound and the place where the artery is ruptured, and place the cautery upon the vein itself, after you have quickly removed your finger; and hold the cautery until the blood is cut off.
If it is expelled when you raise your finger from the opening of the artery, and you extinguish the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from the cauteries prepared in the fire.
Therefore do not cease to do this with one after another, until the blood is cut off.
And beware, lest you burn the nerve which is there, and another harm befall the sick person.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, then it is not possible to cut it off, especially when the artery is large, unless by one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we have said. Or with extraction, when it is not extracted. For when it is extracted, its extremities contract, and the blood is cut off. Or it must be tied with a strong ligature. Or medicines of a nature to cut off the blood may be placed upon it, and it may be bound with a suitable constricting cushion.
However, whoever performs its excision with a ligature and constriction with a cloth, or by applying burning substances, and similar things, does not entirely benefit it, except rarely.
If, however, this should happen to someone, and a physician or medicine is not present, then let him begin with the application of the index finger over the opening of the vein itself, as we have described, and with its very firm constriction, until the blood is obstructed from the upper part of the wound, and above the artery. And the finger should not be removed, keeping it upon it with very cold water, until the blood is congealed and thickened, and cut off.
And meanwhile, let him consider what it needs, from a cautery or from medicine.
Concerning incision, perforation, and bloodletting in wounds, and similar matters.
We have already said in the First Chapter, that every illness in which cautery with fire and burning medicine is appropriate, and its occasions, its causes, and its instruments, and the forms of cauteries.
And I have placed there chapters from head to foot.
And I shall proceed in this chapter along the same path: so that the seeker may have his question relieved.
And before I begin that, it is necessary that you, children, learn that in this chapter there is something from the terror of what is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And because of that, it is necessary that there be more caution in it, because in the operation that is in this chapter, very often evacuation occurs with blood, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or incision on an abscess, or opening of an exit, or the healing of a wound, or the extraction of an arrow, or an incision on a stone, and similar to these: from all of which tremor and fear are associated, and death occurs in many of them.
And I warn you, lest that in which there is hesitation fall upon you, for sometimes the ways of men with the ways of illnesses fall upon you in this art. For among them is he who is angry with his illness, and death is light to him; because of the severity he finds from his illness and its long malice. And with illness there is fear that signifies death.
And among them is he who gives his pipe and enriches you with it, hoping for health, and his illness is harmful.
Therefore, you should not consent to him who comes to you from among them, whose manner is such. And let your caution be more intense than your greed and your rapacity. And do not presume upon anything from it unless after certain knowledge, which verifies to you that to which praiseworthy succession has arrived.
And use in the treatment of all your sick persons, prognosis and indication of that to which health is suitably.
For in that is help for you in the acquisition of fame and glory, and of memory and praise.
May God inspire in you, O children, His understanding, and may He not remove from you direction and inspiration, for it is in His hand.
And I indeed have ordered this chapter, distinguishing by chapters, according to what preceded in the chapter on the cautery, from the neck to the feet: so that your quest for what you want from it, and what you need, may be alleviated.
On the treatment of Water that collects in the heads of children.
Chapter 1.
Know that this ailment frequently occurs to children at birth, or when the midwife tightly grasps the child's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it also occurs from a hidden cause that is not known.
And indeed I have not seen this illness in others than in children, and death has hastened to all whom I have seen afflicted by it; therefore, it seemed to me to omit an operation on it.
And indeed I saw one child of them whose head was filled with water, and the head increased in size every day, until he could not sit on himself, due to the size of his head; and the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture indeed either collects between the skin and the bone, or collects under the bone above the sifac.
And if there is moisture between the skin and the bone, and the abscess is small, then it is necessary to make a single fissure in the middle of the head, in width; and let the length of the fissure be about two knots, until the moisture runs out.
And this is the form of a lancet.
If, therefore, the moisture is more increased, and the abscess is larger, then place those two fissures, according to this form.
And if the moisture is under the bone, and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on every side, and water is pressed out when you press it with your hand towards the interior, and that is not hidden from you, then it is necessary to make three fissures in the middle of the head, according to this method.
And after the incision, extract all the moisture. Then bind the fissures with cloths and poultices. Then apply a fomentation of wine and oil to it until the fifth day.
Then loosen the bandage, and treat the wound with tents and ointments.
And do not allow the head to be bound with equality. And feed the weak person with all dry food of little moisture, until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And another method of incision is that you should consider where the size of the abscess and the collection of water appear.
Because sometimes there is more in the back of the head, or in the forehead, or in the right, or in the left.
Therefore, ascend with the incision where the abscess and the filling of water appear to you: and cut it according to what is possible for you.
And observe, lest you cut an artery, and a flow of blood occur, and the weak person die from that flow with the evacuation of moisture.
Concerning the incision of arteries that are behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When catarrhs occur to someone in the eyes or in the chest, and it is prolonged, and treatment with medicines does not help, then the ultimate treatment for it is the incision of these two arteries.
Therefore, when you wish to cut them, it is necessary that the sick person shave their head. Then rub the area with a rough cloth, so that the arteries appear. Then let the sick person tighten their neck with any cloth they have. Then consider where the vein beats. And the places of both are the places that are depressed behind the ears: and rarely is it hidden, except in some people.
Then mark with ink on each: and cut each with a scalpel carefully on both sides, the incision reaching the bone: and let it be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the scalpel under the artery, and cut it upwards with the incision.
And let the length of the incision be like two joined fingers.
For when the vein is cut, blood flows out with a pulsating discharge, which leaps to the interior with frequent jumps.
If however the artery does not appear to the senses, then it is necessary that you measure from the ear the amount of space of three fingers. Then mark with ink, and cut until you reach the bone.
And that which it is necessary to emit of blood, is six ounces, and perhaps you emit from it more, or less. All of that according to the amount which appears to you from the strength of the sick person and the fullness of his artery.
Then consider the wound. Then if anything remains on the bone from the sifac, then cut it, so that a hot abscess does not occur: then bind the wound with strips of linen cloth.
Then treat it with ointment, until it is healed.
Concerning the extraction of arteries, which are in the temples.
Chapter III.
When a man suffers from chronic hemicrania, or acute catarrhs due to acrid humors, and heat in the temporal muscles, or a severe, chronic throbbing, and similar afflictions: and is treated with the methods of medical care, but it does not help: then we are skilled in these illnesses for the extraction of arteries from the temples, or their cauterization, as we have described.
And the method of operation in extracting them is, that the weak man should shave the hairs that are on the temples, and aim at the artery apparent in the temple. For it indeed shows itself to you by its pulse, and rarely hides itself except in a few people, or by intense cold.
If it hides itself from you, then the weak man shall bind his head with any cloth of his: then rub the place with a rough cloth. Or steam the place with warm water, until it appears to you by a clear appearance. Then take the spatula, which I formed a little before. Then scrape the skin with it easily, until you reach the artery. Then insert a hook into it, and pull it upwards, until you pull it out of the skin, and make it escape from the layers beneath it on every side.
If however the artery is slender: then wrap it with the tip of the hook. Then cut a portion from it according to the amount by which its ends will lengthen and contract, and so that no blood flow occurs. For if it is not cut, and if it is not severed, the blood will not ascend completely.
Then evacuate from the blood six ounces, down to three.
If the artery is large: then it is necessary to tie it in two places with a strong, double thread, of silk or of albohod cords, so that putrefaction does not quickly occur at the wound before its consolidation, and that blood flow does not occur.
Then cut away the excess that is between the two ligatures. Do that at that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery, up to the bone, with a knife cautery, until its ends are cut, and it will stand in place of the same work.
And it is a better work, unless the weak person has a fever, or is of a hot temperament. Because cautery is from among those things that help in ending moistnesses. Therefore it is a better help.
And after the extraction of the arteries, the place must be filled with old cotton, and well-made cushions placed upon it.
And after the solution, treat it with dry medicines that cause flesh to grow, and with bandages, until it is healed.
If meanwhile, during your work, a flow of blood from an artery occurs, then hasten to cut it off with a cautery, or to fill the place with zegi: and bind your hand tightly, until the blood is cut off. If none of those are present for you, then place your finger on the place, until the blood congeals. And apply the place with water of extreme cold, until the sharpness subsides, and it is bound as necessary.
And because what is lighter and easier to extract than an artery, is, to cauterize a vein with this cautery having two blades: and let the sharpness of the two blades be similar to an almond. However, they are much less sharp than two blades. Because if they are sharp, like two blades, the cold hurries both: and they do not cut the flesh with speed. Therefore, since there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire is held in them, and the flesh is cut quickly: with this therefore the work is better than any other work, and lighter, and easier.
And this is the form of cautery.
And let the space between the two blades be the thickness of a finger, after you mark two places with ink: and lay both very hot until they reach the bone, and cut the vein in two places, so that what is between its extremities is lengthened. For they do not heal completely with this cautery.
Chapter IV.
Concerning the treatment of acute and persistent running of tears from the eyes.
When tears are persistent, and their flow is from veins that are on the outer surface of the head's skull; and no medicinal treatment with medications helps them, and you see the patient's face already red, and he feels a crawling sensation on his forehead, like the crawling of ants; and his eyes are weakened, moist, so that their hairs are already corroded with corrosion; and his eyelids are inflamed from the intensity of the tears: then the ancients needed this operation in their treatment, and they named that treatment, with the Knife.
And it is, that you should instruct the patient to shave the hairs that are on his forehead. Then make three equally spaced incisions along the length of the forehead; and let the length of each incision be about two fingers; and one incision should be equidistant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second slightly offset from the movement of the temples, and the third on the other side; and beware of cutting the arteries that are on both sides; and keep your hand away from the junction of the two jawbones, and let the space of each incision be the length of three fingers joined together; and have a piece of sponge prepared, or many dry cloths, with which you dry the blood.
Then introduce a probe with two sharp ends, whose following form is this. From the incision that is on the temple side, up to the second incision; and scrape with it all the skin that is between the two incisions, with the sifac, which is over the bone. Then do that also from the middle incision up to the second incision. Then remove this probe, and introduce into the first incision again another instrument, called a sharp knife, smooth on one side, not sharp on the other side. And this is its form.
And place its sharp side towards the flesh attached with the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone: and push it, until it reaches the middle fissure: and cut with it all the vessels, which descend from the head to the eyes: except that the incision continues up to that which appears of the skin.
Then do that in the middle fissure up to the other fissure.
And after an equal quantity of blood runs out, express the places from the clots of congealed blood.
Then place in every fissure a lint of old linen, and place on top of them pads soaked in wine, or oil, or vinegar. And see that a hot abscess does not occur.
And on the third day loosen the bandage, and administer much infusion with lukewarm water. Then treat it with basilicon ointment, and the other things with which wounds are treated: until it is healed.
Chapter V.
Concerning the treatment of acute tears and catarrhs to the eyes from the interior of the head.
When acute, sharp, persistent catarrhs befall someone, and you see his eyes weakened, small, and their appearance already impaired; and the eyelids ulcerated, and the hairs fall from them, and there is sharp, piercing pain deep within the head, accompanied by sneezing; then, from these occurrences, understand that these matters, and the catarrhs, do not arise except from many veins.
And the better treatment in them is this. And it is to command the sick person to shave his forehead. Then, make one incision in the middle of his forehead, or a little further along the breadth, and begin with the incision from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision reach the bone; and move your hand away from the temple muscles, which move during mastication, until the bone is exposed; and you will dry up all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with old cloth, or with flax strips. Then bind it above with pledgets; and soak the pledgets with wine and oil, so that a hot abscess may not occur.
And when you loosen it, and see that the hot abscess is already lessening, then it is necessary to rub the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that causes flesh to grow; as if you take two parts of wheat flour, and four parts of rosin; and prepare a plaster from them, and apply it to the growth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter VI.
Concerning the treatment of that which falls into the Ear.
Whatever falls into the ear is one of four kinds: either a mineral stone, or something like a stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as a chickpea and bean; or it is a liquid substance, such as water, vinegar, and similar things; or some animal.
Therefore, when a stone, or a kind of stone, from those which do not adhere to, nor grow in, the ear, falls into the ear, expose the ear to the sun. If you then see the stone, then instill into the ear something of violet oil, or sesame oil. Then administer its extraction, and make it sneeze with a condiment, and close the nostrils at the time of sneezing, after you place in a circle around the ear scraps of cloth, or wool, and extend the ear upward.
For it often goes out with this treatment.
If however it does not go out, then administer its extraction with tweezers, that is, small pincers: whose form is this.
If therefore it goes out with the tweezers, it is good.
And if not, then administer its extraction with a thin single-hooked instrument of few folds.
If therefore it does not go out through that, then make a tube of bronze, and insert the end of the tube into the ear canal a long way, and fill that which is around the tube with softened wax mixed with oil, so that there is no way for wind except through the tube: then extract it with your wind by strong suction.
For often it goes out with that which we have recounted. If however it does not go out in this way, then take a small amount of birdlime, or glue, that is, prepared glue, with which birds are caught, and place it at the end of a stick after you have wrapped a suitable bit of cloth around it; then insert it into the ear canal with ease, after you have dried the ear from moisture.
If therefore it does not go out with all that we have recounted, then hurry to incision, before a hot abscess or spasm occurs.
And the method of incision is, that a phlebotomist should first open a vein in the head, and extract blood from him according to the measure of his strength. Then make the sick person sit between your hands, and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear near his fat in a low place of it; and let the incision be of a crescent shape, until you reach the stone; then remove it with whatever instruments you can.
Then suture the incision quickly, and treat it until it heals.
If the thing falling into the ear is one of the grains that grow and swell, then administer its extraction.
If, however, it does not yield to you for removal, then take a thin, slender spatula, according to this form.
And administer it with the removal of that type of grain that falls into the ear, and do not do it when you think the grain is already moistened by ear vapor, until you put many fragments; then extract them with hooks, or with a fine probe, or with suction, as we have said, for its extraction is made easier.
However, if water enters the ear, it is necessary for the patient to use sneezing, first of all; and already he has filled his ear with some old cotton; and he is lying on the ear in which the water is. If therefore it emerges through that, it is good.
And if not, then take many stones, according to the length of a finger, thin, smooth, and heat them with fire for a short time, and let the patient insert one of them into his ear's opening; and let him jump on one foot from that side, and strike with another stone on the stone that is in the ear. Therefore, he should not cease doing that with stone and stone until all the water emerges.
And now the water emerges in such a way that you take one from papyrus, or from reed, and insert one end of it into the ear; and ignite the other end with fire until most of it is burned. Then again another reed, and do that many times, until all the water emerges; and extract it with a cannula according to what was previously stated about the stone.
In extracting an animal that has entered it, if it is of small body, such as a flea, and similar to it, treat it as I said in the division.
But if it is of large body, which is apparent to the senses, then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks; and its removal is easier than all things that are held in the ear.
In extracting a worm that has been generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we said in the division, and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment does not help: then it is necessary that you examine the ear in the sun. If therefore anything of the worms appears there, then extract it with forceps, or with fine hooks.
If however nothing appears there, then take a cannula, which has this form, narrow at the bottom, wide at the top; and introduce the narrow end into the ear according to the amount that the patient tolerates it; then suck with it by strong suction. Do this repeatedly, until all the worms come out.
If however they do not yield to you for removal, then coat the circumference of the cannula with wax, as I said in the stone [section].
If they do not come out with what we have said, then administer the distillations which the ancients have already experienced in killing worms, and you will find it in the treatise on distillations.
And let your pouring of oils and medicines into the ear be with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. As you see, make it itself from silver, or bronze, narrowed at the bottom, in which there is a small hole, wide above.
Let therefore the impelling part that is in the concavity of the cannula be made of bronze properly. And if you wish, take a ray, and wrap linen around its extremity with a suitable wrapping; then pour oil, or juice, or whatever medicine you wish from these into the cannula; and let it be in the ear; then insert the ray with the linen from above, and squeeze it with your hand with moderate pressure, until the oil is driven into the cavity of the ear, and the sick person feels it inwardly; and that which is poured into the ear should be warmed with fire for a short time, and beware lest the substance being distilled into it be very cold, for medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter VII.
Concerning the treatment of sudden ear blockage.
Sometimes infants emerge from their mothers' wombs unperforated.
And sometimes blockage in the ear canal occurs in people due to a wound, or a growth of flesh in it.
And this blockage sometimes occurs deep within the ear cavity, and is not accompanied by visual signs; and sometimes it occurs close to the exterior of the cavity, and is accompanied by visual signs.
Therefore, that which occurs deep within the ear, and is not perceived by sight, is in most cases difficult to cure.
And in that which is accompanied by sight, it is necessary to place the patient's ear in the sun, and observe it; then, if you see an apparent blockage, open it with a thin spatula, the form of which is as follows: its end should have a small width, with one part being pointed; and the rest of the spatula should be of two smooth sides, so as not to injure the ear.
If therefore the blockage is from flesh grown in the ear canal, then grasp it with a fine hook and cut it away with considerable ease until all the flesh is removed.
If the blockage is deep within the ear, then take a thin, smooth rod, and warm it briefly with fire; then insert it into the ear canal.
If therefore you sense a blockage with the patient experiencing impaired hearing, attempt to open it with care, and be careful not to injure the ear nerves.
Then apply a bandage to the ear, according to the size of the opening you have created, wrapped in Egyptian ointment; do this for several days until you are certain of the wound's healing.
Or take a bandage and dip it in rose water, and sprinkle crushed zegi powder on it, and use it.
If you see that the ear has already become suppurating, then it is necessary to remove the bandage, and apply another bandage wrapped in a cloth made with rose oil, until the suppurating heat subsides; then treat it until it heals.
If, however, a flow of blood occurs, then immerse a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear; and use the remaining treatment, which stops the flow of blood described in the treatise on distillations.
Concerning the treatment of the eyelids.
Chapter VIII.
These inflammations that occur in the eyelids are sometimes moist and sometimes dry.
Therefore, you must grasp them with forceps or with a hook, and cut them with a scalpel from their roots. Then place powdered lead over the affected area.
If blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a fine, lens-shaped cautery.
And to cauterize them is better after incision. For they often return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned, and they do not return.
Concerning the treatment of granulations of the eyelids of the eye.
Chapter IX.
Often something occurs in the eyelids that is similar to granulation in its strength and hardness, and on account of this it is called Granulation: and it is an aggregation of thick moisture in the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
Therefore the operation on them is that you should consider, then, if it is a granulation growing on that which appears on the eyelid, and it moves to every part, and its condition is easy.
Therefore make a slit over it by its width. Then scrape it away from every part with ease, until it is gone. Then suspend it with a hook and cut it off.
If however its excision is not prepared for you, unless after you pierce the eyelid with a small incision, then that harms the sick person in some way.
If however the slit is large, then gather it with stitches and care for it until it is healed.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For ointment restores it and causes it to flesh over.
And if the granulation is sloping towards the inner surface of the ear, then invert the eyelid, and suspend the granulation with a hook; besides which you need an incision, and press it from every side.
If the eyelid is extended by an incision, that will not harm it in any way.
Then wash the eye after the incision of the granulation with salt water, and treat the place with that which causes it to flesh over, until the sick person is healed.
Concerning the treatment of Axirnath of the eyelids.
Chapter X.
Axirnath is a fat that is generated in the tissues of the upper eyelid; it most often occurs in children. It burdens their eyes, and they suffer catarrhs in the mornings, and cannot look at the sunlight, because tears flow to them, and because of this you see them lying on their faces constantly, and they sleep on them. Their eyelids are damp under their eyebrows, which have now been covered with swelling.  The prominence of the Axirnath is visible to the eye, and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the Axirnath between them.
And the method of its operation is that the sick person should place their head in your bosom.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare strings on it, and make a circle from that string according to the amount that contains the Axirnath on all sides. Then place it on that, and press it with your fingers on all sides, so that the Axirnath is collected in the middle of the circle.
Then find in the middle of that moisture, with a lancet, the fissures along its width, and let not the fissure be greater than that which is made in phlebotomy in depth.
Or it is necessary that you pierce the whole skin, until you reach the Axirnath, for in many dispositions the Axirnath proceeds from the fissure according to the shape of a white fatty fragment.
Therefore extract it with the cloth, which you have inserted between your fingers on the outside: and you turn your hand to the right and left, until it is dispersed; and beware, lest you add to the fissure, so that you do not reach the eye with the lancet and injure it.
If however the Axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the fissure, you shall add a little to the fissure with ease, until the Axirnath proceeds. Then extract it, as we have said.
Then immerse the cloths in vinegar and water, and place them upon the place, and bind it with bandages.
And there are men who grind salt and place it in the concavity of the fissure, so that what remains of that moisture liquefies.
Then treat it until it is healed.
If however a hot abscess occurs in the place, then treat it with soothing plasters until it is healed.
Concerning the methods for Ataxmir of the eye, that is, when the eyelid of the eye softens, whence it comes about that it wrinkles as it should not.
Chapter XI.
When superfluous hairs grow in the eyelids, beyond the natural hairs, and increase in number: then they harm the eye, and cause various illnesses such as constant weeping, and softening of the eyelids, and white discoloration, until it becomes the cause of the destruction of the eye.
And indeed Ataxmir of the eye is treated according to four methods. Or by cautery with fire. Or with a sharp medicine, according to what has been stated in the chapter on cautery. Or Ataxmir is treated by incision and suture. Or by tubes, according to what I will say.
You should place the patient's head in your bosom. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If therefore it turns, it is good. And if not: insert a needle into it, on which there is thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle with the thread penetrate from above, and let it be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the edge of the eyelid where the hairs grow; and pull the thread upward with the eyelid, and turn it with a rod.
Then find in the inner part of the eyelid under the added hairs with a spatula sliding from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct.
Then pull out the thread, and place a small cushion of cotton, or cloth, under the eyelid; then mark on the eyelid with ink, that be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that the shape be according to the size and elevation of the eyelid; for it is already diversified in humans.
For among them are those who need a large amount to be cut from the eyelid, according to the amount that softens from the eyelid.
Then with a spatula, find above the two lines you have marked, and begin from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct, and let the incision be one near the natural hairs, like the thickness of a ray.
Then insert the hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then scrape off the entire thing. Then bring together the two edges of the wound with a needle and fine wool thread; and wipe away the blood, and make any excess thread adhere to the eyebrows with some kind of adhesive, if you wish to do so; and if not, do not worry.
Then leave the suture and threads until three or four days; afterward, treat it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without sutures, and treat it with something that dries and contracts it. For the eyelid is raised at the sealing and joining of the wound, but will be better.
The ancients taught this method of treatment.
However, there is care for the sick person in it, and it is from good operation, and there is no fear in it.
And another method is in Ataxmir also: and it is, that you mark on the eyelid a shape, which is like the shape of a myrtle leaf, as we have narrated.
Then raise the eyelid with three separate hooks, or joined together, according to this following shape, then cut what is superfluous with small scissors, according to this shape, with equal incision.
If however it is not possible for you to hold the hook, and it is not equal: then take a needle on which there is thread, and insert it in the middle of the shape; and insert another thread near the larger tear duct, and a second thread near the smaller tear duct; and gather the threads between your fingers with equality.
Then raise your hand with equal elevation; and raise the skin over which the cotum is marked, as we have narrated.
Then bring together the two lips of the wound with suture, and treat it until it heals.
If, however, an abscess occurs at your incision, or your fissure: soothe that abscess with caerot, and similar things, from soothing ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid, even that its hairs are turned.
Therefore, it is necessary that you use in it what we said about the incision: but suture is more artificial in both. Concerning the treatment of Ataxmir with reeds.
And it is, that you turn the eyelid, and cut the fissure that is within, according to what I have described. Then skillfully make two reeds, or two thin pieces of wood, whose length is according to the length of the eyelid, and their width is less than the width of the spatula; and you have already incised at the ends of each on both sides where you hold the threads.
Then carefully place each one upon that which is left over from the eyelid of the eye, and tightly bind the two reeds on both sides with a strong ligature, and leave it for a number of days. For the tightened skin will die, and turn black, and rot, until it falls off by itself.
If it is delayed, then cut it with scissors. Then care for it until it heals.
When it is consolidated, the eyelid will be lifted: and the hairs will not prick.
And this is the form of two canes, which you will make according to this figure, and quantity in length and width.
Chapter XII.
Concerning the lifting of pricking hairs in the eye with a needle.
The operation in this is that you take a thin needle, and introduce into it a fine, smooth silk thread. Then tie its ends, and each end with a very small knot: and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then place a separate fine thread underneath it in the Alinxotati, and tie its ends if you wish.
Then let the sick person place their head in your lap, and be close to the sun so that the operation is clear, because it is a subtle or minute operation.
Then introduce the needle with the alinxotati into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you pull it out above the natural hair.
Then pull the hair out above the alinxotati, and insert that hair into the alinxotati; if it is one, or two, or three, not more.
Then draw your hand with it until the hair, along with the alinxotati, emerges from the eyelid with the natural hair.
However, if the alinxotati is pulled out and the hair does not emerge with it, you will pull the alinxotati downwards, with the thread which you have placed in it, until the alinxotati emerges from the upper opening, and you see it with the eye's vision.  Then you will pull the alinxotati and the thread, and remove both, and close the eye, and leave it closed for two days or three, until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, then either associate a long hair from the natural hairs with it, and lift it with it, or leave it until it lengthens after a period, and then lift it.
Chapter XIII.
When this Alxatrati happens to the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this Alxatrati is either natural or accidental.
Accidental therefore it is made from the consolidation of a wound, or a section, or cautery, and things similar to those.
And the method of treatment in it is, that you find that consolidation, and that you separate its lips, and place in what is between them a linen bandage, and tie it, until it heals.
And it is not necessary that you use in its treatment things that dry and constrict: for if you do that, the Alxatrati returns worse than it was: indeed, use in it things that soften, such as mallow: and embrocate with water, in which cooked is marshmallow, flaxseed: and with dyaquilon ointment, which is liquefied with some of the oils: and wrap with it a linen bandage, and treat with it.
And the treasure of your treatment is, that you strive with all your ingenuity, that it does not consolidate, according to the form which was at first.
And this indeed operation which we have said, is not except a certain rectification of Alxatrati, that the form of the place does not return according to what it was entirely.
Concerning the treatment of Alxatrati which is done on the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkling or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter XIV.
This Alxatrati, which occurs lower down, is that which is truly called Alxatrati. And it is natural, and it is accidental.
And the accidental kind results from a wound, or a fissure, or a cautery, and things similar to those.
And the method of operation in it is that you should take a needle, in which there is double thread, and dip it into the flesh, and make it penetrate from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread reaches the two extremities of the flesh.
Then extend the flesh upwards with the needle, and cut it with a broad spatula. If therefore the shape of the eyelid returns as it should, it is good.
And if not, take a probe, and place it in the fissure, and turn the eyelid with it: and split two fissures on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the ends of the two fissures be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and a corner is made from them: until when they are gathered together their shape becomes this figure, and is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh by the amount that is its sharp lower side, from that which follows the eyelid. Then bring together the separated parts; sew it with a thread of wool.
Then treat them with what we said, of softening medicines, and bandages, until they are healed.
And if a laceration occurs from a fissure, and suture, or cautery: then it is necessary that you split a flat fissure under the hairs of the eyelids, even according to what preceded. Then separate between the two lips with a bandage, according to what we said.
And the sum of the discourse on the treatment of Alxatrati, when it is above or below, is that the operation should proceed according to the preparation of the form of the Alxatrati.
For it itself has many varieties in form, and a prudent craftsman prepares the remedy in whatever way possible for it, until it returns the figure to the natural form, or near it, and the patient himself, according to what is suitable to him from the operation and instruments in many arrangements.
Chapter XV.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva, or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation occurs in many people, so that the upper eyelid consolidates with the white of the eye and prevents it from its natural operations.
Therefore, you should insert the tip of the probe under the eyelid and lift it upwards; or extend it with a hook; then, cut the adhesion with a fine spatula, which should not have the sharpness of other spatulas, but be somewhat blunt, so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula is removed, and does not injure the eye with its sharpness.  Your incision should be such that you scrape off the adhesion easily, until the eyelid returns to its natural form, and the entire adhesion is healed.
Then, pour salty water into the eye, washing it with water; or distill into it from a sieve, a solution of ziniar in water.
Then, separate the eyelid and the eye with linen thread; and place over the eye wool soaked with egg white; and after three days, use the sieve and consolidants, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the toenail and the prominence of the tear-producing flesh.
Chapter XVI.
Know that the toenail is according to two ways. Or it is nervous: and it is similar to hard, fine sifac. Or it is not nervous, similar to frozen white moisture: which when it touches iron, or you try to grasp it with a hook, it is severed, and the hook does not stick to it.
And both species do not begin, unless from the larger tear duct, until it goes gradually, until it covers the sight, and obstructs the light, and the movement of the eye.
And the method of operating on it is: that the sick person should place his head in your lap. Then open his eyes, and raise the eyelid with your hand. Then collect the nail with a hook of few folds, and extend it upwards. Then take a needle, and insert into it a hair from a horse's hair, or an ox's, or a cow's: and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle a little, and fix it in the middle of the nail: and pierce it with the needle, and tie the nail with the thread, and extend it upwards: and scrape with the hair the side of the nail which follows the pupil, as if you were sawing it with the hair up to its end.
Then cut the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a thin spatula, or with small scissors: and leave the natural tear duct tissue so that a constant flow of tears does not occur from its incision.
And sometimes the nail is extended with only a hook, or with a thread, as we have said. Then it is scraped with a gentle spatula, of which this is the shape.
And be careful that the spatula does not touch the horny coat, and that a rupture occurs in it, and the pupil is pierced.
Then, after the incision, place some crushed salt, or a preparation made from ziniar, in the eye, and leave it until the next day. Then treat it with whatever is appropriate, until it is healed.
If the nail is not nervous and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor is a hook fixed in it, then this type of nail is often treated with medicines and heals as I have described in the division.
If you wish to cut it, then open the sick person's eye, and take a thin, gentle, weak-pointed spatula, according to this form. And scrape the nail from above with care. If you see that it thins and breaks, and the scraping has imprinted on it, then distil from your hour into the eye a sieve made from ziniar, or from a red sieve, or ground salt; and bandage the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the operation on it until the whole of it is removed. Unless there is a hot abscess in the eye; for then leave it alone, and treat the abscess until it heals.
Then repeat the operation on it with the scraping, until it heals.
If, however, the fleshy prominence of the tear duct causes a great injury to the eye, it should be suspended by a hook and a portion of it incised; and do not complete the incision, so that the flow of tears is not interrupted.
Then instill into the tear duct a red dye, or one made from ziniari, until it heals.
Concerning the incision of Alguardenic and the flesh grown in the eye.
Chapter XVII.
Sometimes in some people's eyes, a reddish flesh grows, either compounded or superimposed, until it covers the pupil, or approaches it, or presses against the eyelids, and perhaps turns the eyelids outward, and resembles a rose-hip.
And the treatment in it is also that the infirm person should place his head in your lap. Then open his eyes, and gather all that flesh with suitable hooks for it: and hold it with one forceps, or tweezers.
Then cut into the flesh first, until all of it is finished with a scalpel. And be careful of the eye, so that you do not injure it during the operation: and let your incision, with which you cut it, be either with a scalpel, or with a spatula, as we described in the incision of a hoof; or with small shears, with which a scab is gathered, as its form will appear after this chapter.
When therefore your incision is complete, and the whole of the alghardenici is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt, or distill into it a red powder, and similar corrosives from medicines: and place over the eye externally a poultice of white of egg, so that it is protected from a hot abscess.
If anything of the alguardenici remains, and hot blood dominates you, and you fear a hot abscess, then leave the eye and treat it with that which calms the abscess.
Then repeat the operation on it again until it is healed.
And similarly do with the added flesh that occurs in the eye of this type.
However, it is necessary that you avoid the treatment of any weak eye by a natural or accidental way, by operating with iron, and do not put your hand in any of these operations, until you see that the limb is tolerant of that operation.
Concerning the collection of Sebel from the eye.
Chapter XVIII.
Sebel are red veins that are woven over the eye, and hinder the eye's function of sight; and they weaken the eye with the length of days.
Therefore, it is necessary for you first to consider whether the eye in which the Sebel is present is strong and has no illness other than the Sebel; then collect the Sebel from it.
And it is that you should instruct the ill person to place their head in your lap.
Then suspend those veins with one hook, or two according to your judgment; and let the hook be a thin, double hook, according to this form, or let there be two hooks graded into one body, according to this form.
Then collect those veins with fine scissors with care, and wipe away the blood hour after hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye and they are dissolved; and beware that you do not injure the eye with the tips of the forceps.
And let your operation be in the midday sun, and be very firm in your operation, so that you do not cut anything but those veins.
And when you have finished, then instill into the eye either green or red sief, so that its sharpness corrodes what remains of the sebile.
If, however, it is not possible for you to collect it all in that hour, then plaster the eye with that which soothes the pain of the eye; and leave it for several days until its pain subsides; and protect it from a hot abscess; then repeat over it according to the same method until it is healed.
And this is the shape of the forceps.
On the reduction of a displaced nasal bone.
Chapter XIX.
Physicians call this a fistula, which you treat with a cautery, or with a burning, sharp medicine, according to the preceding account of it.
And if it does not heal, then there is no skill in it: unless you pierce above the abscess at its origin, and extract all the moisture and pus within it until the bone is exposed.
When the bone is exposed, and you see corruption or blackness upon it, then scrape it with an instrument of this form.
And it is called a "rough head" instrument.
Let it be made of tempered iron, and its head be round like a ring, pointed with a file's point, or with a fine-pointed aliskilfer's point: and place it upon the corrupted area of the bone.
Then rotate it between your fingers, and press your hand a little while, until you know that the corruption has been scraped away: and do this several times.
Then restore the place with drying, astringent medicines.
If therefore the place is consolidated, and flesh grows there, and the course of pus is cut off; and it remains for forty days, and is not renewed, and does not become suppurated, and no accident occurs in it, then you know that it is now healed.
And if not, then there is no skill in it, except to return the fistula to the nostril's opening, according to this form.
And it is, that you detect the second bone with iron, or with a sharp medicine; and when the bone is detected, then take a drill according to this form, let its iron end be triangular; and its wood be a pyramid tapering to the end, as you see.
Then place it upon the bone itself: and let it be near the tear duct: and move your hand slightly away from the eye. Then turn your hand with the drill, until it penetrates the bone, and the sick person feels it; by this that he finds air coming out of it, when he holds his hand over his nose.
Therefore then restore the place with astringent drying medicines, as I taught you.
When therefore flesh is born, and the place hardens, then the matter that was discharged to the outside returns to the nose; it is therefore lighter upon the sick person.
On the reduction of the prominence of the Eye.
Chapter XX.
When the eye protrudes completely, and no harm or diminution occurs in vision, then you should release the patient; then bleed him; afterward apply cupping to the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then apply a liniment to the eye made from acacia, aloe, olibanum, and sarcocolla. Then bind the eye from above with a plaster containing many powders.
And the method of the powders is: that you take soft cloths, many, and each cloth should be doubled over four times; make many of them; then place them one on top of the other, according to the amount needed by the eye; then bind them with a firm binding of hard cloth.
And again apply cupping with suction without scarification.
Then remove the cupping vessels, and leave the eye bound day and night.
However, if the bandage softens again, then it should be bound tightly; then remove the bandage after one day and night, as we said.
If therefore you see the eye has now returned to its tunic, it is good; and if not, then apply again the plaster, and powders, and bandage, and cupping, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the uvea tunic.
Chapter XXI.
When a rupture occurs in the uvea tunic, then it protrudes and proceeds outside the eyelids like a uvea grain, and it disfigures the human form because of it, and the desire is to incise it.  Then the operation on it is as I describe: insert a needle into the root of the uvea from the lower to the upper part.
Then insert another needle, on which is a doubled thread, from the tear duct side, and have it penetrate; and leave the first needle in its position.
Then, cut the location of the doubled thread, and tie the part of the uvea around the needle with it; and tighten it greatly.
Then pull out the needle, and place on the eye wool steeped in the white of an egg; and leave it until the thread with the uvea falls away.
Then treat the eye with that which strengthens it, until the wound is healed.
And sometimes the uvea is tightened from every side with a circle of cloth, until the eye gains great prominence.
Then it is pierced with a fine spatula, which reaches the depth of the eye, and the white moisture flows out, and the eye sinks, and immediately enters: then tighten it until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of Alcomnat.
Chapter XXII.
This illness, called Alcomnat, is nothing but pus that collects in the eye, similar to descending water, and is not the eye itself.
And the method of operation in it is to make the sick person sit on a prepared seat, or upright.
Then take hold of its head with your hands at two points, and move it until you see the pus descend to your eyes. Then it is stopped and does not descend, and the light is cleared: and the sick person sees things as they saw them: and if it did not descend further, know that it is water.
If, however, the descent of the pus is not prepared with what we have said, then make the sick person sit between your hands. Then take a sharp spatula, and with it cut through the corneal membrane a little at the juncture of the conjunctiva and the cornea, at the limbus, until pus emerges.
When it emerges, then instill into the eye warm water in which you mixed honey; or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey; then treat with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of water descending in the eye, or cataract.
Chapter XXIII.
We have already discussed the species of water in the division, and what is suitable for piercing, the speaker explaining and exhibiting it; then take that same thing according to health.
Then it is necessary that you make the infirm person sit between your hands, square, opposite the light, near the sun; and bind his healthy eye, and bind it tightly; then, with your right hand, the eyelid of the eye in which the water is, if the eye in which the water is, is the left eye; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one.
Then take with your right hand an almagda, if the eye is the left one; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one.
Then place the tip of the almagda near the crown, through the thickness of a ray, on the apparent white of the lesser tear duct; then push the almagda with force, and you will revolve your hand with it, until it penetrates the white of the eye; and you will feel that the almagda has now reached the empty thing.
And it is necessary that the measure of the almagda's journey to the depth be the measure of the space that is from the pupil to the end of the blackness; and it is the crown of the eye. For you will see it in the very pupil of the eye's vision, because of the clarity of the cornea.
Then place the almagda up to the place where the water is. Then press it downwards, one time after another.
If, therefore, the water descends immediately, then the sick person sees that above which he opens his sight immediately, and the almagda in his eye.
Then rest a little. If therefore the water descends, then put it down secondly, except that the almagda emerges.
When, therefore, it is fixed, and does not ascend, then extract the almagda with ease, and you turn your hand gradually.
Then dissolve some of the clear gem salt in water, and wash the inside of the eye with it. Then place over the eye a whole piece of lint, or wool soaked in rose oil and egg white, and bind the healthy eye with it.
We, however, and those of our time, place on it crushed cumin with egg white.
If, however, the almagda does not obey you in entering the eye because of its hardness, since it is from men whose eye is very hard, then it is necessary that you take a spatula, which is called Alberid, of which this is the form.
Therefore, with it, scrape only the conjunctiva, and do not put your hand into the hole; and it is only so that you make a subtle passage to the almagda's place.
Then introduce the almagda, as we have said.
And at the completion of your work, prepare for the sick person a properly made bedchamber, in which he sleeps on his back in a dark house: and let him be prohibited from all movement, and from coughing: and place his food that which soothes his nature: and do not let him move his head to the right, nor to the left at all.
And let the bandage be according to its disposition until the third day.
Then remove it in that dark house, and test his sight; and make him see things. Then replace the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not fitting that he do this at the hour of treatment, or immediately after the incision; rather, it is necessary to avoid it, because water ascends quickly through the vehemence of vision.
However, if a hot abscess occurs, then it is necessary to remove the eye before the seventh day, and treat it with that which calms the abscess until it is calmed; then resolve its sight, and place on his face an oral thing with which to regulate his sight under those days, him existing in that dark house.
Then let him emerge from the house gradually, and let him move about in his homes.
And know that in a zaltya, a student is not excused from sight sometimes; then, indeed, let him dare upon the operation.
And now indeed there has come to us someone from those who are of Alayrach, who said that there was made in Alayrach a perforated magdan, by which water is drawn.
However, therefore, I have not seen anyone in your land who has done that; nor have I read it in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of the species from the magdan, so that he who is ignorant of them may stand upon it.
Let them be made of proper bronze; and let their extremities be with this same subtlety triangular, and the extremity pointed.
Chapter Twenty-four.
Concerning the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
Sometimes diverse growths of flesh arise in the nose, some of which resemble a scorpion with many feet; and of these is cancerous, stony flesh, of a dark colour; and of these is that which is soft flesh, not of a dark colour.
In that flesh among these, which is soft, not fraudulent, nor cancerous, you should cause the infirm person to sit between your hands facing the ground, and open their nostrils, and insert a hook into the flesh; then pull it outward, and cut what you have grasped of it with a fine, sharp spatula on one side, until you know that the whole flesh has been removed.
If anything remains of it whose removal is not possible, then scrape it with one of the delicate instruments with ease, until nothing remains of it. However, if blood prevails in you, or a hot abscess occurs, then apply to it what is appropriate; or if it is from malignant abscesses, then hasten to cauterize it, until the blood is cut off, and all the flesh is removed.
Afterwards, vinegar and water, or wine, should be applied to the nose after the incision. If, however, the nose opens, and moisture proceeds from it to the palate, then know that it is now healthy.
If, however, the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then know that within it is flesh grown on the upper bones of the nostril, to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
Then it is necessary that you insert a linen thread of some thickness, and tie many knots in it; and place a quantity of a finger, or less, between each knot; and let the infirm person, as he can, direct one end of the thread into the nose with a probe, or with whatever is possible for him to do; and pull his smelling-thing until it reaches the abscess and emerges above his throat.
And many times indeed, children in schools do something similar to this; and it is an easy thing for him who wishes to do so.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end which emerges above the nose, and the other which remains in the nose. Then apply a saw to the flesh with the knots which are on the thread. Do that until you know that the flesh is now together with the knots of the thread.
Then pull out the thread; and put into the nose, after the blood has been cleansed, the linen cloth that you have soaked in Egyptian ointment: do this for three days, or more, until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
Then place a lead cannula at the end of the thing in the nose, for a few days, until it is healed.
If however it needs a treatment that will dry it out, use that.
To make a nasal douche, with which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it of silver, or bronze: let it be similar to a small lamp, open like a seashell: and its passageway likewise.
And if you wish, make it a closed cannula, like a pipe.
And the shell-like instrument for nasal douching should be full and uncovered; and let it have a handle at its rear, with which you hold it when you heat oil in it, or whatever you wish from juices and flowing substances.
Chapter XXV.
Often a wart is born at the end of the nose, and it grows larger and larger with the days until its appearance becomes ugly.
And therefore it is necessary to cauterize it at the beginning of its appearance, and to eradicate all of it. Then place upon the area either a cautery or a burning medicine that takes its place.
If, therefore, its incision is delayed until it grows large, then consider if it is stony, hard, of dark color, and with little sensation: then do not touch it with iron, because it is a cancerous abscess.
For often I have seen that one who incised a man's abscess, and great harm came from it upon the one who had it.
And if the abscess is soft to the touch, not dark in color, and you see that an incision is possible throughout it, then evacuate the infirmity, and incise it without fear, without anxiety: and treat the area with that which dries and constricts it, until it heals.
Chapter XXVI.
Concerning the suture of the nose, lip, and ear.
Know that when a separation of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, little help is given in it by operation, except in some people.
Therefore, when something of that sort happens to someone, you should consider if it is a recent wound with its own blood: and then bring together the edges of the wound with sutures; then treat it until it heals.
And if the lips of the separation of continuity are already separated, and each part is healthy, then it is necessary to scrape away every part from its apparent skin until it bleeds. Then bring together the two lips with sutures, and tighten them, and powder over it the blood of the dragon, and crushed frankincense; and place upon the powders a piece of diafiniton plaster, and some other of the consolidating plasters; and leave it bound for two or three days. Then loosen it, and change the medicine; and leave it until the threads come apart by themselves. Then treat it with ointment until it heals.
The method of suturing is, that you assemble the separation of continuity, either with needles, as we have narrated in the suturing of the belly; or assemble the separation with thread, as I have made you know.
Chapter XXVII.
Concerning the extraction of knots from the lips.
Many men, indeed, often have hard abscesses in the interiors of their lips, like the grains of herbs: and some of them are smaller, and some larger.
Therefore it is necessary that you turn back the lip, and cut over all its knot, and suspend it with a hook, and cut it from every side.
Then fill the place after the incision with ground zegi, until the blood is cut off.
Then it should be washed with vinegar, and the place where there is astringency should be treated with it, until the wounds are healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
Concerning the incision of fleshy growths on the gum.
Often flesh grows over the gum, which the ancients call a *buluss*.
Therefore, you should suspend it with a hook, or hold it with forceps, and cut it at its root, and allow the pus or blood to run.
Then place on the area ground zegi, or other astringent, drying powders.
If that flesh returns after treatment, for it often does, then cut it a second time and cauterize it; for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter XXIX.
Concerning the extraction of teeth with iron.
Sometimes rough, foul, and sometimes darkened, yellowish, and greenish, hard masses collect on the surfaces of the teeth from the inner and outer sides, and between the gums, until the corruption reaches the gums and the teeth become unsightly from it.
Therefore, you should have the patient sit between your hands, and his head in your lap, and scrape the molar and tooth, where the hard masses, similar to sand, appear, until nothing remains of them, and similarly, do the same for blackness, greenness, yellowness, and the rest, until they are cleaned.
If, therefore, what is on them is removed at the beginning of the scraping, it is good; and if not, then repeat the scraping on them another, a second, and a third day, until finally you achieve what you want.
And know that a tooth requires scrapers of various shapes, many forms, according to how it is prepared for your operation; for the scraper used to scrape the tooth from below is different from the scraper used to scrape between the teeth, according to other shapes.
And these shapes of scrapers should be prepared by you, which you see.
Chapter 30.
Of the extraction of toothache.
You ought to treat the tooth from its pain with all skill, and be slow in its eradication. For there is nothing that will replace it when it is pulled out, since it is a noble substance; until when there is no excuse for its removal, then it is necessary when the weak person is forced to extract it, to strengthen and to assure you of the aching tooth.
For often the pain deceives the sick person, and he thinks that it is in the tooth itself and pulls it out; then the pain does not recede until the sick person's tooth is pulled out.
For we have already seen that from the operation of phlebotomists.
Therefore, when the aching tooth itself is ascertained by you, then it is necessary to scarify around the tooth with a spatula, in which there is something of virtue, until the gum is loosened on every side; then move it with your fingers, or with delicate forceps at first gradually, until you break it.
Then place large forceps in a good position on it, and the patient's head between your knees: which you have already held firmly, so that it does not move; then pull the tooth straight, so that you do not break it.
If, therefore, it does not come out, then take some of those instruments, and introduce it under it from every side with ease, and attempt its movement, as you did at first.
If, however, the tooth is perforated or decayed, then it is necessary that you fill that hole with cloth; and bind it with the tip of a fine radius, so that it does not break at the time of your constriction upon it with forceps.
And it is necessary to do the scarification around the gum on every side thoroughly.
And maintain your strength so that you do not break it, and a part of it remains, and returns upon the sick person from that evil which is greater pain than his first pain.
And beware, lest you do that which ignorant phlebotomists do in their haste, and their audacity in eradicating it, beyond what we have recounted.
For very often they cause great illnesses in people, which are more difficult to break, and all or some of its roots remain; or to extract it with some of the jaw bones, as we have testified many times.
Then it should be washed after its eradication with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
If a flow of blood occurs from the places (for this often happens), then rub some zegi, and fill the place with it.  And if not, cauterize it if zegi does not help you.
The forms of the fine forceps, with which the tooth is moved first, should have long extremities, a short handle, and be thick, so that they do not double when pressed on the tooth with them.  They should be like those with thick handles, so that when you tighten them, they do not give way nor double.  Their extremities should be short, and they should be made of good iron, or well-made steel, with straight extremities.  And on their extremities should be teeth, some of which enter into others and tighten with a fitting, strong constriction.
And sometimes the extremities are shaped like the beak of a bird called a stork, and have a strong constriction.
Chapter XXXI.
Concerning the eradication of teeth, and the extraction of fractured jawbones.
When, during the extraction of a tooth, some part remains broken, then it is necessary to apply a poultice of butter to the area for one or two days, until the area is softened.  Then insert either algasts or forceps, whose extremities are similar to the beak of a bird called a stork. However, this is the form of the forceps.
Let their extremities be made like a file inside, or like aliskilfegi.
If therefore it does not yield to you for extraction with these forceps, then it is necessary to carefully treat the root, and uncover all the flesh, and insert an instrument called a small lever, whose form is this.
Let it be short of limb, somewhat thick: and let it not be soaked, so that it does not break.
If therefore the root emerges through it, it is good; and if not, it is necessary to assist with other instruments whose forms are these.
The form of the first is triangular in extremity, in which there is a certain thickness.
The form of a triangular, slender extremity.
And with other instruments and tools which preceded in the consideration of the teeth.
Also, let there be an aid with this instrument, which is likened to a large hook, of this form, triangular extremity twisted, in which there is something of thickness, so that it may not break, and let it not be soaked.
And know that there are many instruments for the teeth; and likewise, other instruments are not counted, and a skillful craftsman, subtle in his art, finds for himself the instrument according to the operation and the illnesses themselves that they signify.
Because there are illnesses for which the ancients did not name an instrument, due to the diversity of their species.
If, however, a bone of the jaw, or of other bones of the mouth, breaks or decays: then investigate over it in its place with that which suits it from the instruments and forceps that I have mentioned in the extraction of roots, and aid with a grasper, whose form is this.
Let it have something of thickness gradually, so that the bone is gripped or held with it, and does not flee and move until the bone comes out; and restore the place with medicines suitable for it.
If however there is a small decay in the bone, then scrape it from its decay, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter XXXII.
Of the sawing of teeth protruding over others.
When teeth protrude not according to their natural course, a foul form is made by it; and especially when this happens in women and girls, namely those who are for sale.
Therefore, it is necessary that you consider. For if a tooth is born after another tooth, and is not placed, or its sawing or filing is not possible, then remove it; and if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, which has this shape.
It is similar to a small beak, and is made from tempered iron, with a very sharp tip.
And let your incision, by which you cut it, be made over many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that another tooth is not moved.
If however it is prominent and exposed to its filing, then file it with a file made of tempered iron, whose form is this.
Let the whole thing be of tempered iron, and its handle of small, sharp points, like the file with which needles are made.  With which the tooth is filed gradually over many days with ease, so that the tooth is not moved, and does not fall out.  Then, gently, finally, scrape it with certain scrapers.
If a tooth has a broken part that injures the tongue, then it is necessary to file it as well, until the roughness of that fracture is removed and made even; and it is flattened, and does not injure the tongue, nor corrupt speech.
Chapter XXXIII.
Of the retention of teeth with gold wires.
When the anterior teeth suffer concussion and displacement from a blow or fall, and the patient cannot firmly bite down on what they eat lest they fall out, and you have treated them with astringent remedies, and the treatment is not successful, then the solution is to bind them with a gold wire.  For silver turns green and rots after days, but gold is never altered.
And let the wire be of a medium fineness and thickness according to the space between the teeth.
And the method of retention is as follows: take a wire, and pass a double length of it between two healthy teeth; then weave the two ends of the wire between the loose teeth, whether one or more, until you reach with the weave to a healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving back to the starting point, and tighten your grip with care and skill until it is completely firm; and let your tightening of the wire be near the roots of the teeth, so that it does not come undone.
Then cut the excess ends of the wire with scissors; and gather both ends and twist them together; and conceal them between the healthy tooth and the loosened tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue; then let it remain thus firmly fastened.
If, however, it comes loose, or is cut, tighten them with another wire, and it will remain firmly in place throughout the whole time.
And this is the method for teeth, and the way of retention for two healthy teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that one or two teeth return to their places after having fallen out, and are retained as we have described, and remain so; and no one but a skillful, prudent, and experienced craftsman can do this.
And sometimes a piece of bone is fitted: and it is made into the shape of a tooth, and placed in the place from which the tooth is strengthened and fastened, as we said: and it remains, and serves it for a long time.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
Sometimes this ligament which occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, from a wound which has already healed.
And the operation on it is that you open the mouth of the sick person at his head in your presence, and raise his tongue: then cut that nervous ligament across, until the tongue is freed from its restraint.
If there is something hard and knotted in it, and it is from the healing of a wound, then insert a hook into it, and split it across, until you cut the ligament, and the knot is loosened.
And beware, lest there be a fissure deep in the flesh, and you cut the artery there, and a flow of blood occurs.
Then wash the area of the incision with rose water, or with vinegar, or with cold water.
Then place a linen bandage under the tongue, which the sick person should hold all night: so that it will not heal a second time.
If, however, a flow of blood occurs, then place crushed zegi on the area. If, however, blood overcomes you, then cauterize the area with a lentiginous cautery, which is suitable for it; then treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it heals.
Chapter XXXV.
Sometimes, an abscess occurs under the tongue, resembling a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and perhaps it grows so large that it fills the mouth.
And the treatment for it is to open the sick person's mouth in the light of day, and examine the abscess.
If therefore you see it of a dark color, or black, hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, then do not touch it, for it is cancer.
And if it inclines toward whiteness, in which there is moisture, then thrust a hook into it, and cut it with a fine scalpel, and free it from all sides. If blood overcomes you during your operation, then place pounded zegi over it, until the bleeding ceases; then return to your operation, until you extract it with its entirety.
Then wash it with vinegar and salt, and treat it with the appropriate remaining treatment for it, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of an abscess of the two tonsils.
Chapter XXXVI.
Sometimes there occur between the throat, that is, the glands which resemble almonds, which occur externally: and they are called the two tonsils. When you treat them with what we said in the division, and they do not heal, then consider.
If therefore it is a hard abscess, of dark color, with little sensation: then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of red color, and its root is thick: then do not touch it with iron either, fearing a flow of blood, but leave it until it ripens. And then either you will pierce it, or it will rupture by itself.
And if it is white in color, round, and its root is thin: then it is necessary to cut it.
And the operation on it is, that you consider before its operation, if its abscess has quieted and been completely filled, or if it is diminished by some reduction: then therefore make the sick person sit in the sun, and let his head be in your lap: and open his mouth, and let a servant take between his hands, and press his tongue downwards with an instrument, whose shape is this.
Let it be made of silver, or bronze, which is as thin as a knife.
When therefore you press the tongue with it, and the abscess is shown to you, and your sight falls upon it: then take a hook, and fix it in the tonsil, and pull it outwards as much as you can, except that you pull out some of the coverings with it.
Then cut it with an instrument, whose shape follows this. It resembles shears; however, its ends should be curved, so that the opening of each is in a straight line with the other's sharp point: therefore take care that it be made of tempered iron, or of tempered steel.
If however this instrument is not present, then cut it with a spatula, whose shape is this. It should be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other.
And after you have incised one tonsil, cut the other according to this same type of incision.
Then, after the incision, let the sick person gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
If however a flow of blood occurs, let him gargle with water in which the peels of pomegranates have been boiled: or with the leaves of myrtle, and similar astringents, until the flow of blood is stopped: then treat him until he is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses arise in the throat, besides the two tonsils. Therefore, cut those according to what I have said about the incision of the two tonsils equally.
And indeed I have already treated a woman for an abscess which had arisen between her throat, which was drawing towards blackness, and with little feeling, which had almost closed her throat: and the woman breathed through a narrow passage, and I had already forbidden her food and water to drink: and she had already reached death, if it had remained for one day, or two days.
And the abscess had risen so much that it had sent out two branches, until they emerged above the two nostrils.
Therefore, I quickly hastened and fixed a hook into one of them. Then I pulled it, and a large portion came out from it.
Then I cut it off where I grasped it from the nostril.
Then I did the same to that which came out from the other nostril.
