Then I opened its mouth, and pressed its tongue; and afterwards I fixed a hook into the abscess; then I cut off a part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it.  Therefore, the woman's throat was healed, and immediately she began to drink water, then she ate food.
Therefore, I did not cease to cut from that abscess many times for a long time, and the abscess succeeded the place I was cutting, until it was excessively prolonged and with it.
Therefore, I devised and cauterized the abscess between the throat.
Therefore, it stood from the addition; then I walked away, and I did not know what God did to it after me.
Concerning the incision of the abscess of the uvula.
Chapter XXXVII.
When the catarrh descends to the uvula, and it becomes abscessed, and is long, then it is called perpendicular; and if it is in the lower part thick, round, then it is called a grape.
When it is treated with what we said in the division, and the treatment does not help; and you see that the hot abscess has calmed down from it, and it is subtle; then it is necessary to cut it.
And what is collected from it round, and does not have length, and is bloody, or of a brown or black colour, and does not have sensation: then it is necessary to avoid its incision. For there is fear upon the sick person in it.
Therefore, when you see it according to the manner which I have said, from its whiteness and length, you should make the sick person sit before the sun; and press his tongue with the instrument, of which the narrative preceded; then fix a hook in the uvula and pull it downwards; and cut it with one of the two instruments that I have mentioned in the incision of the two tonsils.
And it is necessary not to cut from it, except that which is added beyond the natural limit without addition.
For if you cut from it more, you will injure the voice and speech.
Then after the incision, use that which we have narrated in the incision of the two tonsils, and treat it until it is healed.
If, however, the sick person is terrified by the incision, then it is necessary that you use skill in cauterizing it without fear or fright.
And the method of cautery on it is not, except with sharp water; and it is as if you place the head of the sick person in your bosom. Then press his tongue with the instrument we have mentioned. Then take from the water I told you about in the chapter on cauterization, and make with it quicklime that is not extinguished, and place it neither thick nor thin, and fill with it the hollow of this instrument, which has this shape.
Let the concavity in which the medicine is placed be at its extremity, like the concavity of the radius of a spoon.
And place the instrument with the medicine upon the uvula itself; and let the sick person lie upon his side, so that saliva may run from his mouth from the internal medicine, so that nothing descends from it to his throat and harms him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you shall apply it to the uvula for the quantity of half an hour, until you see it already blackened and the biting of the medicine calmed.
And if you wish to take a poultice, and wrap it around the extremity of the radius, and instill the poultice with medicine, and introduce the radius with the poultice into the cannula from above, until the poultice adheres to the uvula.
Do that repeatedly until you achieve what you wish regarding the cauterization of the uvula. Then leave it; for it dries and falls off after three or four days.
If you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it: and after the cauterization, wipe around the uvula with a poultice soaked in butter, and cleanse with it what is around it from the medicine: and rinse with cold water.
And treat externally with embrocations, and internally with gargles, until it is healed.
This method of treatment is therefore more beneficial than incision and further from danger.
And sometimes the uvula is also treated with something finer than a cauterization or incision: for it is treated with fumigation, according to this method. Take calamint, hyssop, oregano, rue, sseah, chamomile, and wormwood, and similar herbs: and gather them all, or some of them, in a pot, and immerse them in vinegar, and boil: and let the pot be lined with good clay: and let there be a hole in the middle of the pot, upon which a concave instrument should be placed according to this method.
Let it be made of silver, or brass: and let the end, on which the pomegranate is, be introduced into the sick person's mouth, until the vapor ascends to the uvula over the cannula, until the uvula is darkened much.
Then repeatedly over it many times, until it dries.
And beware, lest you perform this treatment at the beginning of the arrival of an abscess. For it usually adds to the abscess: and you should not do it except at the decline of its hot stage of the abscess.
If however this instrument is not present for you, then take a cannula, and place a piece of egg shell on its end: so that the sick person's mouth is not burned: because the egg shell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth: and this is from good treatment with health.
Concerning the extraction of thorns and bones from the throat.
Chapter XXXVIII.
Often a bone, or a fishbone, or something else adheres in the throat.
Therefore, it is necessary to extract that which is apparent from them, over which sight falls, after pressing the tongue with an instrument near the sun, and it is declared to you what is in the throat.
And what is not apparent to you and is hidden in the throat, it is necessary that you make the stomach weak before the food is digested in the stomach. For perhaps the attached thing will be expelled by vomiting.
Or let the weak person swallow a piece of turnip, or a lettuce root, or swallow a piece of dry bread; or let him take a piece of soft dry sea sponge: and tie it to a thread, then let him swallow it. When therefore it reaches the place where the spines or bone are, and is extracted, then it emerges.
If therefore it does not emerge with what we have said: then use an instrument of lead according to this form.
Let it be thicker than a radius for a little while, and at its end let there be a curvature, which he should introduce into his throat with ease: and let him have his head raised upwards.
And let him be careful not to touch his epiglottis, so that coughing does not occur to him, and let him push with it the bone or spine, or let the physician introduce it with his hand.
But that which the sick person introduces is better, because of his knowledge of the place of the attached thing: and let him push downwards, or pull his hand with the instrument upwards, the whole of it according to the quantity that is prepared for him, until it emerges.
Concerning the extraction of a leech.
Chapter Thirty-nine.
When a leech is treated with what I said in the division on treatment with medicines, and it does not help, then consider in the throat of the sick person before the sun, or in the sun after you have pressed his tongue with the instrument that I told you about.
If therefore your sight falls upon the leech, extract it with a small hook, or with suitably fine forceps.
If it is not possible with them, then take a concave cannula, and introduce it into the throat of the sick person, nearly to the throat; then introduce into the concavity of the cannula a heated iron with fire. Do this many times; and let the sick person abstain from water all day.
Then let him take a bowl full of cold water, and open his mouth in it; and let him rinse his mouth with it, and not swallow anything from it; and let him move the water one hour after another with his hand. For the leech immediately, when it senses water, falls.
If however it does not emerge with what we have described, then fumigate the sick person with lice, or with asa with the instrument which I have described in the fumigation of the uvula.
Do that many times, because it will fall itself.
And the method of operation in fumigation is, that you take a pot in which there are live prunes with fire; and the pot is covered with a lid, in the middle of which is a hole; and the end of the instrument is placed in that hole.
Then project the fumigation, and let the sick person place his mouth at the end of the cannula, and close his mouth, so that the fumigation does not escape, until you know that the fumigation has already reached the leech. For the leech will fall immediately.
If it does not fall, then repeat the fumigation many times.
And let the sick person endure thirst, and eat salty things, and garlic, and not drink water.
Therefore, it is necessary that it depart with this treatment.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech is extracted from the throat, when it falls upon it in sight.
It is similar to forceps, as you see: except that it has this curvature that is inserted into the throat. And its tip is similar to the beak of a bird called a stork, in which there is the roughness of a file: when it grasps something, it does not release it at all.
Concerning the opening of abscesses, and their incision, and their general treatment.
Chapter XL.
Abscesses are of many kinds, in many ways, according to the recollection of them, namely one, and one in this book: and they are diverse in their perforation and incision, from two methods.
One of which is a type of abscess within itself, and in that which it encompasses from moistures.
And the second method is because of the places in which it occurs in the body.
Because an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the anus; and an abscess occurring in a fleshy area is different from an abscess occurring in the joints; and each of them has its own judgment from the operation.
And of abscesses there are some which should not be pierced, unless after the maturation of the pus in them, and its completion: and of them there are some which should be pierced while they are raw, and do not mature according to completion.
Such as abscesses which are near the joints.
Because when an abscess occurs near a joint, and its duration continues until that which is around it putrefies, perhaps the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted; wherefore it is a nearer cause of contraction of that limb.
Or it is an abscess on a principal limb: because if you delay its perforation until it matures, it will harm either that limb or the principal limb through it.
Or it is near the anus, therefore pierce it unripe; and it is not fitting to pierce a raw abscess, not of complete digestion, except that which is near the anus, lest it putrefy deeply, and extend to the interior of the anus, and become a fistula, or become in its terminal point incurable.
It is necessary that you know the time of perforation of the abscess, which is mature according to completion: and it is by the subsidence of pain, the recession of fever, and the diminution of redness, and pulsation, and sharpness of the abscess head; and the other signs.
And it is necessary that you make the same perforation in the lower place of the abscess, and of more prominent eminence; and let the perforation extend in the length of the body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands and feet, and in the places of the tendons, and cords, and nerves, and arteries, and finally in all similar places where there is no bending.
In those however which bend, proceed with the perforation along that limb.
When however the abscess is in fleshy places, then it is better that you leave its perforation until its digestion is confirmed, as we have said according to completion.
Because if you perforate it before that, the course of the virus from it is prolonged, and there is much filth and uncleanliness, and perhaps its edges will become hardened, and its depth.
And some abscesses are perforated according to the width of the body, in case of necessity, or according to the need of the limb.
And it is necessary that you administer a small perforation in small abscesses, and a large perforation in large abscesses, or many incisions, similar to the size of the abscess.
And there are abscesses in which the skin must be hollowed out; and it should be incised when it has already become like a cloth, and has reached a point where it is already dead, as happens in many cases, such as boils and buboes.
And some of them are cut with an incision having three angles. And from among them there is one that is incised like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Like an inguinal abscess.
And among them are some in which a round incision, and a crescent-shaped incision are administered; and similar incisions to them.
That which does not have a head, however, such as mild, superficial abscesses, should be pierced with a superficial, or merely flat, perforation.
And when the abscess is large and has already collected much pus, and you pierce it, you should not be hasty and extract all the pus at that time; rather, extract a portion of it. Then plug the abscess until another day. Then extract some more of the pus again.
Do this repeatedly according to the gradation, until it comes out; especially if the sick person is weak in strength, or a pregnant woman, or a young child, or an old and decrepit person, for the animal spirit is often dissolved by the exit of pus, and perhaps the sick person dies, and you do not realize it.
Therefore, observe this chapter with great caution.
And after your perforation, with which you perforate these abscesses, it is necessary that you clean the wound, and consider. If therefore the wound of the abscess is small, or is a flat incision, then apply bandages of linen, or old cloth. And if the abscess is large, and there are many fissures of perforation, it is then necessary that you insert a bandage into every fissure, until they meet one another.
And if in the abscess you have cut away a part of the skin, or have hollowed it out around: then it is necessary that you fill it with old cloth, or as when cloth is woven, or shaved with flax-wool without moisture; and bind it until the third day.
Then discard it, and treat it with what is appropriate of ointment, until it is healed.
If however, bleeding occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and vinegar, after soaking a cloth in them; and place it repeatedly over the bleeding area.
If however, the bleeding persists, then it is necessary that you administer the regimen, and the treatment, and powders, which I have described in many places of this book of ours, and by division.
If however, it is in the time of winter, and the location of the abscess is of many nerves: then it is necessary that you infuse cushions with wine and warm oil; and place them over the affected areas.
And if it is in summer, and the areas are fleshy: then it is necessary that you place cushions soaked in water and oil, or in wine and oil, and make the whole thing cold. Until when the third day shall have passed, as we have said, then it is necessary that you open the abscess, and cleanse it, and administer to its treatment what is appropriate for it, until it is healed.
This, therefore, is what is necessary from the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally.
According to the path of distinction, however, I have already spoken of every abscess, in what way the path to its treatment is chaptered individually.
Chapter XLI.
Concerning the incision of abscesses which occur on the scalp.
Small abscesses occur on the scalp, and they are of the species of Tumors, which the membranes that are sacs contain them; as if they were the gullets of a hen.
And their species are many.
Some are fatty; and some contain moisture like mud; and some contain moisture like fritters and gruel; and similar to them, and some are hard, stony ones.
And in the incision of all of them, and their extraction, there is no fear, as long as the incision of them does not oppose an artery.
And the operation in their incision is, that you should try them with an instrument, whose form comes after this, which is called an Intromissum, until you know what they contain.
For if it is that which contains moisture, then cut it by an incision along its length with a superficial incision, according to this form.
And begin with the incision from line b. to line g. When the moisture is thus evacuated, then scrape the container which holds that moisture, and do not leave anything of it at all.
For it often returns if anything is left of it.
Then immerse the cotum in Egyptian ointment, if it is present; and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with it, and leave it until another day. For it will corrode that which remained of the container.
Then again apply the cotum with Egyptian ointment a second and a third time, and if you need it, until you are sure that nothing remains of the container; then treat the wound with the ointment, until it is healed.
If the abscess contains a fatty lump, then cut it according to this form, the incision having the shape of a cross; and insert hooks between the two sides of the incision, and scrape it from every side; and try your skill in removing the membrane that contains it.
If however the artery offers resistance, then do that which I shall tell you afterwards.
And similarly do in the abscess, if it is stony, with the same incision and treatment, according to what we have said.
And the incision over a stony abscess is easier, because it is less bloody and moist. And sometimes there are some of these abscesses that are on the head in some people, with which there is absolutely no moisture.
That is because I incised an abscess on the head of an old woman, and found an abscess like a solid and hard stone, rough, white, which could not be broken; and if someone struck it with something, it would injure them.
In other abscesses, however, occurring on the head, apart from these abscesses that occur on children's heads and at the roots of the ears, the incision of all of them should be a superficial incision.
Or make a perforation of them always from their lower part, so that the course of pus is eased downwards.
Then treat it with what is appropriate for them from the treatment.
Concerning the incision of tumors on the neck.
Chapter XLII.
These abscesses often occur in the neck, and under the armpits, and in the groin: and sometimes they occur in the rest of the body.
And those which arise from them on the neck, are one, and many: and they generate from each other: and every tumor from them is within its own proper tunic, as is in the knots and abscesses of the head, as we have narrated.
And the types of these tumors are many. And from them are stony ones; and from them are those which contain moisture; and from them are malignant ones, which do not obey treatment.
Therefore, in that one of them which you see to be well-disposed upon touch, whose proximity to the skin color is manifest, and which moves to every part, and is not connected with the nerves of the neck, nor with the veins, nor with the artery, nor is deep: you ought to separate it by a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body from line b to line g, and scrape it from every side; and spread the two lips of skin with a hook, or with many hooks, if you need it, as we said in the abscesses of the head, and extract it gradually.
And it is a precaution that the vein or nerve not be cut.
And let the scalpel not be very sharp, so that it does not injure your hand with the incision, or disturb the sick person, and you cut what is not necessary.
However, if you cut a vein or artery, or it hinders you from the operation, then place on the wound pounded zegi, or certain powders that stop bleeding, and constrict the wound, and leave it until the sharpness of the abscess subsides, and the wound softens; or until it begins and wants to putrefy.
For the blood is then stopped.  Therefore return to your operation, until you complete it.
Then inquire with your index finger if any small strophulus or other small things remain: therefore, cut them and clean them.
If a large vein is in the root of the strophulus, then it is necessary not to cut that strophulus from its root; rather, it is necessary to tie it with a strong doubled thread, and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old lint, which you have already soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then care for it.
If the entire strophulus is cut, then it is necessary to bring together the two edges of the wound, and suture it immediately, after you see that no excess remains.
However, if you see that excess remains because of the size of the strophulus, then it is necessary to attempt to cut its root and extract it; and administer the suture that we have described.
And any humors contained within the strophulus, pierce it with a superficial puncture, so that the place of its maturity appears to you; and place the puncture from that which follows below the body, as we said.
Then, after the puncture, apply dressings with ointment, and similar things, to corrode what remains of the corruption, until when you see the wound already cleansed; then care for it with a generative flesh ointment, until it is healed.
Chapter XLIII.
Concerning the incision of the epiglottis from an abscess occurring within the throat.
The ancients said this incision into the epiglottis, and I have seen no one in our land who has performed it; and this is the writing of their words.
In those suffering from synocha, the incision of the epiglottis should be avoided; for it brings no benefit, because all the veins and lungs are weak.
However, in those who have a hot abscess in the mouth, or throat, or both tonsils, when there is no sickness in the canal, it is necessary to use the incision of the epiglottis, to avoid the harm that results from suffocation.
Therefore, when we wish to do this, we should cut the epiglottis beneath three or four circles of the epiglottis, with a small incision across the width, at the point between two circles, in such a way that the incision is into the soft tissue, not into the cartilage.  This place is suitable for the incision because it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are distant from it.
If the surgeon is timid, then it is necessary to extend the skin of the throat with a hook. Then he should split the skin until he reaches the canal, avoiding the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should cut the soft tissue as we have described, and the incision of the canal is signaled by the phlegm that emerges from it, together with the rupture and severance of the voice.
And leave the wound open for a time.
When therefore the hour in which suffocation is feared has passed, bring together the two lips of the wound in the skin, and suture it itself alone without cartilage.
Then administer medicines that cause flesh to grow until it is healed.
Says Albucasis. The summary of this account we have given is not applicable unless they see that some of these abscesses have already closed the throat of the sick person; and the sick person is raised above death, and his breath wants to be cut off; they go to cut the epiglottis, so that the sick person may breathe over the place of the wound with some respiration, and may be saved from death; and for this reason they ordered the wound to be left open until the malice of the illness is consumed, and its malice is for three days, and like itself; then, therefore, they ordered the wound to be sutured and cared for until it is healed.
And that which I saw myself is that a maidservant took a knife and thrust it into her throat, and cut a part of the lung passage with it.
And I was called to treat her, and I found her groaning, like the groan of the dead.
I therefore explored the wound and found little blood that had issued from the wound. Therefore, I trusted that she had not cut a vein, nor a conduit, and wind was coming out of the wound.
I hastened then, and sewed up the wound, and treated it until it was healed; and nothing happened to the maidservant except hoarseness in her voice; and she returned after days to a better state of her health.
Therefore, I say that there is no fear in the section of the epiglottis.
Concerning the section of the hernia of the throat.
Chapter XLIV.
This abscess, called the hernia of the throat, is a large abscess, according to the color of the body; and it is very common in women.
And it is according to two ways: either it is natural, or it is accidental.
In the natural one, certainly, there is no talent.
The accidental one, however, is according to two ways: one of which is similar to fatty lumps. And the other way is similar to the abscess that forms from the knotting of an artery. And in it there is fear, and it is not necessary that presumption be made to it with iron, unless in that which is small; and you should test it and investigate it with an instrument. If therefore you find it similar to a fatty lump, and it is not suspended by any vein, then cut it, as lumps are cut, and extract it with whatever it contains from the cyst, if it is in a cyst; and if not, then search its entire extent. Then treat the place with what is necessary in treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter XLV.
Concerning the section on the types of Lumps.
There are many kinds of lumps, and I have already stated all their kinds in the division; and it is necessary for me to describe here the difference between a lump and a fistula, since it causes doubt.
I say therefore, that when a fistula is present, there is heat, fever, and intense pain, until the ebullition of the superfluity subsides and the putrefaction is complete; then the fever and heat cease.
However, with a lump there is no heat, no fever, nor pain, and it contains them in cysts, which is a special sac for them, and it is according to the color of the body. And their beginning is like a chickpea, and it becomes like a batecha, and larger or smaller.
And they are of two kinds: either fatty, or containing moisture. And the colors of moisture are many, according to what I have said in the division.
Therefore, when you arrive at the treatment of a lump, you should examine it, and first investigate it with an instrument called the "Intromissum," according to how its shape will appear in the chapter after this one.
And the method of investigation for abscesses and all lumps is that you should take this instrument and insert it into the most moist place that you find in the abscess.  And you should slowly revolve your fingers with it until you know that the instrument has penetrated the skin. Then place your hand according to the size of the abscess.
Then withdraw the Intromissum, and consider what emerges in its wake.
If, therefore, running moisture of any color emerges, then cut it with a superficial incision, as I said in the other abscesses.
And if no moisture emerges in the wake of the Intromissum, then know that it is greasy.  Cut, therefore, over it with an incision shaped like a cross, in this form, as I taught you.  And suspend it with hooks, and peel away the skin from every side with ease.  And keep a kit if you can upon it, so that you remove it [the node] whole with the lump.
If, however, the cyst breaks open during the operation, and it is not possible to remove it whole, for this often happens, then remove it piece by piece, until nothing remains of it.
For if something small or large remains of it, the lump returns most often.
If it overcomes you, and a small amount remains of it, then fill the wound at its completion with certain sharp corrosive powders.  And bind the wound, and place upon it what sits as a hot abscess, and care for it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
If, therefore, the lump is large, then sew its two lips together, and treat it with what promotes healing.
If, however, there is a pulsating or non-pulsating vein there, then hurry and fill the place with pounded zege, and leave it bound for two or three days, until the wound is putrid and the blood's boiling ceases.  Then return to cutting out what remains of the lump.
Chapter XLVI.
Concerning the instruments for cutting and piercing from outside.
Among them are the forms of instruments inserted, and they are of three kinds.
For among them is a large one, and among them is a medium one, and among them is a small one.
Let them be made of iron, with square ends, well-formed, so that they readily enter the abscess.
And these are the forms of instruments, and no, that is, piercing ones: and one of them is called beris, that is, piercing: and they are of three kinds.
For among them is a large one, and a medium one, and a small one.
They are suitable for investigating abscesses, and exits, and fistulas, and buboes, from that which is between the bones.
And others are made round, smooth, and easy, like large needles with which saddles are sewn, namely of amber, or of bronze, or iron, or silver.
And the best of them are those made of amber.
And also from black lead, which are suitable for testing fistulas, whose concavity has reflections, so that they may be reflected with their smoothness with those reflections.
And they are again of three types. Because from them are long, from them medium, and from them short, according to the amount of the fistula's width and its constriction.
The shape of Hooks, and there are many types.
Because from them are simple, namely those with one curve: and they are of three types as you see, Large, Medium, and Small.
And from them are blind hooks: and they are of three types.
And from them are hooks having two curves, and they are of three types: and from them are hooks having three curves, and they are of three types: and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the shapes of scalpels, with which knots and abscesses are cut and scraped: and they are of three types: because from them is large, medium, and small.
Their cutting edges are sharp, the other edges are blunt: and they are not placed thus, unless that with them a benefit is done in scraping knots, in fear of incision of a vein, or nerve; and so that the infirm may be healed, and find a little tranquility from the burning than he finds in scraping of an abscess.
They are made of air, and are similar to a Radiol, with which alcohol is made: and on the broad end is a hidden Spatumilis point: and it runs inwards, and outwards when you wish, as you see.
The shape of the spatulas that are hidden between the fingers during the perforation of abscesses: and the sick do not perceive them: and they are of three types, large, medium, and small.
Forms of cupping instruments with which the flow of blood is cut off: and they are of three types, large, medium, and small.
They are made of air, or brass, round, somewhat elongated, as you see: and be of a fine subtlety.
And it is necessary that these types of cupping instruments be large and small with you, so that you may cut off blood with them quickly when needed, and then when medicine is not available to you.
However, do not use them for the removal of blood in every part of the body, and do not use them except on fleshy areas, such as the thighs of the legs, and the hips, and the upper arms, and the breasts, and the belly, and the groins, and similar fleshy, moist parts.
And sometimes smaller instruments like hazelnuts are made from them, according to their shape, with which blood is cut off when it flows from a phlebotomy site, or at a vein or artery incision; and sometimes they are made round according to this shape.
Chapter XLVII.
Concerning the care of the breasts of men that resemble the breasts of women.
Sometimes the breasts of certain men swell at the time of completing sleep (that is, when they begin to become polluted in sleep) until they resemble the breasts of women, and they remain foul-looking and suppurative.
Therefore, whoever abhors that, should cut above the breasts with a crescent-shaped incision, according to this shape from line b. to line g. Then scrape off all the fat. Afterwards, fill the wound with a healing medicine. Then bring together the two edges of the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
If, however, the breast inclines downward and softens due to its size, as happens with women, then it should be split on its two upper sides with two incisions similar to the crescent shape, each of which is contained within the other at the ends of each, until there is a larger line containing the smaller one, according to this shape from line b. to line g. Afterwards, scrape off the skin between the two edges, and remove the fat, and administer what we said about sutures and powders, which is necessary for treatment, until it is healed.
If, however, you do not complete the incision that you ought to make, because of the patient's discomfort or because of the flow of blood, then you should fill the wound with cotton soaked in a corrosive ointment, and leave it until it corrodes what remains of the fat. Then treat it until it is healed.
Concerning the perforation of an abscess that occurs under the armpit.
Chapter XLVIII.
These abscesses which occur under the armpit are of the kinds of hard Strophulae, which contain cysts, and of those which contain moist humours.
Therefore, those of them that contain moist humours, should be incised with a lunate incision, according to this form, from line b to line g. When, therefore, all that is in the abscess, of pus, has been evacuated, then fill it with old lint, and leave it until another day.
Then the lint should be removed, and it should be treated with a suitable ointment for it, until it is healed.
If the abscess is of the kind of the knotty Strophulae, then incise upon it, as we have said in the section on Strophulae, evenly.
If, however, the wound extends in such a way as to emit pus, then apply a cautery to it, according to what we have said in what has been said before.
Chapter XLIX.
Concerning the incision of an abscess which occurs because of an artery and a vein.
When an artery bursts, and the skin above it consolidates, an abscess often results from this; and similarly, a vein also, when it bursts, will have an abscess and swelling occur.
And the signs by which it is known that an abscess and swelling are due to an artery and a vein are: because, when an abscess is due to an artery, it is long, gathered deep within the body; and when you push the abscess with your fingers, you feel as if there is a creaking sound to it.
And that which is due to a vein is round on the surface of the body.
And incision upon this abscess is an error, especially that which is under the armpits, and the groins, and the neck, and in many places of the body; and it is very large. Therefore, its treatment with iron should be avoided, and that which is also in the extremities, or in the head, should be avoided.
Therefore, for that which is of them from the swelling of the artery's opening, make an incision in the skin lengthwise. Then open the incision with hooks, then the artery; and free it from its coverings, until it is exposed.
Then insert a needle beneath it, and make it penetrate to the other side, and bind the artery with a double thread in two places, according to what I taught you in the extraction of the two arteries which are in the temples.
Then pierce the place, which is between the two ligatures with a lancet, until all the blood which is in it comes out, and the abscess is resolved.
Then administer a treatment that produces pus until the ligatures fall away; afterwards treat it with suitable ointments for it, until it is healed.
If the abscess is due to the incision of a vein, then you should hold with your hand whatever you can of the abscess with the skin. Then insert a needle under the place you held with your hand, and make it penetrate; and let a double thread be on it, until you extract it from the other side. Then tie the abscess with a good ligature, as I told you in the ligature of a grape, from two sides with two ends of the thread.
If however you fear that the thread will be pulled out, then insert another needle with another thread under the entire abscess at the incision of the needle: press, and tie your threads in four places.
Then cut the abscess in its middle, until what is inside it comes out; then cut the excess skin, and let what is tied from it.
Then place upon it a poultice which you have soaked in wine and oil. Then administer the treatment, which is done with linens and ointments, until it is healed.
Chapter L.
Concerning the abscess which occurs from the twisting of nerves.
Just as an abscess occurs in the artery and vein, so too does an abscess occur in the nerves: which occurs in them through a blow, or occurs from excessive labor, and similar causes; and it happens most often in those places of helpers, or of the heel, and in every place where the joints move: and it is a serious abscess.
Its color is similar to the rest of the body, and it is in many dispositions without pain; and when it is squeezed with violence, the sick person feels in it a sensation similar to numbness.
And it is not a deep-seated abscess within the body, rather it is beneath the skin, and it moves to every part, and does not go forward or backward, nor behind to the back.
Therefore, whatever of them is in the joints, should not be incised with iron. For perhaps a contraction will result.
In that which is in the head or forehead, however, cut the skin with a lancet. If therefore it is a small abscess, then hold it with scissors, and cut it away from its root.
And if it is large, then suspend it with a hook, and scrape it: then remove it, and close the wound with sutures: and treat it until it heals.
Chapter LI.
Concerning the incision of an abscess occurring in the belly.
Some people frequently have in their bellies, and in the rest of their bodies, growths which are called Fungalia, by reason of their resemblance to fungi.
Their root is subtle, and their head is thick, and their lips are turned inward. And some of them are small, and some of them grow very large.
And I myself have seen a man who had two growths in his belly, which were more like fungi than other things: there was no difference between them, white, with small roots, and their lips turned inward, and they split, and moisture always flowed from them.
Therefore, I cut them, and found one to weigh approximately ten and eight ounces, and the other approximately six ounces.  And the operation for cutting them is, that you should then consider, if the patient is moist, and the color of the growth is white, moist, with a fine root; then cut it with a broad spatula, and have cauteries ready in the fire.
For often much blood is expelled at its incision; therefore, hurry if blood overwhelms you, then cauterize it.
If you see a patient who is faint and fearful of incision with iron, then take a thread of lead, well-made, and bind it tightly around the growth.  The method of which is the one we discussed, and leave it for two days.
Then add to the lead constriction. Therefore do not cease binding the lead, whenever it presses on the growth, until it is severed, and falls away of its own accord without effort.
If however the growth is of large root, then in its incision there is something of fear and terror; especially that which is in the belly.
However, it is possible that you may cut half of it. Then cauterize it, so that it does not return.
Then treat it until the wound is healed.
And beware lest you presume to cut a growth that is dark-colored, with little sensation, and of foul appearance: for it is indeed a cancerous abscess; and I shall come with a description of Cancer after this.
Chapter LII.
Concerning the treatment of the navel eminence.
The navel eminence arises from many causes. Or from fissures of the diaphragm above the abdomen; and from it protrude the pleura, or the intestines, according to what happens in other ruptures. Or from blood flowing from a vein or artery, according to what has been mentioned previously. Or from a constriction of wind within it.
If however it is due to a fissure of the diaphragm and the protrusion of the viscera, then the color is similar to the color of the body, and it is smooth, without pain, and appears in various locations. And if it is due to the protrusion of the intestine, then its location is, with the matter we have described, of a more varied nature. When therefore you press it with your fingers, it disappears; then it returns, and perhaps there is a sound with it; and it increases greatly at the entrance of a bath, and with strenuous exertion.
If however it is due to moisture, it is smooth, then it does not disappear when you press it with your hand, nor does it contract, nor does it increase.
If it is due to blood, then with these signs the abscess appears black on the side.
If however it is due to formed flesh, then the abscess is serious, large, hard, and fixed to a single quantity.
If however it is due to windiness, then its touch is smooth.
And the operation on it is that you should consider then whether the prominence of the navel is due to blood of the artery, or vein, or windiness: then you ought to refrain from its treatment. For in that there is fear and terror, as I taught you in the chapter, in which I said abscesses due to artery and vein.
If the prominence of the navel is due to the intestine, or the zirb, then you ought to instruct the sick person to hold his breath and stand extended. Then mark with ink around the entire navel. Thereafter, instruct him to lie on his back between your hands. Then encircle with a wide spatula around the navel over the spot you marked with ink. Thereafter, extend the middle of the abscess upwards with a large hook. Then tie it firmly around with thread, or with a silk chord or strong ligature; and let the knot of the ligature be tied tight.
Then open the middle of the abscess extended above the ligature. If therefore you find it already captured by the ligature, then pull out the tied part, and insert the index finger into it, and push the intestine inwards towards the belly; and if you find a winding, then take it with a hook and cut away its excess.
If, however, it is an artery or vein that is opposite you, then surround it extensively. Then return to your operation. And take two needles, and insert in them two strong threads; and insert the two needles around the circumference you have made around the abscess in the shape of a cross, making them penetrate.
Then tie off the abscess in four places above the needles; and if you wish, remove the needles, and leave the area until the extended flesh putrefies, and falls off by itself; or cut it when it becomes softened and putrefied.
Then treat it with what is necessary of ointments until it is healed.
If the eminence of the navel is from fleshy matter in it, or from moisture, then it is necessary to cauterize the abscess, as we have said. And draw out the blood, or moisture, which you find in it.
Then treat it with what consolidates the place.
Chapter LIII.
Concerning the treatment of CANCER.
We have already spoken in the division of the species of Cancer, and how is the way to its treatment with medicines; and caution must be taken in its treatment with iron, so that it is not ulcerated; and we have spoken of Cancer generated in the womb, and caution in its treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes Cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, such as Cancer that is in the breasts or in the thigh, and the like, from the limbs located to draw out the entire thing: especially when it is small and beginning.
However, when it is old and large, one should not approach it.
For I myself could not cure anyone from it, nor have I seen anyone before me who has attained to it.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we said, is that you should proceed, and free the infirm person from black cholera sometimes. Then phlebotomize him if you find it with manifest fullness.
Then prepare the infirm person with the preparation in which he is to be placed with the operation.
Then throw into the Cancer the hooks that suit it. Afterwards, hollow it out from every side with the skin, according to scrutiny, until nothing remains of its roots; and let the blood run, and do not sever it quickly, rather express the places, and wipe away the entire thick blood with your hand, or with whatever instruments are possible there.
If however, the flow of thick blood from the incision of an artery, or vein, causes you opposition, then cauterize the vein until the blood is severed. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of dropsy.
Chapter LIII. We have already discussed in the division of the types of dropsy, and how the collection of water occurs, and the signs of each type, and its treatment with medicines.
And that which is treated with iron is only a type of ascites alone; and it is not approached with iron in the case of tympanitis, nor in the case of hyposarca at all; for that is mortal.
When you have already treated this type of dropsy, tympanitis, with medicines, and your treatment has not been successful, then consider. For if weakness has already overtaken the sick person, or there is some other ailment with him besides the dropsy, such as if there is a cough, or looseness of the bowels, and things similar to these, then beware of treating him with iron, for there is fear of that.
If however you see the sick person of exuberant strength, and there is no illness with him besides dropsy alone, and he is neither a child nor an old man, then the method of operation in him is that you raise the sick person standing between your hands, and a servant behind him should express his belly with his hand, driving the water to the lower part of the chest.
Then take a pointed spatula, according to this form. Let it be of two sharp parts, long, with a sharp tip like a spatula. However, let it have some degree of shortness gradually, so that you do not proceed with it in the operation all the way to the intestine and harm him.
Then consider. For if the generation of dropsy is from the part of the intestines, then it is necessary that you lengthen the incision from the navel a quantity of three fingers, downward in a direct line above the pubis.
If, however, the generation of dropsy is because of illness of the liver: then let your incision be to the left of the navel by three fingers.
And if its generation is because of the spleen, then let the incision be on the right side; and it is also not necessary that the incision be on the side on which the sick person wishes to lie, so that superfluities do not flow to that weak place.
Then pierce through the entire skin with the instrument. Then introduce into that incision, and raise your hand with the spatula between the skin, and if you do this, as if you were to scrape it, and let the quantity that is scraped be the quantity of a fingernail, or approximately that.
Then pierce, if you do this, until the spatula reaches the hollow place: and that is the place of the water. Then pull it out, and introduce into the hole an instrument of this shape. And it is similar to a cannula; it is made of silver, or bronze, or brass, smooth, polished; to which, on its lower part, is a small hole, and on its sides are three holes, two on one side, and one on the other side; and the end of it is cut, according to the manner of cutting a reed, as you see; on its upper end is a ring.
For when the instrument reaches the water, then it itself descends immediately upon the instrument, therefore evacuate from the water in a short time a moderate quantity.
Because if you evacuate more from it than necessary, perhaps the weak person dies in a short time by the dissolution of his animal spirit, or a faint that approaches death happens to him.
But evacuate from it according to the quantity of its virtue, and according to what the disposition of the patient signifies to you, from the strength of his pulse and from his condition.
Then withdraw the instrument, and retain the water.
For it is retained immediately because of the skin which retains the opening that is over the sifac: of which I have told you, that you should perforate it in that manner.
Then again use the instrument on the following day, if you see the patient tolerate it, and also withdraw a small amount from the water.
Do this for several days without fear, and guard yourself from error, until little remains of the water.
However, if you fear for the patient, and you discharge a large amount of water, then treat him with burial in hot sand, and with much sweat in a bath and the sun, and let him fast: and treat him with drying medicines, until he is healed: and cauterize the stomach, liver, and spleen after the water extraction, as I have described in what preceded.
On the treatment of infants born with their urine passages not perforated.
Chapter LV.
When any infant emerges from its mother's womb, and the opening of its urethra is not perforated, or the opening is narrow, or in the wrong place.
Therefore, it is necessary to assist with its perforation immediately when it is born, using a very fine Spatumili, according to this form.
Then place a thin leaden nail in the opening, and bind it; and keep it there for three or four days.
When, therefore, it wishes to urinate, let it be removed, and let it urinate. Then return it.
And if you do not place lead, it is possible that the urine which flows over the place will not allow it to close.
However, the one whose opening is constricted is treated with lead, as we have said, for many days, until it is enlarged.
However, those in whom the opening is in the wrong place are harmed. For, in those who are born, the opening is at the end of the penis: therefore, they cannot urinate forward until they raise their penis upwards with their hand. Nor is there generation for him because the semen cannot reach the womb in a straight manner: and it is a very foul disease.
And the method of operation in him is that the sick person be laid on his back. Then extend his penis with a strong pull of your left hand, and cut the head of the penis from the glans, with a sharp instrument or a sharp spatula, as if cutting a reed or as if you were shaving something, so that its middle comes forth like the head of the penis, and so that the opening falls in the middle in the proper way.
And beware of the flow of blood in your operation. For this often happens. Therefore, apply to it what stops the blood, and treat the wound until it heals.
Concerning those things that occur in the foreskin and the head of the penis, and its corruption, blackness, and adhesion.
Chapter LVI.
These blemishes frequently occur on the penis, and there is a prominence of foul flesh.
And from it some are malignant, and others not malignant.
Therefore, the non-malignant one should be suspended with a fine hook, and cut away until the entire area is cleansed. Then place upon it a poultice soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then treat it afterward with the ointment dyafinicon until it heals.
If it is malignant, of foul color, then it is necessary to apply a cautery to it after its incision and scraping.
If, however, the Albather is on the foreskin of an uncircumcised person, and part of the Albather is inside the foreskin and part of it outside, then it is necessary to remove the Albather that is inside first until it is consolidated, then treat the exterior; because when you treat it simultaneously, the foreskin is not safe from being pierced.
And they also occur in the testicles, and in the foreskin, blackness and corruption. Therefore, it is necessary to cauterize all that has already blackened and is about to corrupt, or is already corrupt.
Then apply a line over it with honey that has been mixed with the flour of pomegranate bark and crushed, sifted herbs.
Then treat it with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
If however a flow of blood occurs, then apply a cautery with a lunar cautery, according to this form.
For a cautery is helpful for two conditions simultaneously, namely for a flow of blood, and a wound when it is corrupted.
If the head of the penis is corroded and destroyed in an hour, then one must insert a lead cannula into the urethra so the sick person can urinate over it.
However, when the foreskin adheres to the head of the penis, and this adhesion does not occur unless the foreskin is healthy, and circumcision is not required on it: and sometimes adhesion occurs because of a wound or abscess, therefore, it is necessary to scrape it with a blunt Spatumili until the ligament is loosened, and the head of the penis is freed from every side.
If it is difficult to discern these things thoroughly, then it is necessary to scrape off some of the head of the penis and from the foreskin. This is because when the foreskin is thin, it might perhaps be perforated quickly because of its thinness.
Then separate the foreskin and the head of the penis with a thin linen cloth that has been moistened in cold water, so that they do not adhere again.
Then treat it with astringent wine until it is consolidated.
Chapter LVII.
Concerning the cleansing of infants and the treatment of accidents that befall them.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity, like other wounds; however, since it is a procedure of our making, at our will, and its use is specifically in boys, it is necessary that we delineate a better operation and a gentler path leading to health.
Therefore, I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books, because they do not use it in their laws, and it is only something we have acquired through experience.
This is because I have found the majority of craftsmen and phlebotomists using cleansing, circumcising with a razor and scissors, and using astringents and ligatures with thread and incisions with a fingernail.
And indeed, I have already tested all these methods, and have found no better cleansing than with scissors and a ligature with thread.
Because in cleansing with a razor, the skin often escapes. Because the foreskin has two layers, therefore perhaps the outer skin is cut, and the inner layer remains; therefore, it is necessary to cut again to cut the other, and future pain will result. And with cleansing done with astringents, that is, digital, which the surgeons use, the incision of the extremity of the male member is not secured, because perhaps it enters its hole.
In cleansing with a curette, perhaps the iron slips away and spoils your operation; and the skin of a boy is short-lived, and many times they are born in such a way that they do not need cleansing; and I have seen that.
However, cleansing with scissors and ligation with thread has shown me a good experiment, because scissors are proportionate to the incision: for the sharpness that is above is like the sharpness that is below. Therefore, when you press your hand, the two sharpnesses are proportional, and they cut according to one operation at one time; and a compression of a similar shape is made, containing the skin of the member on all sides, with which error does not fall at all.
And the mode of the proper operation is that you make it seem, especially to a boy, if he is of those who understand a little, that you do not do anything but tie a thread to his member; and you leave him until another day.
Then make him rejoice and be glad in every way possible to you from it, by what his understanding receives. Then have him stand between your prepared hands, and let him not be seated; and hide the scissors in your sleeve, or under your foot, so that the boy does not see them at all, nor anything of the instruments.
Then introduce your hand to his member, and blow into the skin, and pull it upward until the head of the member emerges. Then cleanse it from the dirt that has collected on it.
Then tie the marked place with a double thread. Afterwards, tie a second ligature a little lower. Then hold the lower ligature's location with your index finger and thumb, with good retention, and cut between the two ligatures. Then lift the skin upwards quickly, and extract the head of the rod. Then allow the blood to run slowly (for it is better) and place powder on top. And upon the powder, place a cloth on which the yolk of a hard-boiled egg in rosewater has been spread, mixed with good fresh rose oil. And leave it on top until the next day, and treat it with the rest of the treatment until healed.
The form of the scissors for cleaning is this.
Let the two ends be blunt and intersecting, so that the nail's branch does not protrude from them; and let the length of the two sharp points be equal to the length of the two handles.
An error, however, occurring in cleaning is that perhaps the inner skin entirely, or part of it, escapes at the incision.
Therefore, it is necessary to extend it from your time with your fingernail before the abscess of the place, and to cut it according to equality.
If you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then pull it out with a hook and cut it.
However, if three days have passed and that which is under the rod remains swollen, suppurated, then leave it until the abscess is ripe, and remove the pus with ease, and cut it according to what is prepared for you, and be cautious of the head of the rod. For that will not harm it.
Therefore, treat it with what consolidates a wound from the powders, which we have described in the treatise on powders, and if it is cut from the skin beyond measure and protrudes to the upper regions, that will not harm it either.
Therefore, treat it with what we have said, until it is healed.
Chapter LVIII.
Concerning the extraction of retained urine in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder occurs from an obstruction caused by a stone, or congealed blood, or pus, or a grown flesh, and similar things to those which we have mentioned from the methods of treatment in the division.
And if the urine is not discharged, and you see that its retention is due to a stone, which is already in the neck of the bladder: then it is necessary that he be weak, bent over his knees. Then his feet should be placed upon his back, and he should extend his stomach from the upper part of his back, and the weak man should strain his breath as much as possible for him. Thus, the stone is forced from the neck of the bladder towards the rear: wherefore the urine is discharged.
If it is not released with what we have said, and the matter is severe upon the sick person, then it is necessary that you administer its extraction with an instrument, which is called a syringe, whose form is this: Let it be of silver, and let it be fine, smooth, concave, like a bird's feather quill in its fineness, long in the likeness of one and a half palms, to which a fine tube should be attached to its head.
And the method of the urine's exit through it is that you should take a doubled thread, and tie to its end wool, or tow, with a good ligature, and insert the end of the thread into the lower part of the syringe; and cut with scissors if any wool remains, but insert it into the cannula with force, like a lever.
Then anoint the syringe with oil, or with butter, and with egg white, and make the sick person sit on a seat, and embrocate the bladder, and its tube with moist oils, and olive oil, and lukewarm water.
Then introduce the syringe into the tube with ease, until it reaches the root of the tube.
Then double it upward from the navel.
Then push the syringe into it, until it reaches the bladder, and the sick person feels that it has reached the empty place; and this is not done in this order unless because the passage, in which it goes, is one in which there is tortuosity.
Then extend the thread with wool gradually. For urine will follow the wool.
Then pull it out, and cause the urine to run through the urinary passage; and repeat the syringe, and do not cease to do it, until the bladder is emptied, and the sick person finds relief.
Concerning the quality of bladder clysterization with a Clepsydra.
Chapter LIX.
When a sore occurs in the bladder, or blood coagulates in it, or pus is confined in it, and the force is to distill into it waters and medicines: then let it be done with the instrument which is called Clepsydra, and this is its form.
It is indeed deaf, that is, closed. And in it are three openings, two on one side, and one on the other side, as you see.
And the concave place, in which that which propels it is, be according to the quantity that fills it without addition, until when something is pulled with it, humidity is drawn out; and when it is propelled, it is expelled with it to a distance: according to what the clepsydra does, by which naphtha, that is, Greek fire, is projected in naval battles.
Therefore, when the force is to project the humors into the bladder, introduce the extremity of the clepsydra into the humor, and pull with that which propels it upwards, for the humor is drawn into the concavity of the clepsydra.
Then introduce its extremity into the tube, according to what we have narrated in the syringe. Then propel the humor with that which propels it; for that humor reaches the bladder immediately, so that the sick person feels it.
A subtle form of clyster for injecting the bladder.
Let it be made of silver, or brass: whose head be higher, similar to a small alcamas, and beneath it a sinus into which a ligament falls.
Then take the bladder of a ram, and put into it the humor with which you wish to inject the bladder. Then tie it between two sinuses with a strong ligature, using a double thread: and warm that humor a little over the fire.
Then introduce the end of the clyster into the tube, then squeeze your hand over the bladder with the appropriate constriction of the humor until the infirm feels that the humor has already reached the bladder.
If, however, you do not have a bladder present, then take a piece of parchment, and make a circle from it, and pierce it from all its circularity. Then introduce a strong thread into those holes, as they revolve. Then gather the circle, as a loaf of bread is gathered: after you have put in it what you wish of humors, and oils, and waters.
Then tie it to the instrument: and do with it as you did with the bladder by pressing with your hand, until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter LX.
On the extraction of the Stone.
We have already spoken in the division of the species of stone, and its treatment with medicines: and I have stated the difference between a stone generated in the kidneys and the bladder, and the signs thereof in its entirety: and I have described the stone in which operation with the hand and incision is required: and the stone generated in the bladder properly is such: and that which is fixed in the urethra: and I am recounting it with clear exposition and brevity.
Therefore, I say that a stone generated in the bladder happens most often in children.
And among its signs is that the urine issues from the bladder like water in its subtlety: and white sand appears in it, and the infirm person rubs their urethra and suffers with it, and often hangs down. Then it rises and, with it, the anus opens in many of them.
And the cure is easy for children, so long as they reach fourteen years. And it is difficult in the elderly. However, in young people it is intermediate, in that it lies between those two: and the one whose stone is larger, the cure is easier; and if it is small, the cure is the opposite of that.
Therefore, when we come to the cure, it is necessary first to cleanse the sick person with an enema, which will remove all the faeces that are in their intestines: for it sometimes hinders the finding of the stone during examination.
Then the sick person should be taken with their feet, and shaken and moved downwards, so that the stone descends to the depth of the bladder, or jumps from a high place sometimes.
Then have him sit between your hands prepared, and let his hands be under his thighs, so that the entire bladder is inclined downwards.
Then examine him and touch him externally.
If therefore you feel a stone in the space, then quickly proceed with a section upon it.
If it does not fall under your touch at all, then you must wipe your index finger with the oil of your left hand, if the sick person is a child; or the middle finger if he is a full youth; and insert it into his anus, and examine for the stone until it is under your finger; and turn it gradually towards the neck of the bladder.
Then press upon it with your finger, and drive it outwards against the place whose section you wish; and command the attendant to press the bladder with his hand, and command another attendant to extend the testicles with his right hand upwards; and with his other hand, to remove the skin which is under the testicles at the side of the place where the section is.
Then introduce a cutting spatule, whose shape is this. And make a cut in that which is between the anus and testicles, and not in the middle, to the side of the left buttock; and let the section be upon the stone, and your finger be in the anus; and let the section be transverse, so that the section be wide externally and narrow internally, according to the amount that it is possible for the stone to exit from it no greater.
For perhaps the finger, which is in the anus pressing the stone at the section, and it emerges without difficulty.
And know that if the stone has angles and edges, therefore its exit becomes difficult because of that.
And if it is smooth, like a gland, and round: and its exit becomes easy.
Therefore, to the one that has angles and edges, add to the fissure.
If it does not emerge thus, then it is necessary to devise upon it; or to bind upon it with a suitable bandage, whose extremity is like the Moon, which binds upon the stone and does not escape from it; or to introduce under it a subtle instrument, of curved extremity.
If however you cannot upon it, then enlarge the opening a little. If anything of blood overcomes you, cut it with a zegi.
If however the stones are more than one, then drive the largest first to the opening of the bladder. Then cut upon it. Afterwards drive the small one after it; and similarly do if there are more than two.
If it is very large, then it is ignorance to cut over it with a large incision. Because it happens to the patient one of two things, either that he dies, or that he suffers constant leakage of urine; because the place is not entirely consolidated.
But administer its expulsion until it emerges; or apply ingenuity in its fracture with forceps until you extract it piece by piece.
When you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with incense, aloe, and dragon's blood; and bind it, and place over it a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or rose oil, and cold water; as is done for a hot abscess.
Then lay the sick person on his back, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day. When you have loosened it, embrocate the place with water and much oil.
Then treat it with diaphaniac ointment and basil ointment until it is healed.
If however a hot abscess is added to the wound, and corrosions, and things similar to those: as if the blood were congealed in the bladder, and the urine were prevented from passing; and it is recognized by the passage of blood with the urine. Therefore, insert your finger into the wound, and extract that blood. For if it remains, it provokes the corruption of the bladder and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt; and apply whatever is appropriate for treatment, of every kind, until it is healed.
And it is necessary in the entire time of treatment, that you tie the two thighs, and collect, for the reason of the medicines placed on the site.
If there is a small stone in the passage of the penis, and it is fixed there, and prevents the exit of urine, then treat it with what I said before you reach the stage of incision. For many times you will be spared the incision by this treatment, for I have already proven it.
And it is that you take a perforator of iron, whose form is this, with a triangular point, sharp, fixed in wood.
Then take thread, and tie the penis with it under the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then introduce the piercing iron into the penis with ease, until the perforator reaches the stone itself: and turn the perforator with your hand on the stone gradually: and try to pierce it, until you make it penetrate on the other side.
For then urine is released immediately.
Then press your hand upon that which remains of the stone from the outer side of the penis. For it will break and come out with the urine, and the sick person is healed.
If however this treatment is not prepared for you, due to something that prevents it: then tie a thread under the stone, and another thread above the stone. Then cut above the stone on the penis itself between the two ligatures. Then extract it. Afterwards, loosen the ligature, and cleanse the congealed blood which forms in the wound.
And a ligature of thread under the stone is not necessary unless so that it does not return to the bladder; and another ligature on top, unless so that when the thread is loosened after the stone's expulsion, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound.  And for that reason, it is necessary for you to lift the skin upwards when you tie the upper thread, so that it returns to its proper place and covers the wound, as we said.
Chapter LXI.
Concerning the extraction of a stone in women.
Little stone is generated in women.
However, if a stone happens to any of them, then the cure is difficult, and it is forbidden due to many reasons.
One of them is: that the woman is perhaps a virgin.
And the second is, because you do not find a woman who reveals herself to the physician, when she is chaste, or having husbands.
The third is, because you do not find a woman who well knows this art, especially the operation with the hand.
And the fourth is the distance from the stone's location: wherefore it needs a deep incision, and in it is fear.
If necessity provokes it, then it is necessary that you take a female physician, well-knowing: and few are found.
If, however, you entrust it to her, then seek a chaste, skillful physician, and present a skilled midwife well-versed in women's matters, or a woman who hints at a part of this art: make her present, and especially to her, that she may do everything you command her from inquiry about the stone.
Firstly, that he should see if she is a virgin: then it is necessary that he insert a finger into her anus and seek the stone.
If therefore he finds it, he should grasp it under his finger, and then command the midwife to insert her finger into the weak one's vagina, and inquire about the stone, after placing her left hand on the bladder and compressing it with a good pressure.
If, therefore, he finds the stone, then it is necessary that he gradually move it from the opening of the bladder downwards with all his strength, until he reaches the root of the thigh with it.
Then he shall cut upon it at the opposing half of the vulva at the root of the thigh, from whichever side it is prepared for him, and feels the stone in that part; and his finger shall not be removed from the stone, grasping under it; and let the incision be small at first. Then insert a probe into that incision.
Then insert a probe into that incision. If, therefore, he feels the stone, then let him add to the incision according to the proportionate amount that the stone emerges from it.
And know that the species of stone are many. For among these there are small, and large, smooth and rough, long, and round ones with branches.
Therefore, know its species, so that this may be made clear to you concerning that which you wish.
If, therefore, blood overcomes you, then add in the place of the zegi, ground, and hold it for one hour, until the blood is cut off.
Then return to your operation, until the stone emerges: and do as I have prepared for you, from the instruments, which I said in the extraction of the stone in men, that you may aid yourself by them in your operation.
If, however, a flow of blood overcomes you, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery that has been cut: then put powder on the place, and bind it with ligatures of fitting constriction: and do not change it; and leave the stone, and do not extract it. For perhaps the weak woman will die. Then treat the wound.
When therefore the sharpness of the blood subsides after days, and the place becomes putrid, then return to your operation, until the stone emerges.
Chapter LX II.
Concerning the incision of a watery hernia.
A watery hernia is not present unless there is an aggregation of moisture in a white sifac, which is under the skin of the testicle containing the egg; and it is called sifac.
And sometimes it is in its own membrane, which nature prepared for it in the part of the egg, until it rots; which is another egg, and is the skin of the testicle between the white sifac, which we have said; and that does not happen except rarely.
And indeed the generation of this hernia is from the weakness which occurs in the testicles; wherefore this matter is poured out upon them. And sometimes it happens from a blow to the testicles.
And this moisture has many colors. For indeed its color is either sloping toward yellowness, or is blood-red, or fecal, black, or watery white, and that is what is more.
And the signs by which it is known where the collection of water is, are these. For if it is in the white sifac that we spoke of, then the abscess is round in length a little like the shape of an egg: and the testicle does not appear, because the moisture contains it on every side.
And if the moisture is in its own tissue, then the abscess is round like part of an egg, and because of this a man estimates that it is another egg.
And if the moisture is between the skin of the testicles and the white sifac, then it falls under the senses.
Therefore, when you desire knowledge of the color of the moisture, then experience the color of the moisture in the abscess with the inserted square, whose form preceded. Therefore, according to that which emerges in the trace of the Insertion, judge about that which is between it.
When, therefore, we come to treatment with iron, then it is necessary that you command the sick person, that he be bled if it is possible for him, and you see his body full.
Then let him be laid upon his back upon a slightly raised object, and place many cloths beneath him.
Then sit you upon his left side, and command the attendant to sit on his right, who shall extend his rod to one of the two sides of the skin of the testicles, and to the region of the navel of the abdomen.
Then take a wide spatula, and cut the skin of the testicles from the middle lengthwise, until near the pubis; and let the incision be according to the straightness of an equidistant line that divides the skin of the testicles, until it reaches the white substance enclosing it; and scrape it and observe it, or beware of this, that you sever it; and let your scraping, by which you scrape it, be on the part that hangs more with the testicle; and carefully probe with the scraping according to the amount that is possible for you.
Then pierce the substance full of water with a large perforation, and remove all the water.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with hooks, and extend the substance upward, and do not touch the skin encompassing the testicles; and cut the substance however it is possible for you to cut it, either entirely or in pieces, especially its thin side. For if you do not carefully investigate, there is no certainty in its incision, that the water will not return.
If, however, the testicle protrudes externally from its skin during your operation, then when you have completed the incision of the substance, return it. Then bring together the two lips of the skin of the testicles with sutures.
Then treat it like the other wounds, until it is healed.
If you find a corrupted testicle with a different blackness, then it is necessary to tie the vessels, which are suspensory, fearing the flow of blood.
Then cut off the testicle with its suspensory ligament, and extract the testicle: afterwards treat it as we have said.
If, however, water has accumulated in two parts, then know that there are two branches: therefore, cut the other part in the same way as you did to the other, equally.
And if it is equal to you, so that it is one operation, then do it: then wipe away the blood. And introduce into the sections wool dipped in oil, or in rose oil: and place outside other wool which you have dipped in wine and oil, and spread it over the two testicles and the belly: and place over them doubled cloths, and they are cushions: and tie them over with a bandage, which has six parts according to this form.
Then take two cloths, and sew them together, and fill them with dyed wool, according to this likeness: and let the ends with which it is pressed be made of linen, or damp wool: and let them be joined together with a bandage on the lumbar region of the breeches: and one end will take over the anus up to the back, and the second will take to the side of the pelvis: and two ends over the two thighs below, and two other ends over the two groins, and let the entire bandage be gathered in the lumbar region of the breeches.
And sometimes this branch is artificially treated with a cautery at the place of the incision with iron: and it is that you take a thin, knife-like cautery, and cut with it the skin of the testicles: and it is heated, as we have described: until, when a white, water-containing substance is exposed, then take the cautery, according to this form.
It resembles a Greek letter.
Then pierce it with it, and let it be heated, until all the moisture is expelled.
Then spread the sifac with hooks, and scrape it with a heated thin cautery, and cut it as much as possible, until you eradicate the whole of it.
And be careful, from the egg, lest the fire touch it.
And know that this operation is safer, and further from the flow of blood, and better than the operation with a section.
If, however, contrariety arises for you at the hour of the section, or at the hour of the cautery a hot abscess, or a flow of blood, or anything else: then it is necessary that you treat whatever contrariety arises for you from that with the cure that is suitable for it, according to what has been said before.
If, however, the sick person is timid, and does not expect this operation, due to its horror: then administer the perforation according to what I narrate it: and it is so.
When it is certified to you by what we have said concerning signs, that the branch is watery, then it is necessary that you make the sick person sit upon a raised seat.
Then perforate the abscess in its lower part with a wide spatula, and place the perforation along the length of the body, and let the opening be wide, until all the water runs out.
Then place a dressing over the affected area, and bind it, and allow the remaining water to run out; then treat it until the wound is healed.
If, however, there is resistance to the outflow of water at the time you perforate it, and this is only because a white membrane creates resistance in the wound opening and hinders the escape of the water: then it is necessary that you insert a pen into the wound, or an instrument with which the water of dropsy is extracted, whose form preceded, or add to the wound operation for a little while.
