And know that the water returns, and collects again after six months, or about that. When it collects again, then it should be perforated according to the same method, and the sick person will pass their days.
Chapter LXIII.
Concerning the treatment of fleshy tumors.
Know that in the incision upon this tumor, there is fear leading to destruction in many dispositions. Therefore, it seems to me that its removal is saving from it.
And I am recalling the operation upon it, and the appearance of the abscess.
I say therefore that many abscesses occur in the bodies, of which the testicles are composed: and it happens from many causes, namely.
Or from an acute or sharp superfluity that is poured onto the testicles. Or from a blow.
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it: and it is a serious abscess.
And perhaps it is a stony abscess, whose color is dark, and no feeling is in it.
And sometimes it arises from the knotting of arteries, and their swelling: as its recollection has already preceded: or from the swelling of veins.
Therefore, that which arises from the swelling of the arteries is known by the arrangement of the abscess when you compress it with your fingers. And it is not necessary that you touch it completely.
That which is made from the swelling of veins, however, does not spread from it anything upon compression with the fingers.
And now it is possible that a section be made upon this kind, and upon the remaining flesh: and it is as if you separate the skin of the testicles. Then extend the egg upwards, and extract it with white sifach: and free the suspensory from the vessels, and tie the vessels: and cut the suspensory after you have freed it from all parts of the egg.
If therefore the egg is consolidated with those born flesh, then it is necessary that you pull the egg, and cut it.
And if there is adhesion between some of the siphaket, or in that which is between the vessels, then it is necessary that you free all that adhesion, and cut it with a circular incision.
If however there is a birth of flesh in the place of adhesion which is behind, then it is necessary that you cut all of it, and extract the egg, as we said.
When therefore your operation is completed, then fill the wound with wool infused in rose oil and wine; then treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter LXIII.
Of the treatment of Ramicis, which is with the Vite.
Vitis is an abscess enveloped by a certain wrapping, similar to a bunch of grapes, with a softening of the testicles; and it makes difficult for the weak person movement and exercise, and walking.
And in the treatment of this illness there is fear, because of that which preceded it in remembrance.
But it is necessary, as we say, for the operation on it to be according to what the first ones did.
I say therefore that it is necessary to make the sick person sit on a raised seat. Then, push down the suspensory of the testicles. Then grasp the skin of the testicles with your fingers, near the vessels that are close to the penis; and let another servant hold them, and stretch them with a strong extension.
Then, cut with a wide, sharp spatula, a transverse section directly across the vessels, until the vessels are exposed.
Then, scrape it from every side, as we told you in the extraction of the arteries that are in the temples.
Afterwards, fasten a needle with a double thread to them, and tie it in the first of the places where the veins occur: and tie it again in the last of them. Then cut it in the middle, in a straight section along the length of the body, and extract what is gathered of putrid, corrupted humors.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds, in which pus is likely to generate, so that the vessels nourishing one testicle fall. For there is no evil through it.
If, however, it happens to all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be extracted along with the vessels, so that the egg is not deprived of nourishment because of the incision, and it wastes away, or dries up, and there is no help with them.
Chapter LXV.
On the treatment of intestinal colic.
The outcome of this colic is from a section that occurs in the distended sifach over the abdomen in the groin area from a marvel of the belly.
Why do the intestines descend from that rupture to one testicle? And this rupture is either from the sifach, or its extension.
And these two types occur from many causes: either from a blow, or a jump, or a shout, or the lifting of a heavy object, and similar things.
And a sign, when it is from the extension of the sifach. It is that which happens gradually over a long time, and does not happen suddenly: and there is an abscess equal to the depth: because the sifach compresses the intestine.
And a sign of it, when it is from a section of the sifach, because it causes intense pain to occur suddenly from its beginning; and it is a different abscess, appearing under the skin nearby; and that is because of the exit of the intestine and its course towards the exterior of the sifach.
And sometimes it emerges with the intestine into the zirbum; and this is called a Ramex intestinalis, and zirbia; and sometimes it occurs with flatulence.
And sometimes faeces run into the intestine and are retained there; and with it is the harm of the sick person, because it causes a slight pain, and especially when it is pressed.
And in the treatment of the species of this illness, there is fear with iron; therefore it is necessary that a fall be avoided in it.
And the method of the operation is, that you command the sick person to reduce the intestine with his hand into the interior of his cavity, if it obeys the return. Then let him be laid back on his neck between your hands, and elevate his legs.
Then stretch the skin which follows the skin upwards, and cut the skin of the testicles completely along its length. Then fix hooks in the two lips of the incision, according to what you need to open the incision with them; and let the incision be, according to the amount, that it is possible for an egg to come out of it.
Then scrape the siphak, and what is under the skin of the testicles, until when a white, hard siphak is revealed on every side: then introduce your index finger into that which follows the testicle, into that which is between the white siphak, which is under the skin of the egg, and the second siphak, and detach with it the adhesion which is on the posterior side of the egg.
Then move the skin of the testicles inwards with your right hand, and with this lift the white siphak upwards with your left hand, and lift the egg with the siphak towards the incision; and order the servant to lift the egg upwards; and you detach the adhesion which is behind the complete separation.
And examine with your fingers, that there is nothing there from the intestine wrapped in the hard white siphak. If however you find something from it, then push it towards the lower belly.
Then take a needle, in which is a thick thread, made of linen from ten threads; and introduce it near the last siphak, that is, the tunic, which is under the skin of the testicles following the incision; then cut the ends that protrude from the thread, until there are four threads. Then place some of them over others in a cross shape; and tie with them the alsifach, about which we spoke, because it is under the skin of the testicles with a strong ligature, until no blood vessels which nourish them can send anything to them: so that a hot abscess may not arise from it. And place again a bandage second, outside the first bandage, distant from it by less than two fingers. And after these two bandages, leave from the siphak, which is under the skin of the testicles, a quantity the size of a finger; and cut the rest completely around, and remove the egg from it.
Then cut in the lower part of the skin of the testicles the incision from which blood and pus flows, as we described in what has gone before.
Then apply wool soaked in oil, and place it in the wound; and apply the bandage that we described. And sometimes the white siphak that we mentioned is cauterized after its incision, fearing a flow of blood. For this often happens. And leave the bandage until it falls off by itself.
If however its falling is delayed, then it should be moistened with warm water; and place upon it that which we mentioned, until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with the remaining treatment of wounds, until it is healed.
Chapter LXVI.
Concerning the windy ram.
In this type of ram, I have seen no one who dared to use iron for its treatment.
And the first, indeed, said that it happens as we have described in the ram which is with the vine. That is, to tie the vessels after incision, to pull them down with ease; then to cut in the middle, and to treat it finally with that which opens it, until the vessels fall; and to treat the wound, as we said, until it is healed.
Chapter LXVII.
Of the rupture which occurs in the groin.
When rupture occurs in the groin, as we said, and the area protrudes, and nothing from the intestines descends into the testicles; and if it does descend, it is little, and returns at all hours; but if time is prolonged with it, a fissure is added in the scrotum, until the intestines descend into the scrotum.
And that happens from the stretching of the scrotum, which is in the groin, as we said. That is, because the scrotum is stretched; then the area softens, and an abscess forms, and it protrudes.
And its treatment is with a cautery, as its previous description stated.
And sometimes it is treated with an iron, according to this method: and it is that the sick person lies on their back between your hands. Then cut the place of the abscess and protruding rupture, with a cut across its width, according to the amount of three fingers.
Then squeeze the scrotum and what is beneath the skin, until when the white scrotum is uncovered, which is beneath the skin that follows it.
Then take a rod, and place it over the protruding place of the scrotum, and press it to the depth of the belly.
Then sew two protruding places on the end of the rod from the scrotum, and connect one of them with the other with a suture; then pull out the end of the rod.
And do not cut the scrotum at all, nor stretch the egg, nor anything else, as I have made you know in the treatment of the intestinal branch.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds. When therefore you cut the threads, cleanse it, and restore the wound until it is consolidated.
For the scrotum contracts, and no protrusion of the rupture is added.
And the cautery is better in this place, because it is closer to health.
Chapter LXVIII.
Concerning the softening of the skin of the testicles.
Often the skin of the testicles is softened in some men, on account of which the flesh between them is softened, and its appearance is disfigured.
Therefore it is necessary for the one who is asked about its treatment, to make the sick person lie on his back, and to cut off all the skin that is softened over the natural skin.
Then he shall join the two lips with suture; and if he wishes, he shall sew at first the excess of softened skin with three sutures, or four, and make the suture firm.
Then cut between the sutures.
Then treat the remaining wounds with treatment, until they are healed, and the threads fall out.
Chapter LXIX.
On Castration.
Know that castration is excommunicated in our law, and because of that it would be permissible for me not to mention it in this book of mine; and its mention is not necessary except for two reasons.
One of which is that it should be in the knowledge of the physician when questioned about it, and so that he may know the treatment for whoever suffers it.
And the other reason is that we frequently need the castration of certain animals, for our benefit through it, such as rams, boars, and cats, and similar animals.
Therefore, I say that castration is done in two ways. Or by attrition. Or by fissure and section.
In that which is done by attrition, the method of operation is: that we should place the animal in warm water, until its testicles are softened and relaxed, and hang down; then rub them with your hand, until they are dissolved and no longer appear under the touch.
In castration by fissure and section, it is necessary that you hold the animal, and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament, and cut over each testicle with one incision, until when two testicles are exposed; then cut them, after having scraped each one, and do not leave anything on them except a fine sifak, which is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than that which is done by attrition, because attrition perhaps leaves something of the testicles, wherefore the animal desires intercourse.
Then care for the wound until it heals.
Chapter 70.
Concerning the treatment of a hermaphrodite, two methods.
A hermaphrodite is from males in two ways. One of them is, because it appears in it what follows a space, or in the skin of the testicles, in what is between the two testicles, a figure like a woman's vulva, in which there are hairs; and sometimes urine flows from what is in the skin of the testicles.
In women, however, there is one species, and it is a woman's vulva on the pubis as small, completely male testicles, protruding outward: one of which is like a man's penis, and two like two testicles.
And the treatment of three species, two from men, and one species from women, is: because it is necessary to cut away the added flesh, until their impression escapes. Then treat them with the treatment of the other wounds.
In the second species of men, from which urine emerges, which is in the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor is there any treatment of it at all.
Chapter LXXI.
Concerning the incision of the Tentigo, and the protruding flesh in women's vulvas.
Tentigo perhaps is added upon the natural thing, until it is disfigured and its appearance becomes ugly; and sometimes it magnifies in some women so much, until it expands like in men, and reaches to the point of intercourse.
Therefore, you must hold the excess of Tentigo with your hand, or with a hook, and make an incision; and do not be extreme in the incision, especially in the deep root, so that a flow of blood does not occur.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds, until it is healed.
However, the flesh that arises is flesh that is born in the orifice of the womb, until it fills it; and perhaps it emerges to the exterior in the likeness of a tail; and because of that, some first-named physicians call the ailment the tailed sickness. Therefore, it is necessary to incise it as we incise Tentigo, and to treat it until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of Alratica, which is the unpierced vulva of a woman.
Chapter LXXII.
Alratica is, as it were, a woman's vulva that is not perforated, or is a small opening. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is from illness that preceded: and indeed it comes about either from added flesh-growth, or from a subtle or thick sifach; and it arises either deep within the womb, or on its sides, or in its upper part, or its lower part; and it prevents intercourse and conception, and childbirth, and perhaps it prevents menstruation.
And its knowledge comes about through the obstetrician's observation, if an apparent obstruction is nearby.
If however it is not apparent, then seek it with your fingers, or with a rod.
If however the obstruction is from a subtle sifach, and is near the two lips: then hurry, and break it apart.
And it is, that you place cushions on the two lips.
Then place your index fingers of both hands together, and let the woman be on her back with her legs outstretched. Then extend the two lips with strength, until the subtle sifach is broken and the obstruction is opened.
Then take wool and soak it in oil, and place it over the place: and let the woman do this every day, so that the place does not consolidate again.
If however the sifach is thick, dense: then it is necessary to cut it away with a broad spatula similar to a myrtle leaf.
If however the obstruction is born of flesh, then suspend it with hooks, and cut it: and have with you medicines that cease the flow of blood without causing a bite, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then administer a wide lead cannula, so that the wound does not quickly consolidate, hold it for several days: and administer linen cloth made of dry flax, then treat the rest with the usual treatment, until it heals.
And sometimes in the womb there are births of other fleshy masses: it is necessary therefore that they be cut in this way: as long as it is not a cancerous abscess; for a cancerous abscess that is in the womb, there should be no conflict with iron at all.
Chapter LXXIII.
Concerning the treatment of hemorrhoids, Athehelil, and red boils in women's vulvas.
Hemorrhoids are indeed an opening of the veins' orifices, until much blood flows from them continuously.
When hemorrhoids grow old, they become thelil; and I have already spoken in the division of their species and their signs; and I will say here what receives treatment from them and what does not.
Therefore, I say that hemorrhoids which are in the orifice of the womb, and do not appear to the senses, then there is no cure for them with iron.
However, those of them which are in the orifice of the womb above which sense falls, are those which are cured.
Therefore, it is necessary to introduce the woman into a cool house. Then, extend the thelil with scissors, or with a rough cloth, and cut them from their roots.
Then, sprinkle over them, at the flow of blood, from one powder of the incisions without mordication: such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense, and similar to them.
Then let him drive her feet against the wall for one hour. Afterwards, place her in cold water.
If the flow of blood persists: then make her sit in a decoction of sumac, and quince bark, and gallnuts, and similar things.
Then take barley flour, and make it with honey and vinegar, and make a plaster with it over her back.
If therefore the blood is cut off with it, it is good; and if not, make cupping adhere to the back and armpits, without scarification.
Then take wool, and immerse it in the juice of tarathit, or the juice of arnoglossa, or the juice of alaulat branches, that is, aglanter, Pontic wine, and make it adhere to the wound.
When therefore the abscess is quiet, then treat the place with ointments, until it is healed.
Albarth, however, is red, similar to alhaasse heads, and is of harsh appearance.
Therefore, you should cut away what appears from it, according to what I said in the thelil equally, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter LXXIV.
Concerning the perforation of the exit which happens in the womb.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the womb, such as cancer, and stony abscess, and ulcers, and adubelati, and alekilati, that is, herpes, carbuncles, and fistulas, and hemorrhoids, and cracks, and thelil, and hot abscess.
And I have already spoken of all these illnesses and their varieties, and their signs, and their treatments in the divisions; it is necessary that I say in this treatise about the hot abscess that occurs in the womb, when it is from the abscesses which collect pus, and you wish to know how its opening is made with an iron.
Therefore, it is necessary to consider. Then if the pain of the abscess in its beginning is sharp with its percussion, and inflammation, and fever; and you see it red; if the sensation falls upon it, then do not hurry with its perforation; and treat it with that which helps in its maturation, until when the sharpness of the symptoms subsides.
Then it is necessary that you make the woman sit on a seat that has two cushions, and that she recline, or be supported on her back, and that she draw her feet towards the lower part of her abdomen; and that her thighs be spread apart, and that she place her arms under her knees, and tie each with a suitable bandage to it.
Then let the midwife sit on the right side, and administer the instrument with which the opening of the womb is opened.
And it is necessary, when you wish to administer this instrument, that you place it with the radius deep within the woman's womb, so that what enters from the instrument is not more than the depth of the womb, and harms the weak one.
However, if the instrument is more than the depth, then it is necessary that you place cushions on the lining of the womb's depth, so that the entire instrument's entrance into the depth of the womb is prohibited; and it is necessary that the winding, which runs along the instrument, be placed from the upper side; and that the attendant holds the instrument and turns the winding until the depth of the womb is opened.
When therefore the exit appears, and is touched by the hand, and is smooth and soft, then it is necessary to separate it at its head with a wide spatula.
When therefore the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary to place a linen cloth soaked in rose oil, or green oil, in which there is some astringency, on the wound; and place the cloth, and extract the section in the depth of the womb.
And place externally over the womb, and over the lining, clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
Then treat it after two days with an ointment suitable for it, until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and the wound are washed, so that it is irrigated with water and honey; then with water in which the lily root or long aristolochia has been boiled; then return to the ointment.
If the wound is hidden inside the womb, then it is necessary to refrain from treating it with iron, indeed, treat it with what we said in the division.
Chapter LXX.
Teachings of Midwives, how living embryos are treated, having emerged in a non-natural form.
It is necessary for the midwife to first know the form of natural birth.
Therefore, by its signs, when you see a woman pressing downwards, and desiring to smell the air, and feeling relieved from that which is upon her concerning childbirth, and the embryo's exit is hastened: then know that this birth is according to the natural form, and its exit is above its head, and the afterbirth with it: or hanging, or depending from the navel.
Therefore, when you see these signs, then it is necessary that her abdomen be pressed, so that the embryo descends quickly.
For when it descends upon its head, the afterbirth descends with it, and it is cleansed from those superfluities by good cleansing.
However, that which is besides this birth is unnatural, blameworthy.
Because sometimes the fetus emerges upon its feet, and its body emerges before its head, and its feet, and its hands together, or one of its feet: or its head and its hand emerge: or its navel emerges: and perhaps it turns upon its neck, and similar things from blameworthy figures.
Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to be prudent, subtle in all these matters, and to beware of error and deception: and I am showing every figure among them, and how the nature is in it, and how it should be interpreted and dealt with.
When the fetus emerges upon its head in its natural exit, and it becomes violent upon the woman, and difficult is her delivery, and you see her strength already weakened: then have her sit on a seat, or let women hold her: and steam her vulva with decocted fennel and with moist oils.
Then let the midwife insert a small spatula between her fingers, and split the afterbirth with it, and cut it with her nail until that which is in it of moisture flows: and press the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If, however, it does not descend, then it is necessary for the woman to be administered an enema of fennel mucilage, with sesame oil.
Then command her, after the enema, to be compressed: and make her sneeze with condiments, and hold her mouth and nose for an hour. For the fetus will emerge immediately.
If, however, two hands of the fetus emerge first, it is necessary that you return them with ease gradually. If, however, it does not obey in its entry, then place the woman upon a seat, and raise her feet upwards; then shake the seat on the ground: and the woman must be held so that she does not fall during the shaking.
If, therefore, the hands do not emerge, and the fetus dies, then cut them and extract the remainder of the fetus: and tie its hand with a cloth, and extract it with equality; for it will emerge.
The exit of the embryo is above its feet. When the fetus emerges above its feet, then it is necessary that you push them upwards; then turn the fetus gradually; afterwards make it equal.
Then take one of its feet and extend it gradually.
When, therefore, they emerge up to the hips, then order him to be compressed, and cause him to sneeze with condiments; for it will emerge, if God wills.
And if it does not emerge with what we have described, then return the fetus gradually until you place it in its natural form. For it will then emerge with easy exit.
If it is prevented with all that we have described, then take from marsh-mallow mucilage, and sesame oil, and dissolved gum: and pound the whole in a mortar with good pounding: then apply it with the woman's vulva, and the lower part of her abdomen. Then make her sit in warm water until she achieves hypocondriacs.
When therefore you see that her lower parts are now warmed, then make her a poultice of myrrh, and apply it. When therefore the poultice holds for one hour, then make her sit on a seat. Then make her sneeze, and pinch her nose and mouth, and press the lower part of her abdomen with gentle pressure. For the fever will depart immediately.
The exit of the fetus is upon the knees and hands. When it exits in this way, then gently insert her hands. Then the woman will lie back on her back, and let her feet hang down: and let her lie on the bed, and fold her hands. Then press on her abdomen gently. Then pull out what is under her feet from the cushions, until it is prepared in the lower part. If the fetus does not emerge, then take her feet and move them with vigorous motion. Then press on what is above the hypochondria gently, until the fetus ascends upwards. Then let her insert her hands, and gently adjust the fetus: and tell the woman to press, until the fetus emerges.
The exit of the fetus, hanging lengthwise from the hands. Let his hand be introduced into the reduction. If however he cannot reduce it, then lift the woman, and make her walk.
If, however, she cannot walk, then make her lie on the bed, and shake her legs with a violent shaking.
Then administer the liniment that you prepared from the mucilaginous substances.
If she receives it by childbirth, then make her sit on a seat. Then strive in the reduction of the hand, and restore the foetus to its natural shape. And make the woman sneeze, and command her to compress herself until it emerges.
The exit of the foetus is over her neck, with outstretched hands, and its face is toward the mother's back. The midwife must hold the foetus's hand, then gradually turn it over, and straighten it easily.
When it is straightened, then place on its vulva what we have described of oil and mucilages.
Then command her to move gradually. When you have done that, then make her sit on a seat, and command her to lean to the right side. Then command her to compress herself, and make her sneeze with condiments.
The exit of the prepared fetus on the side. When it descends to the orifice of the womb in this manner with the afterbirth, then cut the afterbirth with your fingernail until it is evacuated from the moisture.
If therefore it is suspended over the face of the fetus, and its neck, then cut it from the navel so that it is not retained and dies.
Then push the fetus inwards until you place it as it should be, and command the woman to compress herself several times; for it will exit with a slight expulsion.
The exit of twins, or many fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three or four are born. However, that is unusual and rarely happens.
However, five is a thing outside nature, and they do not survive at all; and though they multiply, they are contained by a single afterbirth, and are separated by a membrane, distinguishing them, tied to the umbilical cord of each of them.
And sometimes they emerge like a single fetus emerges by a natural, easy exit, and they emerge by an unpraiseworthy exit, as we have narrated.
If some of them are difficult at birth, and they reach the unnatural shapes we mentioned, then treat them as we have narrated: and let the midwife be skillful, and do what she does with ease and slowness, and devise according to every shape with what is prepared for her from ingenuity leading to health.
A recollection of what is formed in the womb from embryos, and miscarriages.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten are formed in the womb; and it has been verified with me that one woman expelled seven, and another fifteen, all formed, through miscarriage.
That which was done by the measurement of the glorious wise one.
This, therefore, everything ought to be in the knowledge of the Midwife. For perhaps it will come to her similar to that; therefore, let her oppose it with that which is appropriate.
Chapter LXXVI.
On the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you treat a dead fetus with what I have said concerning division from the medication, and it does not emerge, and you are compelled to use an operation with the hand, then you ought to consider.
If, therefore, the woman is of sound constitution, and is not suffering from any of the complications from which harm is feared for her: then it is necessary that the woman be laid on the bed, on her back, and her head inclined downwards, and her legs raised; and the attendant hold her by two sides, or she be bound to the bed, so that her body is not moved during the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the mouth of the womb with moist oils, with the mucilage of marshmallow, and fenugreek, and linseed.
Then let the midwife anoint her hand with these oils and mucilages; then let her introduce it into the womb with ease. Then let her seek with it the place where she can fix the hooks from the fetus, and the suitable places for it.
Therefore, consider then, if the fetus is prepared to descend with its head foremost: then fix the hooks in its eyes, or in its neck, or in its mouth, or in its palate, or under its chin, or in its fork, or in places near the ribs, and under the hypochondria.
If however the descent is with its feet foremost, then in its pubic bone and its middle ribs.
And it is necessary that she hold the hook with her right hand, and place its curve in that which is between the fingers of her left hand; and let her introduce it with her hand with ease, and fix the hook in any of those places that we have mentioned, until she reaches the empty space.
Then let her fix another hook, or a third, if necessary, in opposition to it, so that the contraction is equal, and does not lean to one side.
Then let it be extended with equal extension, and let the extension not only be straight, but rather move in every direction, so that its exit is eased, as is done with a tooth in its extraction.
And it is necessary that in that which is between them, the extension be softened. If a part of it is retained, then the midwife should anoint some fingers with oil, and introduce them from the side, so that she may revolve with them that which is retained.
If therefore a part of the fetus emerges, then the hooks should be moved to another higher place; do this until the whole fetus emerges.
If, therefore, a hand emerges before the others, and it is not possible to return it, because of its constriction, then it is necessary to wrap a cloth around it. Then pull it out until, when the whole emerges, cut it either at the ankle joint, or at the elbow: and thus it is necessary that you should do in the other hand, and in the two feet.
If, however, the fetus' head is large, or constriction occurs in its exit, or water is collected in its head, then it is necessary to introduce a spiny Spatumile between the fingers, and to split the head with it, and to extract the water; or to break it with the instrument called Almisdac: whose form comes with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter that is after this.
Similarly, let him do so if the fetus has a naturally large head, then it is necessary to sever the [fetus] with algengematu, or extract it with a misdach, as we have said. Then pull out the bones with forceps.
If the head emerges and is constricted at the fork, then it is to be split until the moisture in the chest is poured out.
If, however, it is not tangled nor yields to exit, then cut the head into pieces, according to what is possible for you.
If the lower belly has an abscess, or has dropsy in it, then it is necessary to pierce it until moisture runs from it.
If the fetus is above its feet, then its extraction is easy, and its direction to the mouth of the womb is slight.
If, however, it is constricted at the belly or chest, then it is necessary to extract it with a cloth wrapped around your hand; and split the belly or chest until what is within them flows out.
If however, the other limbs are removed, and the head returns, then let the midwife insert her left hand. If therefore the mouth of the womb is open, then let her insert her hand deep into the womb, and seek the head with it, and pull it with her fingers up to the mouth of the womb; then insert into it one hook, or two hooks, and pull it with it.
If however, the mouth of the womb is constricted, due to a hot abscess which occurs there, then it is not necessary to exert labour upon it, but it is necessary then to administer an effusion of moist, fatty things; and administer plasters; and sit in waters which soften and moisten.
If the foetus is on its side, then it is possible to straighten it; then administer what we have said concerning a living foetus.
If that is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces and extracted.
And it is necessary that you not leave anything of the afterbirth inside at all.
When therefore you have completed your operation, then administer what we have said concerning hot abscesses which occur in wombs.
If a flow of blood occurs, then it should be placed in styptic waters, and administer the rest of the treatment.
And I myself have already seen a woman in whose womb a dead fetus was; then she became pregnant again over it, afterwards another fetus died as well. Therefore, a long time later, an abscess occurred in her navel, and it became swollen until it opened, and began to discharge pus.
Therefore I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time, and the wound did not heal.
Therefore I placed upon it some plasters of strong attraction, and bone came out of the place. Then a few days passed, and another bone came out. Therefore I was surprised by this, since there is no bone in the belly.
Therefore I thought, because they were bones from a dead fetus.
Then I sought the wound, and extracted many bones, the woman being in better condition: and now she has lived thus for a long time, and sends out a little pus from the place.
And I did not establish this as extraneous here, except because there is knowledge and assistance in it for the treatment that a skilled physician performs with his hand.
Chapter LXXVII.
Concerning the shapes of necessary instruments in the extraction of a fetus.
The shape of the Vertex by which the matrix is opened.
Below here is the shape of the Strictorium, by which books are equalized, to which are equally two vertices at the two ends of two pieces of wood. However, this vertex should be finer than the strictorium, and to make it, it should be either from ebony or from boxwood.
And the width of each piece of wood is about two fingers: and the margin of each of the two is about one finger, and the length of each of them is a palm and a half: and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two additions of the same kind of wood, which are now fixed to both, whose length is half a palm and a little more: and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the matrix, so that it is opened with them when you turn the two Vertices.
Another form similar to that, finer and lighter.
Let it be made of ebony wood, or boxwood, according to the likeness of forceps. However, at its extremity let there be two additions, as you see here above: and the length of each addition of them is like a palm; and the breadth of each is two fingers.
When therefore you wish to open the womb with it, then cause the woman to sit on a bed, with her feet hanging down, opening what is between her thighs. Then introduce these two joined additions into the womb, and you hold the lower end of the instrument between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument, as you do with shears, equally according to the amount you wish to open the mouth of the womb, until the midwife does what she wants.
Another form of Vertigo, which the ancients called the Impelling Form, by which the fetus is impelled.
The Almisdac form, by which the large head is crushed; and it is likened to a pair of tongs, which have teeth at the end, as you see.
And sometimes it is elongated, like tongs of this form, as you see, with teeth like saw teeth, with which it is cut and crushed.
The form of a hook having one spine.
The form of a hook having two spines.
The form of a scalpel with two ends for severing the head.
The form of two also broad scalpels.
All these instruments are used, and the more their types are multiplied, and prepared by the craftsman, the swifter he is at his operation, and the higher in esteem among men, due to their quantity.
Therefore, do not despise any instrument from among them, unless it is prepared for you. For their necessity is unexcusable.
Chapter LXXVIII.
Concerning the extraction of the Secundine.
When the Secundine is retained at childbirth, then it is necessary that you instruct the woman to hold her breath. Then make her sneeze with remedies, and press your hand upon her mouth and nostrils. If therefore it emerges by this means, it is good.
If not, then take a pot, and pierce a hole in the lid; and place in it herbs that open the mother's womb, such as calamint, rue, dill, camomile, sea-salt, cinnamon wood, and centaury: take all these herbs, or some of them, and immerse them in water, and place them on the fire.
Then place a reed-tube over the hole of the pot's lid, and the other end in the woman's womb: and let her hold it until the vapor reaches the cavity of the womb.
Then make her sneeze, as we have said. For the afterbirth will emerge with ease.
If however it remains after this treatment, and does not emerge, then instruct the midwife to immerse her hand in sesame oil, or marsh-mallow mucilage. Then let her insert it into the vagina, and seek out the afterbirth with it. When therefore she finds it, then let her take it and gently pull it out.
If however it is attached deep within the uterus, then let her insert her hand, as we have described, until when she finds the afterbirth, and pull it out gently along the correct path, so that the uterus does not fall with violent pulling; rather, it should be moved easily to the right and left sides, then the pulling force should be increased. For it then yields, and is preserved, and escapes from adhesion.
If the opening of the uterus is constricted, as we have already described, treat it with sneezing, and with a medicinal decoction of herbs.
If, however, it does not emerge with all that we have described, then beware of tiring yourself out repeatedly trying to pull it out.
But it is necessary that whatever emerges from it to the opening of the uterus be tied to the woman's hip. Then administer an enema with the tetrapharmacon ointment. For this will putrefy it after days, and it will dissolve, and emerge.
However, when it putrefies, a foul odor rises from it to the stomach and head, and harms the weak one. Therefore, suitable fumigations should be administered to it.
Some of the ancients, however, have already experienced the fumigation of nasturtium and dried figs.
The shape of the instrument with which a woman is fumigated in cases of retained Secundinae and menses.
Let it be made of glass, similar to an alembic, or of bronze; and let the wide end be placed over the fire; and let the fumigation be placed over the prunings; let it hold it, until that fumigation is consumed; and again another.
Chapter LXXIX.
Concerning the treatment of a non-perforated anus.
Many children are born with their anuses not perforated, which have already been obstructed by a fine sifac. Therefore, the midwife should perforate that sifac with her finger; and if not, let her perforate it with a sharp spatula; and let her beware of touching the fleshy part.
Then place upon it wool soaked in wine and oil. Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
If however you fear that it will be obstructed, then place a lead cannula in the opening for many days, and let it be removed when the child wishes to defecate.
And obstruction of the anus can also occur from the consolidation of a wound or an abscess. Therefore, that consolidation must be cut, then treated with what we have said about treatment and lead.
Chapter LXXX.
Concerning the treatment of fistulas that occur in the lower part.
Fistulas occurring in the lower part are swellings and thicknesses that occur near the anus externally, or in the space of one of the parts, and are either one fistula or several.
When that swelling has aged, it opens, and from it flows watery white moisture, or thin pus.
And sometimes these fistulas are penetrating. Therefore, penetrating ones are recognized by what emerges during defecation and flatulence when the patient uses defecation; and perhaps worms emerge from them.
And some of these fistulas, when penetrating, are in the space reaching the bladder and reaching the urethra. And some of them penetrate as far as the hip joint and as far as the tailbone.
By this, a penetrating fistula to the anus is recognized, even from a non-penetrating one, that you insert your index finger into the anus and introduce a thin probe into the fistula, made of air or iron, when there is no twisting.
If there is twisting in it, then introduce a thin lead probe into it, or a hair from a horse's mane, until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
If you do not feel it completely, nor does anything pass from the opening in terms of excretion, nor wind, nor worms as I said: then know that it is not penetrating: therefore hurry to the cure.
And when the fistula penetrates to the bladder or to the urinary meatus: then its sign is the discharge of urine from it, and its prevention from this in order that the place may consolidate with medicines.
If however it penetrates to the hip joint or to the hip bone: then its sign is that the probe reaches there, if there is no twisting to the bone: and the finding of pain around the part, and that pus issues from it always: and that the treatment does not work in it, nor does it consolidate with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas there is no cure at all: and their cure is labour and vanity for him, from foolish physicians, upon whom it is formed.
However, in that which is not penetrating and not ancient, healing is hoped for with the saw: according to what I tell you, and I have already proven: and it is that the sick person should lie between your hands on their back: and raise their legs up above their hips, and their hips should be inclined towards their stomach. Then introduce a lead or copper probe, if there is twisting in the fistula, until you know where the probe reaches.
If however the patient feels it around the anus, then it is necessary that you introduce your index finger into the anus.
If therefore you feel the probe already having penetrated itself, detected, besides that you feel between your finger, and between it, some sinew or flesh: then you know for certain that it is penetrating: therefore do not labor on it, for there is no healing of it, as we said.
However, they say that in some men the flesh is healed, and that through medication by which help is hoped for, it is that you cauterize a fine cautery according to the width of the fistula, as preceded, and insert it ignited into the fistula, until it reaches near the anus. Then repeat it twice, or three times, until you know that all those added filtered fleshy parts, which are similar to the quill of a bird's feather, are now burned.
Then treat it with lint wrapped in butter, until those burned flesh parts come out; then treat it with ointments that generate flesh and consolidate it.
If therefore it is healed, it is good.
And if not, then it will never be healed with any other treatment.
And if a protrusion emerges, and it does not reach your finger in the anus, and there is a thick layer of flesh between it and the protrusion, or if such a thing is there: and you see a fistula in that which follows the surface of the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you go on cutting with the protrusion that is in the fistula, until the cut reaches where the protrusion's tip reaches, and the protrusion is released and falls.
Then purify those filtered flesh parts, which are similar to the quill of a bird, so that nothing remains from them.
If, however, blood overcomes you, and separates you from your operation in that you cut those tissues, then it is better, and of more rapid benefit, to cauterize it with fire or with a sharp medicine.
Because cautery with fire brings about good conditions, by burning those added tissues, and cutting off the blood, and drying the moisture.
Then treat it with linens wrapped in butter, or crushed herbs, until the area discharges pus, and those burned tissues are expelled in the pus.
Then treat the wound with consolidating ointments, which generate firm flesh, and there are medicines containing astringency and drying.
For when it is restored, then it is already healed, and you need not fear repetition.
If however the fistula has already reached the depth of the anus, and is prolonged by bodily excess, then insert your finger into the anus and investigate with it.
If therefore you feel a barrier, and between you and it is a membrane of sifac, or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then there is no operation on it, unless according to the path of trust and hope.
This is because it is treated in one of three ways: either to cauterize it, as we said; or to cut it back until you reach its concavity, so that it is possible for you to insert lint, and treatment from nearby. And do not proceed very far with the cutting, so that you do not cut the muscle containing the anus, and cause an unwanted expulsion from the weak bowel. Then treat it with what we said.
Perhaps it will be healed; or you will remove the membrane, and clean it after you have cut it back near the anus, as we said, either with a probe, or with another instrument with a sharp end. Then cleanse those filtered tissues that are in the incision, until you can see clearly with the probe.
Then attempt to restore the entire wound with an incision, and an opening above where it is consolidated; and a fistula will remain open in the inner part of the anus, and it will be easier on the patient.
And sometimes the fistula is sewn in this way: and it is when you insert a probe into the fistula; and it is on the side of the anus near the surface of the body with the skin and margin of the anus; then take a probe with a perforated end, which is like an aleshkef needle, that is, for preparing leather, according to this form. And insert into it a thread wrapped with five threads, and about that. Then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula, until it reaches its concavity.
If therefore it is penetrating into the inner margin of the anus nearby, then pull the thread out of that opening; so that you insert your finger into the anus, and pull out the end of the thread, and bring the two ends together; and tie each, and leave it for one, or two days; and the more the thread penetrates the flesh, the more tightly tie it, until the flesh between the two ends of the thread is cut, and falls away; then treat the wound until it is consolidated and healed.
If however the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate in whatever way is prepared for you, unless it is very deep. For it is not in your power to do that because of the arm, so that you do not injure it.
Then do with it as we said, until it is healed.
The shape of the thorny spatula with which the fistula is split, whose curve is very sharp, and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not injure what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter LXXXI.
Concerning the suturing of hemorrhoids, from which blood flows from their incision; and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two kinds. For they are either within the anus, like vesicles of charcoal or prunes; or like bunches of grapes themselves. And from them are small and large ones; and blood flows from them always, and there is one, and there are many. And there are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus, are mostly few in moisture, from which flows yellow water, or little blood with persistent flow; and they are according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is within the anus is, that you command the sick person to strain: and it is to be pressed out, until it emerges from the anus, and the athelil appear to you. Therefore also suspend them with hooks, or hold them with your fingernail. Then cut them at their roots.
However, if the hooks are not retained in them, due to their moisture and their softening: then take them with a rough cloth, or with a cloth made of double-layered wool, and extract them with your fingers. Then cut them, and after the incision, powder some hot powders over them, so that they stand in place of a cautery. Or cauterize them according to what has preceded in the chapter on cauterization. Then treat them with the rest of the treatment, until they are healed.
If the anus does not yield to evacuation, then administer an enema to the infirm person containing a small amount of irritant, so that it is washed out with that which is in the anus, and it yields to evacuation quickly, when the infirm person expels it.
If, however, the piles are external from the anus, then their removal is light, easy: and it is that you seize them with your fingernail, or seize them with a hook, and cut them. Then treat them with what we have said, until they are healed.
And whoever abhors incision with iron, then it is necessary that you administer their suture according to this method. Take a wound thread, and insert it into the needle. Then pull the piles upward, and make it pierce with the needle into its root from the other side: and wrap the two ends of the thread, at the lower part of the needle, and let them be lengthwise: and tighten the piles with a strong ligature. Then tie the thread, and remove the needle.
Do that with all the piles, and leave one of them: nor its own, so that excess blood may run from it.
Then place over the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil, or cedar oil, and place it over the anus: and order the sick person to rest. Then leave it, until it falls.
When therefore the piles fall, treat them with ointments and other remedies until they are healed.
Concerning the treatment of Fissures. For they often occur from the drying up of feces and the constriction of nature.
When therefore they become old and medicine does not work on them, then it is necessary to scrape them with a pointed spatula or with your fingernail until they become moist, and to remove from them the outer layer which prevents them from consolidating. Then treat them until they consolidate as they should.
If however they do not consolidate, then scrape them again with a more forceful instrument than the first, until they bleed profusely and are abraded; then treat them until they heal.
Concerning the treatment of inverted and non-inverted Almirmar, and of dry Athelil and Formicae.
Chapter LXXXII.
Know that Almirmar is nothing other than a round swelling the color of the body, similar to the head of a nail; and it occurs throughout the body, particularly in the lower parts of the feet and toes; and pain arises from it when walking.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut what is around the head of the nail, and hold it with scissors, or with a hook, and uproot it from its root.
Then treat it; and if you wish, cauterize it according to what preceded in its chapter.
From dry athelil, one is a small, rough, filtered, round eminence above the surface of the body. And they often occur on the hands of children.
And its treatment is that you extend the athelil and cut it; or tie it with a silk thread; or with a hair, until it falls. And if you wish, cauterize it according to what was said before with fire or with a sharp medicine; and now inverted athelil are eradicated, and not inverted with the instrument that I will name.
A formica, however, is also a small, filtered, thick eminence on the surface of the body, going deep very much; and when it encounters cold, a sensation similar to an ant bite is felt in it, and it spreads throughout the body; and most of it occurs on the hands.
And its treatment is to take a cannula from a goose's feather or a strong vulture's feather; and place that cannula upon the Formica, until the cannula's circle encompasses its parts. Then turn your hand with the cannula until you separate what is around the Formica; and place it deep in the flesh, and eradicate the Formica from its root.
And if you wish, do it with a cannula of air or iron, according to this form.
Let the cannula's top be raised to a height pulling the deaf wrapped one, so that its grip and its turning around the finger are alleviated.
And if you wish, cauterize the Formica according to what was stated previously in the cauterization of Athelil in the chapter on the cautery.
Chapter LXXXIII.
Concerning the forms of instruments which are administered for clysters in diseases of the anus, and for emptying the bowels, and for colic.
Sometimes a clyster is made from silver, or from bronze, or from cast copper, or from hammered copper.
And sometimes it is made from these instruments small, and large, according to those in which they are administered.
Therefore, that which is administered in the treatment of small children, should be small: and those whose anuses are weak, or painful, so too should their clysters be.
Large clyster.
Its tube, that is, the upper head to which the bag is tied, should be large in this way; and there should be a distinction so that the bag is tied as you see, and its lower end which is inserted into the anus, should be blunt, that is, not open, smooth, very much, drawing to thinness; and on one side of it there are two holes, and on the other side one hole.
And the size of the hole should be according to the thickness of the rays, and a little thicker; and the bag in which the medicines are placed should be from the bladder of an animal, or from the egg-shell, which should be in the form of a small asofrat; whose root should be one palm.
And it should be pierced with many holes, as it is rolled, and the space between each hole should be the thickness of a finger. Then insert strong thread, made of ten threads or thereabouts, through those holes, and tie the bag to it, as if it were an asofrat. When the bag is therefore placed in it, it should be tightened at the head of the enema between the two distinguishing constrictions firmly. Then administer the enema with it.
And this is the form of the bags, depicted here, and the holes.
However, the quality of administering the enema with it is: to fill the bag, or bladder, with the medicine, which should be heated according to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is not too hot, or too cold.
Then the patient shall lie on their back, and raise their legs upwards; and place underneath them a *inthaan*, that is, a hide on which people lie, or a linen cloth.
Then anoint the end of the clyster with oil, or with egg white, or with the mucilage of fenugreek, or similar substances. Then introduce it into the anus with ease. Then let the attendant grasp the bag with both hands simultaneously, and squeeze it forcefully until all that is in the clyster is evacuated into the intestine.
Then remove the clyster, and let the patient raise their legs towards the wall.
If the medicine compels him to the outcome, then let him hold it as long as possible. And if he sleeps with it all night, it is more beneficial.
If, however, you administer the clyster in illnesses of looseness of the belly, and its exit is hurried: then repeat the clyster, until he is cured.
If there are hemorrhoids or damaging abscesses in the anus, then let the clyster be very fine, gentle, from the clysters with which children are treated.
And this should be the form of a fine clyster, above which there is tight skin.
Chapter Eighty-four.
I have already said in the division concerning the treatment of wounds that it was appropriate for this book, and I will now describe their treatment with the hand, and with medicines, in accordance with their entirety.
Therefore, I say that wounds are diversified according to the thing by which they are made, and according to the place upon which the wound falls.
Therefore, the things by which wounds are often made are like the throwing of a stone, or the cutting of a sword or knife, or a blow with a spear, or wood, or an arrow, and similar things from many objects.
And the wounds that are made according to the places of the body are, such as a wound that falls upon the head, or neck, or breast, or belly, or liver; and similar ones from those members.
And I am recounting the treatments of certain wounds, so that I may establish that reasoning and rule concerning the remaining wounds.
And I will begin with simple wounds of the head, because the treatment of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
I say therefore. When a simple wound occurs on the head, and there is no fracture of the bone: then consider, if it is from the throwing of a stone, or similar things, and it has only broken the skin; and it is a large wound, and you fear a bad outcome from a hot abscess; then hurry, and immediately perform a cephalic phlebotomy; and do not delay it; and draw from its blood according to the measure of its power; if nothing prevents it from the phlebotomy, especially if the blood flowing from the wound is small in quantity. And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded area.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded area. And let the patient beware of repletion, and wine, and heavy foods.
If, however, something prevents the lessening, then lessen from food, and let him not approach meats, nor wines.
And if a hot abscess occurs on the wound, apply a poultice infused in rose oil alone, or with wine that contains astringency.
If, however, you are certain of no hot abscess, then place on the wound, if it is recent with its own blood, and the air has not changed it, the powder which I will describe shortly; and if the air has already changed it, then apply certain ointments to it until it is healed.
If, however, the wound is large, or is from a sword cut, and the like; and its edges do not come together by means of pledgets, then bring them together with sutures, according to what I will describe in wounds of the belly concerning their sutures.
If, however, the wound is already exposed by the bone, and hangs, and its retention is only in a small suspender: then cut it. Then treat the wound with medicines, and restore the hard flesh in place of the skin, and order the sick person to be nourished with foods that are substantial, such as heads of grain, and cooked wheat, until it is like a pulp; and things similar to these.
If you find putrefaction in the skin, and it does not adhere to the bone, then cut away all the putrefied part, and cast it off; then treat it.
If, however, in these wounds there is an artery, or a vein from which blood flows; and it is not stopped by medicines, then inquire about the artery. If therefore you find it not cut, then cut it with a Spatumili, or tie it. If necessity compels it when what we have said is not helpful, then cauterize it until the blood is stopped.
If, however, these wounds are small, simple, then their treatment is easy, slight; so that you pulverize this powder upon them, because it consolidates them before air changes them.
Method of the flesh-healing powder. Take one part of frankincense, and two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of quicklime; let the whole be finely ground and sifted; and fill the wound with it, and bind it with a good bandage, until it adheres well to it and coagulates with the blood.
Then bind it over with bandages and leave it, and do not loosen it while it remains, so that it may not fester. For the medicine is not removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes lime alone does this, when frankincense and dragon's blood are not available.  Even dragon's blood does the same in many large wounds, when it is appropriate to put it on them and bind them, and are fresh wounds with their own blood, which air has not altered.
If, however, air has already altered them with some alteration, especially when it is in summer, then this powder is not intended for this position. Therefore, treat it with what we have said, that you place some digestive ointments on them; or place poultices of barley flour made with water and honey on them, until they emit pus. Then treat them with the rest of the treatment until they are healed.
However, if a fracture in a bone occurs with the wound, and it is small, then extract it with force: and I will now describe the treatment of skull fractures in what is to follow.
Of wounds of the neck.
If, however, a wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference between it in treatment, and a simple head wound.
If, however, a nerve of the neck, or artery, has already been cut: then if it is a nerve, there is no skill in it, except to avoid its treatment with that which produces violent astringency, such as ziniar, and zegi, and similar things. Because they harm the nerves, and contract them.
And do not place anything cold on the wound at all: because the substance of the nerves is cold, and its continuation is with the brain, which is higher than the limbs: and let that by which it is treated be from mild medicines, such as lime washed with sweet water sometimes infused with oil, or with rose oil, and tutty, when washed also with sweet water: and the rest of the mineral stones, according to this manner, and moist ointments.
If, however, the wound is large, then administer a suture: or constrict its lips with powders. Then treat it until it is healed.
If however the wound has depth, or a sinus occurs in its lower part, in which pus already collects, then pierce it in the more depressed place in it.
If, however, an artery has been incised in the wound, and its blood flow has followed: then separate, or cut it, and tie it: or cauterize it, if the need provokes that.
If, however, the cartilages of the throat, or the whole throat, have been injured, and the surrounding tissues have been saved, then bring together the skin's edges with a suture over the throat's cartilage, and do not touch the throat itself, but rather level it and restore it to its natural shape. Then bind it with a suitable bandage, and leave it for a few days. Then treat it with ointments which contain astringency and drying, until it is healed.
If, however, the wound has already made an impression on the bone, then investigate it. Then, if there are bone fragments within it, remove them all, or as many as you can; and leave that which cannot be removed until the wound has festered, and its exit is separated and relieved.
If the wound is recent, then powder over it the powder which I have described. If, however, bone is seen in the wound, and it becomes a fistula, then treat it with what is recalled in its chapter.
Concerning a wound in the shoulder blades.
If there is a blow from a lance or a knife, and you see its depth, then consider. If, therefore, wind emerges from the wound, when the sick person gasps from it, then know that the wound is mortal.
If there is no depth to it, and it is fresh with its own blood: then do not place powder on it from the first hour, nor bind it, so that the blood is not retained in its depth, and returns to the heart, and kills the sick person: but place attracting ointment on it, and loosen it twice a day.
If, however, ointment is not present for you, then place old spittle in the wound's opening, so that it sucks that which emerges from it of humidities: and make the sick person sleep upon the wound, so that what is gathered in it runs out.
If, however, three days or more have already passed for the wound, and no spasm occurs to the sick person, nor bad trembling of the heart, nor constriction in the breathing, and you see the rest of his conditions good: then know that the wound is sound. Therefore treat him with bandages and other treatment until he is healed.
If, however, his healing is excused, and it always opens up, then know that it has now become a fistula. Therefore treat it according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from a sword or knife incision, and is simple on the surface of the chest or back, then treat it with what has preceded regarding sutures, if it is large, or with powder if it is small.
If, however, it has already made an impression on the bone, and has chipped off fragments: then investigate the wound, and hasten to extract those fragments, if they are separate. If, however, they are not separate, then leave them until the wound becomes putrid, for the extraction of them will be eased.
However, the treatment of other accidental wounds in the remaining limbs, the judgment is that which we have said.
Concerning wounds of the belly, and the exit of the intestines, and their expulsion.
Rupture which occurs in the belly is great, and medium, and small.
From the great one, therefore, sometimes the intestine, or a number of intestines, emerges.
Therefore, their introduction, and their suture is more vehement and more difficult.
And a small rupture also becomes difficult in another way. It follows, therefore, that their reduction is more difficult if you do not hasten their introduction to their place immediately: because it becomes inflated, opened, and enlarged; therefore, its entry becomes difficult.
Therefore, a middle rupture is better because when it occurs, the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is in these two types.
Know that the suture of the belly is according to four ways.
Two common sutures agree with the suture of the belly, and with the suture of the other wounds of the body; and two other sutures properly agree with the other wounds. However, they are more proper with the suture of the belly; and I tell you each one with exposition and declaration.
Therefore, I say that when the wound is small, and something from the intestine emerges from it, and it becomes difficult to reduce it, then that is due to one of two ways. Either due to the smallness of the rupture, as we said; or because there is inflation in the intestine due to the coldness of the air.
Therefore, when this is so, then it is necessary to warm it, so that you immerse a sponge, or a damp cloth in lukewarm water alone. Or boil it in water with squinancy, and cyperus, and spike, and embrocate the intestine with it, until the inflation is resolved.
Sometimes wine in which there is astringency does it; and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflation.
When therefore you see the inflation already resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which marshmallow or mallow has been boiled. For by it the entrance of it is eased with a small thing.
If, however, its return is excused after this treatment, then make a small incision in the disruption, with the instrument with which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first treatment is better when prepared, than cutting; and no compulsion is made to cut after necessity and instrument failure.
Let one part of it be twisted, sharp, and the other part not sharp, and let the subtle extremity not be with the subtlety of Spatumilis, but rather somewhat blunter: and that is which is likened to a caiada, as you see.
When therefore the wound is enlarged, and the intestine enters, then it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural form to its own proper place, if possible upon it: because it is better.
When, however, the disruption is wide in the lower part of the belly: then it is necessary that you make the sick person lie upon his back, and raise his legs higher than his head. And if it is in the upper part of the belly, then place his head and chest higher than his lower parts.
And similarly if the rupture is in one of the two parts of the belly, then place your intention and your consideration always in this, that the part in which the wound is be higher than the other part.
And thus it is necessary that you administer it in large wounds, and in medium wounds.
In small wounds, however, place it according to what is prepared for you.
Then make it so that a pious servant is present between your hands, who holds the entire rupture with his hand: and bring together its lips. Then uncover from it for the one who follows the suture something after something: and this is the common mode of a single suture.
And it is that you should take a needle, or a number of needles according to the amount of the wound's breadth. Then, leave a quantity of the ring finger, that is, the doctor's finger, from the extremity of the rupture, and fix one needle, through which you insert a thread in two edges of the skin, with two edges thus under the skin from the inside, until you make it penetrate on the other side, and you have already brought together two edges of the skin, and two edges thus; and four rows are made.
Then tighten with the doubled thread in the circumference of the needle several times from both sides simultaneously, until the two lips of the wound are joined by a proper joining.
Then leave the thickness of a finger also, and fix another needle in them; afterward, thread it with the thread, as you did with the first needle. Therefore, do not cease to do that with the needles you need, until you complete the constriction of the entire wound.
And let the needles be medium in thickness and thinness; because very thin needles pierce the flesh very quickly; and thick ones are difficult to enter the skin; on account of that it is necessary that they be medium in thinness and thickness.
And it is not necessary that the needle be fixed in the two close edges of the skin too much, so that the flesh is not pierced quickly, and the wound is opened before it is consolidated, and they should not be extended too far with the suture, so that the wound is not hindered from consolidating.
Then cut the ends of the needles, so that they do not injure the patient in their sleep; and place cushions of cloth on every side, which hold the ends of the needles; and leave them until you know that the wound is now consolidated.
And this type of suture with needles is indeed more suitable in small wounds. Because a single needle, or two, or approximately that many, are enough in their suture.
However, the second common suture is to bring together with the suture four edges: two edges of skin, and two edges of the wound in a single turn, with a needle on which a thread is wound equal in fineness and thickness.
Then, when you have pierced these four edges with the needle, withdraw the needle from the side from which you began, so that the thread is drawn back from the upper part of the wound, so that the suture is like the suture of cloth by which cloths are bound together, and place between each suture an amount of thickness smaller than a little finger.
However, men know this suture.
And with this suture I healed a wound that had occurred to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the wound was a rupture greater than one palm: and about two palms of the middle intestine had emerged from the intestines, and there was a rupture in the middle of the belly.
Therefore, I returned it after his intestine had remained outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
Therefore, the wound was consolidated in a space of approximately fifteen days; and I treated it, and he was healed; and he lived many years after that, engaged in his affairs.
And the physicians had judged that it would not be healed.
And it was a miracle that I did not treat it with ointment. Because I was in a place where no ointment, nor any medicine, was to be found. Therefore, I placed aged linen over the wound twice a day, and I diligently washed it with honey water, until it was healed.
I shall speak of two proper species of sutures, according to the narrative of the discourse.
And it is, that you begin with a pointed suture: and you insert the needle from the outer to the inner part.
When therefore the needle penetrates the skin and the muscle running along the length and breadth of the whole body, leave the end of the thread on the side where you inserted the needle, and make it penetrate to the other end from the inner part to the outer in the other end of the wound.
When you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate in this same end of the wound from the outer part to the inner: and leave the end of the thread, which is on this side, and make the needle penetrate the other end from the inner part to the outer: and make it penetrate with your penetration, as you make it penetrate in the thread end of the wound, which is on its part, until it penetrates the whole thing.
Then begin also from this same side, and sew it with the end that is from the thread on the other side, and pull the needle out of the skin which it penetrated.
Then return it to that skin, and sew the thread end, which is on the other side, with this end of the wound: and pull it out of the skin that is on its side, and do it one turn and another: and do it turn by turn, until you have sewn the whole thing outwardly according to that example.
And the perfection of this suture is that it should sew the skin like the way leatherworkers sew skins, so that you thread the thread one time from one end of the skin, and that you leave it another time on the other side, until you complete it.
The second kind of proper suture, which Galen said.
His speech tells us to sew it according to the way some physicians sew them, so that they gather every part to its appropriate counterpart by nature, and they apply the end of the thread to its other end, and the end of the wound to its other end. And that is done according to what I tell you.
You should place the needle at the nearest end of the wound, on the outside, and make it penetrate to the inside, in that part alone; and leave the two ends of the thread. Then, turn the needle and make it penetrate from the outside to the inside in both ends of the thread, that is, on both sides.
Then turn both, and make both penetrate from the inside to the outside, in the two other ends of the wound, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the common and easy suture, and is a suture whose recollection preceded it.
This is therefore also Galen's discourse.
And some said. The suture of the belly is rectified with two needles. That is, you should insert one thread in each of them; and begin with the insertion of the needle, from where it is at your hand, and make it penetrate to the other side, according to the suture of those who suture hides equally.
And know that when there is a rupture in the middle of the belly, then its suture is more difficult than the other places of the belly.
However, the judgment of the treatment of a wound is like the judgment of other wounds.
Because when you take hold of a fresh wound with its blood, before the air alters it; and you reduce the intestine and suture it, and adjust it; then place over it powdered flesh-forming material.
If the air has already altered the wound, then put on it some ointments that are present to you, until they cause pus; and the threads fall out, and the wound is consolidated, and mirach. Then treat it as the treatment of other wounds, until it is healed.
However, if the medicines are not present to you, then immediately, from the moment it begins to discharge pus, put old lint on it; and change it twice a day, as I taught you, until it is healed.
For you do not need other medication in many dispositions, when the wounds are simple.
If however you fear that the wound communicates with the principal members in the injury, then it is necessary that you submerge soft wool in oil of moderate warmth, or in rose oil; and place it around the places that are between the groin, and under the armpits.
If, however, he feels pain, or prickings, or putrefaction in his intestines (for this often happens), then administer an enema to him with astringent, black, lukewarm wine; especially if putridity has already reached the intestine, and a penetrating exit has been made to its cavity.
And know that that which is from the large intestine is of easier healing; and that which is from the small one is of more difficult healing.
However, the intestine called the Jejunum does not receive healing from a wound that occurs in it at all; and that is because of its multitude of veins, and the size of them, and because of the delicacy of its body, and the proximity of its nervous nature.
If, however, that which proceeds from the wound is a zirbum, and you obtain it fresh, return it according to your reduction, by which you reduce the intestines equally.
If, however, a period of time has passed, and it has already become green or black, then it is necessary to tie it with thread above the spot which has become darkened by it, so that a flow of blood does not occur. For in a zirbum are veins and arteries.
Then cut that which is beyond that ligature, and place the two ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside it, so that the traction of it, and the extraction of it in the case of a zirbum, and the emission of pus from the wound, are alleviated.
A recollection of the wound that happened in the Intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the Intestine, and it is small, then it is possible that it might be restored in some people.  Therefore, because I saw one who was wounded in his belly with a spear thrust, and there was a wound on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old. And it became a fistula, and excrement and flatulence came out of it.
Therefore, I applied treatment, until I was not confident in his healing; nor did I cease to refine it, until he was healed, and the place was consolidated.
After I saw the place already consolidated, I feared for the sick man, that an adverse event might occur in his belly; and no adverse event happened to him from that. And he remained in better dispositions, eating and drinking, and copulating, and entered the bath, and exercised in his activities.
