And now certain experimenters have said that when a wound occurs in the Intestine, and it is small, then it should be tied in this manner. And it is that you should take ants of large heads. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, and place one ant from those that have open mouths upon the two lips of the wound. When therefore it grasps them, and tightens its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres, and does not come loose.
Then place another ant near the first, and do not cease to do this with ant after ant according to the amount of the whole wound.
Then reduce it, and sew the wound. For those heads remain attached to the intestine until the intestine is nourished and healed, and no harm occurs to the sick person at all.
And now it is possible that the intestine be tied also with a fine thread: which has been scraped from the intestine of an animal, attached to it after it is inserted into the needle.
And it is so that you take the end of this thread from the intestine, and it is scraped a lot. Then tie to the end of it a fine linen thread wrapped around. Then insert that thread into the needle and sew the intestine with it. Then reduce it into the disruption.
And this method of sewing with ants and intestines is only according to the path of trust and hope.
Know that a wound that is large, wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines: then there is no remedy except subtlety, nor is its healing at all.
On the treatment of Zuchemen, and Fistulas.
Know that every wound, or abscess, when it grows old and becomes a ulcer, and does not heal, and constantly discharges pus, and is not separated: then it is universally called, wherever it may be in the limb, a Fistula, and we call it Zuchemen.
And a Fistula, in truth, is a filtered, hard, white knot: with which there is no pain, which has the concavity of a bird's feather: and because of that some call it Risati, that is, a feather.
And in some cases it is moist, which emits pus. And perhaps the moisture is separated in some cases. And sometimes this moisture is plentiful, and sometimes scarce; and is thick, and sometimes thin.
And I have already mentioned in the division, when I said wounds, that every ulcer which does not heal, nor flesh grows in it, is not so except for one of nine causes.
Of which one is, either because of the scarcity of blood in the body; or because of its bad quality; or because there is hard flesh between it and its edges, hindering the growth of flesh; or because of a lot of dirt and filth; or because the ulcer itself is putrid, and the material applied to it is of bad quality; or because the medicine is unsuitable for its treatment; or because the decay falling on the region is a kind of pestilence, or through the properties in the region, as happens in Caesaraugusta, in which the digestion of illnesses is difficult, and the natural healing of abscesses is delayed there; or because there is one bone, or a number of bones, in it.
And I have already shown all these causes and their signs, and their treatments with medicines in the division: and it is necessary here that I make you know the treatment of Fistula and Zuken, which is done by the surgical method with the hand.
Know therefore, that Fistula occurs in all members of the body. For among them are fistulas that reach large veins and arteries, or nerves, or sinews, or the intestines, or the bladder, or to one of the ribs, or to one of the vertebrae of the back, or to any of the joints formed by compounding, like the hand and foot, and among them are those that run near the main limb and similar ones.
Therefore, whichever of these fistulas is of this sort, is from illnesses of difficult healing, and does not accept treatment, and dealing with it is laborious and ignorant.
If there is any fistula among them whose depth does not reach these places which I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its treatment, and wish to attain knowledge of it, then take a probe of bronze or iron, if the fistula extends in a straight line, and explore it with it.
But if there is a twisting in the fistula, then investigate it with a thin lead probe. Because lead, with the ease of its body, is alleviated and bent around the twisting.
If the fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to mark it with a probe. Therefore, irrigate one of its openings from among its openings.
For the moisture you irrigate it with goes towards the other openings and flows out from them.
Then inquire carefully in what way it is possible for you to know whether there is a bone, or a nerve, and the fistula's cavity is distant or close; or whether it is a single fistula having many openings, and stand over all of it with all your strength; with your questioning, by which you question the sick person, and the finding of pain when pressed with your hand upon the place, and similar signs and causes of accidents of an abscess.
When you have stood over all of that in the correct manner, then proceed to treatment according to your confidence; and consider whether the fistula is a nearby apparent one, or in a safe place distant from a joint, or a nerve, or an artery, or a vein, or one of the places which I have told you: then cut the fistula as previously described in my narrative, and remove what is in it by filtration, and any corrupted flesh which arises within its opening from flesh, or pustules, and similar things; and treat it until it is healed.
If, however, the fistula is of distant and deep extent, and it is in line, then it is necessary to cut it as deep as possible. Then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh.
Then administer strips wrapped in sharp medicines, and insert them into the cavity of the fistula which you have grasped with the iron tool; do this repeatedly until the sharp medicine corrodes everything remaining in the fistula's cavity from the corruption.
Then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh, until it is healed.
If, however, it is not healed by that, then cauterize it according to what we have narrated.
If, therefore, the cause of the fistula is bone, and this is ascertained by you: then investigate it, and cut it according to what I have told you: if nothing from a vein, or nerve, or principal limb prevents you, as we have said.
If, therefore, bone is found by you, and there is something of corruption and blackness in it: then scrape it, until its entire corruption is removed. Then treat it with that which makes it flesh, until it is healed.
If, however, it is not healed, and discharges pus as it did before: then know, that you have not reached the complete eradication of the corruption.
Therefore, expose it again, and meticulously perform its scraping and cleansing with all your strength.
Then restore it. If therefore it heals, it is good; and if not, then know that that corruption is in a deep and distant part of the body, which you have not reached. Therefore, you have no skill in it, except to leave it to nature.
If a small bone is corrupted, and it is possible for you, extract it with fine forceps suitable for it.
If, however, there are many bones, then carefully perform the extraction of all of them, and do not leave any of them behind with your strength.
If, however, some moisture creates resistance for you, and there is no fear or error in cutting them, then cut away those moistures and flesh, if there are any there, and free the bones.
If, however, you lack the preparation to remove the bones from your time, then apply something to the wound to cause it to fester, and leave it until that which is around those bones, from the flesh and moistures, is rotten.
And guard the wound so that it does not heal. And bind the incision that you made in your operation. Indeed, place over it, if you fear it, a poultice soaked in crushed sulfur with oil; or a poultice soaked in Egyptian ointment, or any green ointments.
For when those moistures, namely, fester, and the bones are separated from them, the removal and extraction of them is eased.
If however, there is one large bone, such as the bone of the leg or the hip bone, and similar to them, and that which is corrupted from it is a large part: then scrape it with the utmost scraping until that blackness and corruption is removed. Then restore the wound.
If however, that which is corrupted within it is a large part, and corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw it and cut it entirely to the place where the corruption has reached: then treat it until it is healed.
And I tell you a story, which happened to a man in his leg: that you might set it as an example and aid in your operation.
There was a young man of approximately thirty years, in whom pain occurred in his leg, from a cause which originated within the body, that is, from the internal organs, until the matter reached the leg, and a large abscess was formed: and there was no external cause for it.
Therefore, time passed with him with the error of physicians, until the abscess was opened, and much matter flowed out. And his treatment was delayed until the leg became fistulous, and many openings were formed in it: all of which emitted pus and bodily humors.
Therefore, a multitude of physicians treated him for approximately two years, and there was no wise person in the surgical art among them, until I was called, and saw his leg, and the matter which flowed from those openings with great force: indeed, the whole body of the man had now become putrid, and his complexion was yellowish.
I therefore inserted a probe into one of the openings, and the probe reached the bone.
Then I examined all the openings: and found that they reached each other from all parts of the leg.
I therefore quickly cut over one of those openings, until I exposed a portion of the bone: and found it corrupted: which was already corroded, blackened, decayed, and perforated, until it had penetrated to the marrow.
I therefore scraped away what was exposed to me, and prepared it from the corrupted bone.
And I determined that there was nothing in the bone but that corruption, which I cut away and scraped. And I had already eradicated it.
Then I applied a restoration to the wound with consolidating medicines for a long time. And it was not consolidated.
Then I tried, and I discovered a second layer of bone above the first discovery; and I found continuous corruption with the bone; and I scraped what appeared to me to be also from that corruption.
Then I attempted its restoration. And it was not restored.
Therefore, I again uncovered it, and I did not cease to cut the bone piece by piece, and to attempt its restoration; and it was not restored, until I cut away about a palm's breadth from the bone and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines, and it was quickly consolidated and healed; and this repetition in its operation and section was not necessary, except for the weakness of the patient's disposition, and his little tolerance, and my fear for him of death; because he was experiencing syncopes and bad occurrences due to excessive evacuations at all hours.
Therefore, he was healed with complete healing: and hard flesh grew in the place of the bone, and a good disposition was established in his body, and his strength returned: and he resumed his usual dispositions; and he suffered no harm from walking at all.
However, if a projecting bone in a body part has already been broken, then it is necessary to saw it in this manner: and thus you should take a bandage, and tighten it at the end of the projecting bone; and instruct someone to pull it upward; and place another bandage of thicker wool over the first bandage. Then tie it over the flesh beneath the bone; and extend its end so that you pull the flesh downwards; and you expose the flesh over the place you wish to saw so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
Place wood or a plank beneath the bone below properly, because when you do that, it does not prevent the saw from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be over the corrupted place for a short while, so that there is no corruption in the concavity of the bone; and it does not appear in its appearance to the senses, and compels sawing again in its place.
If the bone is corrupted, and it is not projecting, but rather its part is continuous with another part, and the corruption is in its middle or in a part of it, then uncover the flesh from all sides completely. Then place wood underneath.
Afterwards saw it from the first part, where the corruption is, until the sawing is continued from the other part; and let the sawing be along the length from the corruption for a little while, as we have said.
If the corruption is in the joint, cut the corrupted joint itself, and saw the bone where it is continuous from the other side.
If however the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no remedy in it except abrasion.
If the corruption is in the comb of the hand, or in the comb of the foot, then the matter is very difficult in it. But it is necessary that you investigate how the corruption appears to you. And you should abrade it, and cleanse it according to whatever arrangement is possible for you, and with whatever remedy is suitable for you: when it does not cause you opposition from a vein or a nerve.
And know that there are many knives and saws for cutting these bones, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness and thinness, and their size and smallness, and their hardness and their rarity. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare a suitable instrument of every kind of operation for that operation.
And know that these operations show you the kind of instrument you need, when you have long study with you. And the knowledge of the methods of this art is a testament to the methods of illnesses: for then you will find for yourself what suits it from the instruments for every ailment.
And I am showing you in the final part of this chapter the number of instruments, which you will place as examples upon which you will cut, and the test by which you will try them upon others.
Shape of another saw.
Shape of the connections of the razor. Let the head of this razor, according to the form of the head of a star-shaped nail, and its points be according to the form of the points of a sharpened file.
And it is not suitable, unless the heads of the joints are rubbed with it when they are corrupted, or the bone is large and great.

Form of the broad razor.
Form of the razor in which there is a concavity.
Form of another slender razor.
Form of another razor with a curved end.
Form of a small, slender, tensed razor.
This is similar to a saw, and the end is like a saw; it is shown in many places by the reason of bones.
Form of a razor suitable for the cutting of that which is perforated from the bones.
Its end is triangular, with sharp ends.
It is made of Indian [material]. And similarly, all that we have said must be made from razors, and knives, and saws.
Concerning the incision of the extremities, and the sawing of the bones.
The extremities putrefy, either from an external cause, or an internal one.
Therefore, when you have treated that corruption with medicinal remedies, and the treatment does not help; and you see the corruption affecting the limb, which nothing else repels; then it is necessary to cut that limb until you reach the corruption, so that it escapes death or the greater harm which is the loss of the limb.
And a sign that this happens is that the limb darkens until it becomes foul; that fire has burned it, or that it putrefies after turning black; until that putrefaction is cut away to the part following the limb, and spreads to the whole body.
Therefore, hurry with its incision, and likewise if the cause of the corruption is from the sting of certain venomous creatures, such as the sea scorpion, and viper, and rattlesnake, and the like.
If, therefore, the corruption, or the injury, is in the extremity of a finger, then cut off the finger, and do not give space to the corruption to spread to the rest of the hand.
And likewise if it happens in the hand, then cut it off from the root of the forearm, and do not give space to the corruption until it takes hold of the upper arm.
And if it invades it, then cut off the arm at the elbow joint.
If, however, the corruption passes, and you see it approximately at the elbow, then in that case death is upon the sick person. And to administer beyond that, in terms of treatment, is the measure of skill.
And similarly do with the foot, when corruption invades the toe, then cut it off at one of its joints; and if it takes hold of the sole of the foot, then cut off the entire foot. If however it ascends to the knee, then cut off the leg at the knee joint.
If however the corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no remedy for it, except release, and to leave the sick person to die.
And the method of cutting or sawing a limb is that you should tighten the ligament beneath the one you wish to cut; and tighten another ligament above the place. And let the surgeon extend one ligament downwards and let the surgeon extend the other ligament upwards. And separate the flesh which is between the two ligaments with a broad spatula until all the flesh is exposed. Then it should be cut or sawn.
And it is necessary that you place linen cloths on all sides, and that the saw not touch a healthy part; and that an added injury and a hot abscess befall the sick person.
If however an internal flow of blood occurs while you are operating, then cauterize the place quickly, or place upon it certain powders that staunch the blood. Then return to your treatment until it is complete.
Then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage, and care for it until it is healed.
And I tell you that it happened to a man in his foot, this same happening that I tell you.
That is, because it happened in his foot, a blackness, with a burning which resembles the burning of fire: and that corruption first occurred in his toe, until it began the whole foot.
Therefore, the man hastened from himself, after he saw the corruption descend upon the limb with its vehemence, which he found from its pain and burning: and it struck him at the joint, and he was healed.
And after a long time passed, that same corruption happened to him in the index finger of his hand.
So he came to me, and I tried to recover that superfluity with what I placed on his hand from medicines after cleansing his body: and the superfluity was not repelled, and it began to proceed to another finger: until the corruption began in the hand.
Therefore, he called me to cut his hand: and I did not want that, hoping for a recovery of that superfluity from me: and I also feared death for him at the incision of his hand. Because the man's strength was over the case.
After he despaired of me, he returned to his own land: and it came to me about him, that he hurried, and cut his entire hand: and he was healed.
And I did not bring this account, unless it should be an aid concerning that which falls from this kind of illness: and that it might be a signification by which it may be signified, and according to which it may be acted.
Concerning the treatment of Sinuses, and the quality of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in certain fleshy members, and the abscess's space prolongs itself until it forms a collection of pus: then it bursts or is perforated, and that which is in it of pus comes out, and a hollow place remains, like a vessel: and the skin that is over it, like a cloth that is now thinned: and it does not reach its final state of decay, nor does it affect the mouth, nor the nerve, nor the ligament: it deserves to be called a Sinus: and it is not called a Fistula: however, if its space prolongs itself until the decay affects any of these members, then it is called a Fistula, or zuken.
And its treatment is to cut away that entire skin, as it revolves: and especially when it has thinned and become like a cloth: and you trust that it does not adhere to the place, due to its decay.
However, if you hope that the skin adheres, and that decay does not result from that final adherence: and if there is thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with an enema: and it is to be considered as a Sinus.
Then if it is large, and the pus that is emitted from it has a foul odor: then enema it with Egyptian medicine, which is vinegar, and oil, and ziniar, taking equal parts, to be mixed in a vessel: and let them be boiled over fire, until the medicine turns red: and it comes to the thickness of honey.
Then take from it what you need: and dissolve it with water and honey, and enema the sinus with it: and compress its opening, and leave the medicine in it for the amount of two hours.
Then extract it with pressure: do this for so many days, until the sinus is cleansed, and the foul odor is removed.
And sometimes it is enemated with ash water, when this medicine is not present for you: and it is so that you should take the ashes of vine wood, or ashes from the holm oak wood: and pour water over it. Then strain it, and enema with it, until you are confident that the sinus is now cleansed.
If the sick person does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine, nor the ash water, then enema it with water and honey, to which something of crushed ziniar has already been mixed. Or enema it with mixed honey and wine, since it is the property of honey to cleanse and wash away; and wine causes the sinus to adhere, especially when there is an excess of astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you do it several times, and are confident that the corruption of the sinus has been removed: then enema it with that which causes flesh to grow there.
And take from the dyafinicon plaster, dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine, or enema it with certain medicines and other ointments, whose properties I have written in the treatise on ointments.
If the sinus opening is constricted, so that the enema cannula does not fit: then enlarge it slightly with iron, or place a linen cloth wrapped in Egyptian ointment or in basil ointment on it, until it is enlarged.
And similarly, if the opening is also wide, then bring together its edges with sutures, and leave it of a size that the enema can enter without additions.
And similarly, if the opening from which pus flows is elevated upwards: then cut it in the lower part within, so that the pus flows downwards; because when pus is constricted in the depth of the sinus, it prevents flesh from growing there.
If you cannot cut the sinus approximately below the place you wish, then try to prepare the limb in such a way that pus flows from it easily, according to the preparation you have. Try to not retain any pus at all within, and take consolidating medicines that are put on the sign, suitable for what you want. And spread them over a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such size that it covers the entire sinus. Then cut with scissors a hole, larger than the sinus opening, directly in the opening of the sinus. Then spread some mild ointments on another cloth, according to the size of the hole; and place it over the former, further away from the sinus opening, so that the pus may more easily flow out from it; and do not remove the large cloth, except after a few days, at one time.
However, the one over the sinus opening, is the one you remove every hour, so that what gathers in it from the pus may come out; and so that you may know whether the matter running from it is much, or little; or digested, or undigested.
And with this, again consider the location of the sinus itself, whether the master of it feels pain in it, or there is no pain in it, and whether there is an abscess in it, or not.
Therefore, when your treatment is thus extended, and you see the matter that flows out over the sinus opening, then know that the sinus is now adhering, or approaching adhesion: then therefore, undo it in the morning, or on the third day; and when you undo the wound, then change another cloth, and renew the ointment.
However, if thin matter flows from the sinus after a long period, then do not despair of its adhesion, for its healing is approaching, because there are people who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds, and there are those who act contrary to that.
If, however, undigested pus flows from the sinus after many days, then know that the sinus does not adhere.
If, therefore, the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and its condition prolonged, then add to the drying medicines, but let the physicians make sure that the medicines are moist in their substance and dry in their effect; like the unguentum dyafinicon, when superfluity of the addition of zegi is added to it.
And of the more final means by which it is treated, it is that you take the unguentum dyafinicon, and dissolve it with rose oil, and sprinkle upon it old wine, equal in its substance.
Then mix it with that, and use it.
And now it does a similar action to this, when the unguentum dyafinicon is not present to you, honey, when you boil it until it thickens, and use it. Or take myrrh, and aloes, and frankincense: grind the whole thing, and powder it over the honey while it is over the fire. Then line a cloth over it, and bind it over the sinus: and line with the honey that you boiled until it thickened: and powder spices over it, and put over the cloth, and bind it.
And sometimes long aristolochia, and the roots of the lily of the valley, and the lily are administered in it: and the herb flour, and centaury. Use them simple, or aggregated, grind them, and sift them: and powder them over the honey which I mentioned, and use it.
If the sinus condition persists and is not cured by this treatment, then know that there is corruption within it; or it is already making an impression on the bones, or on the other hard members, or ligaments: then treat it with the treatment of fistulas, as was previously described.
Concerning the treatment of whitlow and worn nail, and the incision of the added finger, and section.
A whitlow is an abscess that arises under the nail of the thumb of the hand or foot, and perhaps arises in the other fingers.
Therefore, when its condition is prolonged and its treatment neglected, and the abscess becomes a hot abscess, or it corrupts and discharges pus until it gnaws at the root of the nail, and perhaps corrupts it entirely, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, until it has a foul odor; and the end of the finger becomes wide, and its color becomes dark.
Therefore, when you treat it, as we have said in the division, and your treatment does not help, then you must cut with a knife all the superfluity that remains from the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For the cautery is very helpful.
If however the bone is healthy, and the nail is also healthy, and the superfluous parts of the nail are now pressing the flesh inwards, and begin to pierce it and injure it, then you must place a thin spatula under the nail-part that is piercing the flesh, and push it upwards; and cut the flesh with ease, and put on that which remains of the flesh corrosive and burning medicines until the entire part of it is removed. Then treat it with ointments until it heals.
If however corruption has already affected the bone, then it is necessary to cut the bone and remove it: for it will never heal while the corrupted bone remains in it.
If you see corrosion and corruption affecting the finger, then cut it in one of the knots, according to the remembrance of what has been said previously. Then treat it until it heals.
If, however, a nail suffers abrasion or impact, and severe pain occurs, then it is necessary to first bleed the patient. Then cut the nail with a sharp spatula in a transverse cut from top to bottom.
And be careful that the cut does not reach the flesh beneath the nail, for you will cause the patient intense pain by doing so, and it will be the cause of added flesh growing in that place.
Then treat the place with something that relieves pain.
A finger, however, that is added in some people's hands, is perhaps entirely fleshy; and perhaps there are bones in some of them, and perhaps there is a nail in it; and some of them originate at the root of a joint, or its origin is in some nodes of the finger. Therefore, the one that originates in some nodes of the finger does not move; and the one that originates near the joint of the finger perhaps moves.
Therefore, the one that is fleshy among them, its incision is easy. That is, cut it at its root with a broad scalpel.
However, the treatment of the one whose origin is in the root of the joint is difficult. Therefore, abstain from its incision.
However, in the one that arises on the finger at the last of the nodes, then it is necessary to first cut its flesh with a round incision, down to the bone. Then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for it. Then treat it until it heals.
However, the consolidation that occurs between the fingers often happens either from the person's birth, that is, is born with it, or from the consolidation of a wound, or from the burning of fire, and similar things.
Therefore, it is necessary to separate that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural shape. Then place between them strips of cloth, or cloths soaked in rose oil, so that they do not become consolidated quickly, and separate them: or place a thin sheet of lead between them until the place is consolidated as it should be.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs between certain fingers and the palm, then cut that consolidation, according to what is prepared and suitable for the shape of the limb.
Concerning the incision of a defect.
Ulcers are convoluted, thick veins, filled with melancholic humors, which occur in many parts of the body; and their effect is most often in the legs, especially in the legs of those who travel and work, and carry burdens.
Therefore, it is necessary that you administer a purging of the body from black bile, sometimes with strong purging.
Then bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
The treatment of these, however, with iron, is according to two methods. One of them is that they be cut, and the black blood be drawn out. And the other method is that the vein be raised, and the whole of it be drawn out.
However, its incision is according to this method. First, bathe the leg thoroughly in hot water until the thick, foul blood is resolved. Then bind the patient's leg from the upper part of the hip, down to the lower part of the knee, with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one, or two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then raise, that is, draw out the blood in your hand from the lower part of the leg, up to the upper part, and the phlebotomist must first do this for the patient. Then cut the nail, going lower until a quantity of blood sufficient and tolerable by the patient's strength emerges.
Afterwards, tie it (the vein); and order him to abstain from foods generating black bile; and again perform evacuation and phlebotomy when the veins are full, and that harms the patient.
Its elevation, however, is according to this method. Shave the patient's leg, if it has many hairs. Then immerse him in a bath; or apply a warm water embrocation to his leg until it reddens and the veins swell; or, if a bath is not available, have him exercise vigorously until the limb is heated.
Then cut the skin in a straight line along the vein's course: either at its end near the knee or at its lower part near the heel.
Then, open the skin with hooks, and scrape the vein from every side until it appears to the senses:  and at its appearance, you will see it sloping from a red to a black hue. When it emerges from the skin, you will see it white like a chord.
Then, introduce a probe underneath it until, when it is lifted and emerges from the skin, suspend it with a blunt, gentle hook.
Then cut another incision near that incision by three fingers' breadth. Then scrape the skin from above the vein until it appears. Then lift it with probes as you did, and suspend it with another hook, as you did at first.
Then cut another incision, or several incisions, if you need them. Then lift, and cut it at the very last incision near the heel.
Then pull it out to the incision that is above it.
And do that until you pull it out from the incision that is higher, or higher than all the incisions, until when the whole thing comes out: then cut it.
If, however, it does not obey you in pulling and lifting it, then insert a needle with a strong double thread, and tie it, and pull it out: or insert a probe under it, and turn your hand in every direction until it comes out. And be careful not to cut it. For if it is cut, it becomes difficult for you to lift it greatly, and it enters upon the sick person from it harm.
When therefore you have pulled it all out, then put on the wound site wool soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil, and care for it until it is healed.
If, however, the vine is contracted, having a twisting to which there is a wrapping to the parts, and is not as we said according to straightness, then it is necessary to cut into it in every direction of its twisting and its wrapping places.
Then suspend it with hooks, until you lift it all: and be careful in your incision on it that you do not cut a vein, or wound it, for it will become difficult for you to lift or remove it. Therefore, preserve your prudence.
The form of the incident by which the vine is healed.
The form of the blind hook.
It does not have the curvature like the other hooks, nor a sharp tip so as not to wound the vein: and it has a thick, smooth bend, because if it were thin, it would cut the vein with its thinness: indeed, let it have an excessive thickness, as we have said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in legs in hot lands, such as Algezi and the land of the Arabs, and in arid, hot lands, of little tenderness, that is, in which neither grass nor tree grows.
And perhaps it is generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And indeed its generation is from the putrefaction that occurs under the skin, as happens within the bodies of snakes and worms, and worms generated between the skin and flesh.
And the sign of the beginning of the effect of this vein is that a violent inflammation occurs in the leg. Then the place becomes blistered. Then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that blistering, as if it were the root of a plant, or an animal.
When therefore its extremity appears from it, then it is necessary that you wrap a piece of lead over it, whose weight is from one drachma keil up to two drachmas, and tie it; and leave the lead hanging from the leg.
Whenever therefore something emerges from it to the exterior, wrap it in lead, and tie it. If it extends a great deal, then cut off a part of it, and wrap the remainder. And do not cut it at the root before the whole of it emerges. For if you cut it, it contracts and enters the flesh, and causes an abscess and putrefaction in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore it is necessary that it be gently treated and extracted gradually until it emerges from its last part, and nothing of it remains in the body; and from this vein indeed there emerges in some men what is in its length five palms and ten. And it has already come to me that it emerged from a man of twenty palms.
If, however, it is incised during your time of treatment, then insert a rod into its opening and pierce it with a long opening, together with the body, until all the matter within it is evacuated; and treat the putrefaction of the place with medicines for several days. Then treat it with the treatment of abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the joint of the foot, or in the foot itself; and many openings occur to it, and a branch emerges from every opening.
Therefore treat it, as we said in the division, and with what has been previously mentioned.
Concerning the incision on worms generated under the skin, and it is called the Disease of cattle.
This disease is called in a certain land among us the Disease of cattle; because it often happens to oxen, and is a small worm that is born between the skin and the flesh.
And it travels throughout the body, ascending and descending; and it is made evident to the senses by its movement from limb to limb, until it breaks through wherever it breaks through the skin, and emerges.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humours. As happens with worms and snakes, that is, intestinal worms, and threadworms in the belly.
And it is feared because of its malice. Because when it travels through the body, and ascends to the head, and reaches a location, then perhaps it bursts open there and emerges, and destroys the eye; and this happens many times.
Therefore, when one wishes to cure it and extract it, then it is not done except at the time of its movement and its appearance to the senses.
Therefore, it is necessary to tie what is above it and what is below it with a strong ligature. Then cut above it and extract it.
If it penetrates into the flesh, and you do not find it, then apply a cautery to the place with fire until it dies.
And the greater fear is its corruption, by which it corrupts the eye, as we said.
If, however, you see it already having reached the head near the eye, then tie the eyebrow beneath it with a good ligature. Then cut above it, and extract it.
And it is necessary for the sick person to use cleansing of their body, with medicines that dissolve bad putrid humors.
And let him beware of foods that generate putrefaction.
Concerning the incision above the sickness of Nakir.
This sickness is called Nakir in our land: and it is a pain that happens in certain limbs; then it changes from limb to limb.
And I myself have indeed seen it, as I relate to you. I was called to a sick woman in certain villas. And she uncovered her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm's cord.
And after I had stayed an hour, I saw one swelling move with a tremor, as a worm moves, ascending to her shoulder more quickly than possible: to be like quick silver, when it runs from place to place.
Therefore, the pain receded from that place, and settled in the shoulder.
Then I sat for an hour, and it ran to the rest of the body, until it reached the other arm.
Then she told me that it was turning throughout her entire body, as I had seen.
I was therefore amazed at the speed of its change from limb to limb. And I myself had not previously seen this illness with my own eyes in such a manner. However, I have seen many who find pain changing from limb to limb: and I did not see it with my own eyes, as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think it to be anything other than because she was of those who are of dry-bodied people with detected veins: hence, that airiness appears to the senses in that changed form.
And it is necessary that they not appear in this way in those who are delicate, and with moist bodies, with hidden veins.
When therefore you wish to cure it, and the patient feels that pain: then if it appears to you with the eye, as we said, then grasp above it, and below it quickly: and cut above it, until that compressed airiness comes out: and cauterize the place.
If however you do not see it with your eyes: then cure it with shaking of the body, and with that which expels airiness, and pacifies them with foul pills, and pills of serapion, and similar things.
Concerning the extraction of Arrows.
Arrows differ according to their kinds and according to the places in which they strike the body.
Their diversity is, indeed, because some of them have large heads, and small ones, and concave ones, and blunt ones. And some of them have three angles. And some of them have tongues. And some of them have stars.
Those which are according to the members on which they fall are according to two ways. For either the members are from the principal concave members, such as the brain, and the heart, and the liver, and the lungs, and the kidneys, and the intestines, and the bladder, and similar to them. Therefore, when it falls into any of these members, and signs of death appear to you, which I will relate to you later, then you ought to avoid extracting that arrow from them, for death follows it in that in which it is, in many dispositions. And when these bad signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden deep within the member, then extract it, and treat the wound.
For the signs of the brain, when an arrow falls into it, and penetrates the bone, and the siphac, which is above the brain, comes forth, are: because vehement madness, and scotoma, and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and swelling, and redness of the tongue, and spasm, and confusion of the intellect, and vomiting of cholera, occur from it. And perhaps blood comes forth from the nostrils, or from the ears. And perhaps speech is severed, and the voice is destroyed. And white moisture like gruel emerges from the place of the wound. And from it emerges something like the watery aspect of flesh.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then refrain from treatment, and extract the arrow if it does not emerge.
The signs of an arrow striking the heart are: that it is near the left nipple; and that one feels as if it were fixed in a hard thing, not in an empty thing. And perhaps the arrow's movement is similar to the movement of a pulse; and black blood flows from the wound. And that is followed by coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting. Know therefore, that death descends without a doubt.
And the sign of an arrow wounding the lung is the exit of frothy blood from the wound: and the vessels which follow the neck become suppurated, and the sick person's complexion changes. And he breathes out a large, deep breath: and seeks the attraction of cold air.
If the arrow strikes the covering, which is in the breast, then it is near the small ribs: and there is a large gasp with violent pain, with a sigh: and all the shoulder limbs are moved.
And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then perhaps undigested food will exit from the wound.
And if the arrow strikes the belly, and is fixed in it, some excrement, or zirb, or already ruptured intestine, will exit from the wound. Therefore, there is no hope for its cure, nor for the extraction of the arrow.
If the arrow strikes the bladder, and urine escapes, and something proceeds from it outwards, and the pain is stronger upon the weak, then know that it is mortal.
However, the remaining members, such as the face, and neck, and throat, and shoulder, and the arm, and the vertebrae of the back, and collarbone, and hip, and leg, and similar members are saved according to various dispositions, when the arrow does not strike an artery or nerve, and the arrow is not poisoned.
And I tell you what I have seen concerning these arrows, so that you may indicate through that on your treatment.
Because an arrow struck a man in the tear duct of his eye, at the root of his nose. Therefore, I extracted it from the other side, under the fat of his ear: and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which struck him in the fat of his eye under the lower eyelid.  And the arrow was already hidden, and I did not obtain from it except a small extremity, with which it adheres to its shaft.  And the arrow was a large arrow of the Turkish bows, with soft iron, in which there are no ears.
Therefore, the Jew was healed, and no evil accident occurred in his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arabic arrow, and it is the kind that has ears. Therefore, I cut above it between the two guides: and it was already submerged in his throat. Therefore, I worked with it, until I extracted it: and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from the man which had fallen into his belly: and we thought that he would die from it.
After therefore thirty days had passed, and nothing was changed upon him from his dispositions, I cut above the arrow: and I worked upon it, and extracted it: and he was healed, and no evil accident happened to him.
And I saw a man whom an arrow had struck in his back, and the wound was consolidated over it. After therefore it was after seven years, the arrow exited at the root of his thigh.
And I saw a woman whom an arrow had struck in her belly, and the wound was consolidated over it, and the arrow remained within her: and nothing was changed in her dispositions, and the woman found no harm in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man, whom an arrow struck in his face: and the wound was consolidated, and it remained, causing him not much pain: and many similar things to these.
And I extracted the arrow from the king's nobleman, which had struck him in the middle of his nose, and had already bent slightly to the right side: and the arrow was completely hidden.
I was therefore called to treat him after the arrow's impact for up to three days. I therefore found the arrow wound to be very severe.
I therefore sought it with a fine probe, and did not sense it: and indeed he was finding a prick and pain under his right ear. I therefore hoped that that prick was from the arrow's tip.
I therefore plastered the place with a plaster having attractive and digestive properties, confident that the place would become abscessed and that the arrowhead would appear so that I could cut upon it: and no sign occurred in the place signifying that the arrow had struck the location.
I therefore applied the plaster to it for many days: and no sign occurred, and the wound meanwhile healed: and he remained weak from its extraction for a space of days, until he felt the arrow one day within his nose. And he told me that.
And he told me that. I therefore applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to that wound for many days, until it opened: and when I probed it, I felt a slender arrowhead adhering to the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound through that sharp medicine, until the arrowhead appeared visibly to us: and I spent about four months with him in that time.
Then, after the wound had been enlarged, and it was possible for me to insert the forceps into it, and move it, and it did not yield to extraction, I did not cease to refine and devise means upon it, until I began, one day, with forceps suitably fashioned, as their form will appear at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And indeed physicians judge that the cartilage of the nose is not restored. Therefore I restored it, and the wound was consolidated, and the sick man was healed with complete health; and nothing at all harmed him.
And I tell you the quality of the extraction of some arrows: so that you may place it as a rule and meaning upon that which I have not said.
Because the parts of this art and their distinctions are not comprehended with the narration; nor does that book comprehend it, and a prudent craftsman compares little things to many things, and the present to the absent; and he finds a new operation and a new instrument in the descent of foreign things of this art when they occur.
Therefore, I say that arrows do not emerge from the limbs in which they are fixed, except in two ways. Or by traction from the place from which it entered. Or by the contrary of the other part.
And that which emerges from where it entered is either an arrow proceeding into fleshy tissue: and is pulled, and emerges.
If it does not yield to exit from the hour in which it fell, then it is necessary to leave it for some days, until the flesh which is around it has rotted: and its traction and its exit will be alleviated.
And similarly if it is fixed in the bone, and does not yield to you for exit, then leave it also for some days: and repeatedly try it with traction and movement every day, until it emerges.
If it does not yield to you for exit after days, then it is necessary to pierce around the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a fine drill, until the arrow is enlarged. Then pull it, and extract it.
If however the arrow is already hidden in a place of the body, and is concealed and hidden from sense: then seek it with probes. If however you feel it, then pull it out with certain instruments suitable for its extraction.
If however you cannot get at it, because of the constriction of the wound, and because of the length of the arrow deep within: and there is no bone, nor nerve, nor vein there: then enlarge over it, until the wound is widened, and it is possible to extract the arrow.
If it has ears by which to hold it, then free the flesh fixed in them from every side, by all the means you can, and if means are not enough to free the flesh from the breaking and twisting of the ears, until it escapes.
And when you administer the extraction of the arrow, wherever it is, then use the twisting of your hand, with forceps, in all directions, until you make it escape: and by the finest subtlety, so that the arrow is not broken, and its extraction and pulling become difficult for you.
If you cannot get at it within an hour, then leave it for a few days, until the surrounding parts putrefy. Then try again: for it will be easier then.  But if a flow of blood opposes you, then administer what we said about treatment in its chapter: and guard your skill from incision of a vein, or nerve, or tendon.
And use every means possible for the arrow's escape, and let it be with ease and calmness, or with slowness and firmness, as I told you.
And it is necessary, when you pull the arrow, that the patient be weak according to the form in which the arrow was lodged. Because it is more helpful. If, however, that is not possible for you, then administer what is possible for you from the figures.
However, an arrow that emerges from the opposite side of the other part, either already protrudes from it outward, or the arrow's tip makes a swelling on the skin's surface, and you see it projecting: therefore, cut above it, and let the cut be the amount that forceps can grasp in it.
Then pull it out; for it will easily be extracted.
If, however, it is retained in the bone, then turn your hand in a circular motion until the arrow presses against the bone and enlarges itself; then pull it out.
And if not, leave it for a few days; then try it again, until it emerges.
If the arrow's wood is within it, then push it out along with it.
If, however, the wood has fallen away, and you wish to administer a push, then insert a concave instrument, so that its concavity enters the arrow's tail; then push it out with it.
If, however, the arrow is hollow, then push it with an instrument that enters it. For the arrow is lightened by it.
If the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary to cauterize all the flesh in which the poison is, if it is possible for you. Then treat it with whatever is suitable for it.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side, and is near enough to that which you touch with a probe, and it is possible for you to cut above it, then cut, and beware of cutting a vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then sew the wound, if you see it, and treat it until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps with which arrows are extracted.
Let the ends be similar to the beak of a bird, made of iron; when they grasp the arrow, they do not release it.
And many forms are made from them, large, small, and medium, according to the quantity, size, and smallness of the arrow, and the breadth and constriction of the wound.
The form of the impelling concave one.
And the form of the impelling deaf one.
This is a deaf extremity like a ray, so that its entrance into the concave arrow may be eased, and it may be impelled with it.
Of the phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins to be bled, of which the use has run through the body, are thirty.
Of these, sixteen are in the head: two pulsating veins, which are behind the ears, called the two Herbs; and two arteries, which are appearing in the two temples; and two veins which are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, which are called the Lookers; and a prepared vein in the middle of the forehead; and a vein which is at the extremity of the nose. And two Guidegi, which are in the neck. And two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins which are in the lower lip. And these are the veins which are called Algecherich. And two veins which are under the tongue.
The veins, however, that are bled in the arms, are five veins in every arm.
One of them is the Cephalic, and it is from the wild side; and the common people call it the vein of the head. And the Fusca. And is the Middle vein, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilic and a branch of the Cephalic: and the common people call it the vein of the body. And the Basilic, and it is situated on the domestic side; and it is also called the Titillicaris. And the common people call it the vein of the belly. And the Cord of the arm, and it is situated above the elbow; and it is that which is bled from, and it is that which appears above the thumb with clear appearance.
And the Saluatella: and it is the vein which is between the ring finger and the little finger; and it has two branches.
And in the leg and foot there are three veins. One of them is that which is under the curve of the thumb from the wild side. And the second is the Saphena; and its place is at the heel from the domestic side. And the vein Nesse; and its place is at the heel from the wild side.
And in the other leg are three veins similar to those.
Concerning the phlebotomy of two veins behind the ears.
Phlebotomies of the two veins behind the ears are helpful for chronic colds, and hemicrania, and sahafati, and bad old head ulcers.
And the quality of the phlebotomy is according to what I describe. And it is that the patient's head be shaved, and his occiput be rubbed in the location of the two veins with a rough cloth, with good friction. Then the patient should tighten his neck with a strip, until two veins appear. And their locations are behind the ears in two depressed places of the head. Therefore, seek them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse beneath your finger, there mark with ink.
Then take a phlebotomous knife, and it is the one which is called Incidens. Then insert it beneath the vein in the skin, until the phlebotomous reaches the opening. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards, and cut the vein with the skin with an incident incision. And let the length of the incision be the amount of two joined fingers, or about that: and cause as much blood to exit as you wish.
Then bind it with bandages, and leave it until it heals.
And already in the beginning of the book there was a description of their incision, and their cauterization.
The phlebotomy of the two arteries which are in the two temples is a benefit for the ancient hemiplegia, and for crooked paralysis, and for incessant ophthalmia, and for the flow of excessive acute fluids to the eyes.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is according to what I tell you. Let the sick man hold his neck tightly, until two veins become distinctly apparent to the senses, and their pulse be demonstrated under your finger. Then mark the signs with ink.
Then raise the skin above the vein upwards with your index finger, and insert the phlebotomous incision underneath, and raise the vein upwards. And cut it, as you did in the other two veins, and cause blood to flow according to your need.
Then loosen the sick person's constriction, and place your finger upon the vein for one hour. Afterwards place over it a cloth and cushions, and bind it tightly above the constriction; and leave it until it heals.
And already the remembrance of them, and their incision, and their extraction has preceded in the beginning of the book.
Indeed, the benefit of phlebotomy of the frontal vein is after phlebotomy of the Cephalic vein, for the old ailments of the face; and for hemicrania, and redness; and foul ulcers.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is, according to what I tell you. The sick man tightens his neck with a bandage until the vein appears. Then take the instrument called Fossorium.
Place the prominent point that is on the head of the Fossorium over the vein itself, and strike upon it with a comb, or with something else in its manner; and allow the blood to flow according to the quantity you wish. Then loosen the wound of the sick person, and bind it until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a wide phlebotome. However, it should not be a phlebotome with a sharp end, like other phlebotomes, but rather one with a broad end. And phlebotomize it in a transverse manner, since the bone is close by; and perhaps the phlebotome will break in it when it is slender.
However, the aid of the two veins which are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes is for the eye from scabies and redness; and for speckles in the eye, and for ailments of the face.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is such that it binds a weak neck with a strip. Then phlebotomize them, and you be standing over his head.
And let the phlebotomy be made transverse to the length slightly, with a small phlebotom, a little broad, for there is no flesh in the place. For if the phlebotom is of a subtle extremity, perhaps it breaks.
Then make the blood run out as much as you need. And place a compress over them, and bind them for one night, and unbind them.
Phlebotomy of the veins of the nose is beneficial for acute fevers, and violent headaches, and ailments of the face, such as hemicrania, and redness that occurs in the face, especially when it is old.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that it binds the weak neck. Then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a slender, long lancet, and insert it into the middle of its nostril, between that which distinguishes the nose according to its straightness. Because the vein does not appear to the senses there, and because blood begins to flow immediately.
And you ought to hold your hand with the lancet for a little while, and cause blood to run according to your need. Then bind it with a bandage. For it is restored quickly.
Indeed, phlebotomy of the Guidegi's veins is a help against shortness of breath, and the beginning of leprosy, and melancholic illnesses, and impetigo and bad ulcers, and those that corrode, that is, summer herpes.
And the quality of phlebotomy of them is such that the infirm person should be bound tightly beneath both his shoulders with a bandage, and the artisan should stand over the head of the infirm person, and the infirm person should sit on a seat.
Then the phlebotomist should make a vein incision to the length of the phlebotomy, wide for a short time. Then draw out a moderate quantity of blood, or according to that which you see necessary for it. Then do likewise with the other vein.
Afterwards, loosen the bandage, and bind the two veins with a moderate ligature, so that the infirm person is not over-constricted. And leave it until morning. For the wound is healed.
Concerning phlebotomy of the veins, Algeberich. The veins, however, according to Algeberich, are a help in phlebotomy: because they contribute to the sore throat, and the corruption of the gums: and to bad ulcers: and cracks of the lips, and bad ulcers, even those that are in the nose, and in its surrounding area.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is such that you should make the infirm person sit before you, and bind his neck with a bandage. Then roll back his lip, and look at the two veins, one of which is to the right of the lip, and the second to its left: and the demonstration of each is by the blackness of both. And that is true, because in their surrounding area are small, black veins. Therefore, cut each with a single incision.
If however there is doubt upon you, and you do not know which of them it is, then aim at the incision of the larger and more manifest of them.
And similarly do with the two veins which are in the upper lip: and indeed, the custom mostly runs with it: and cut the two veins which are in the lower lip.
Concerning phlebotomy of the veins under the tongue after the phlebotomy of the cephalic veins. However, the benefit of the phlebotomy of the two veins which are under the tongue is that phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is for the obstructions which are in the throat, and the ailment of the uvula, and the ailments of the mouth.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is such that the weak person sits between your hands in front of the sun: and raise his tongue, and look under his tongue on one side of it a vein, and on the other side of it a vein: and the color of each of them sloping towards blackness.
Therefore phlebotomize each of them: and beware lest you extend your hand too far when phlebotomizing them. For under them are arteries, and perhaps a flow of blood will occur from those arteries.
The three veins, however, which are phlebotomized in the elbow, are those in whose phlebotomy custom has run in all men.
And their phlebotomy is done according to two methods. Either by pricking with a broad myrtle phlebotome: or a small olive-shaped one, sloping: or by cutting with a knife-shaped phlebotome, and it is Alnesil. And this is its form.
And from them are broad and narrow types, according to the wideness and narrowness of the veins. The form of the myrtle phlebotome should be broad, as you see. And it is suitable for opening concave, full, proceeding, apparent, large veins, and those which contain thick, turbid blood.
And this phlebotome is of smaller width, and of finer extremity.
And it is suitable for the phlebotomy of fine veins, which contain thin, yellowish blood.
The Basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, is a help for phlebotomy. Because it extracts blood from illnesses that are under the throat and neck, from that which follows the chest, and the abdomen: and it is fitting for the phlebotomist, when phlebotomizing it, to be cautious, and to be above fear from it, for the artery is under it. Therefore, if he errs, and adds in the phlebotomy puncture, he will strike that artery, and a flow of blood will occur. Therefore, it is necessary that he not phlebotomize it with the phlebotomy lancet; rather, let him use a cutting instrument, like the Alnesil.
If the Basilic vein does not appear clearly, then it is necessary to abandon it, and switch to another, or look for one of its branches; or phlebotomize the brachial vein in its place. For it is clearly visible, and cut it with the phlebotomy lancet Anesil, as we have said.
If, however, you wish to phlebotomize the same vein, then it is necessary, before tightening the arm, to feel the area, until you locate the pulse. Then mark the area with ink, next the arm, and cut the vein with a concave incision using the phlebotomy lancet Alnesil, as we have said. And beware that the incision does not fall a distance from the artery's location.
And when you see, as you tighten the ligature, inflation in the place you marked with ink, then that inflation is the inflation of the artery: therefore, avoid it.
If you see blood at the phlebotomy leap, like a child's urine, and the blood is thin, red: then know that it is from the artery's blood. Then quickly place your finger on it for a long space, then remove your finger. If, therefore, blood is cut off (for it is often cut off), then tighten the arm and release it; and let the patient beware of moving it; and let it be on his fear.
And do not move it for several days, until it is healed.
If, however, the blood is not cut off, and it overcomes you, and there is no medicine present for you at your moment, then cut the artery if it appears to you. For its extremities are contracted, and blood is cut off.
Or take the bark of the fig tree, and split it; take one half, and tie it over the place of the vein's constriction with a bandage and dressings until another day.
Then if the blood is cut off, it is good. And if not, then treat it with that which preceded it in the memory from the place of powders that cut off the flow of blood; and the cutting off of its blood is not difficult in many cases, because of the small place of the wound, and the possibility of a bandage from the arm.
Phlebotomy of the Fusca vein is helpful, because it draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; because it is composed of a branch of the Basilica vein and a branch of the Cephalica vein, as we have said.
And it befits the phlebotomist to be cautious when performing phlebotomy on it, for there is a nerve beneath it. Therefore, if the phlebotomist, while making the incision, encounters a nerve, numbness results, and the healing becomes difficult, and perhaps it will not heal completely.
And this nerve frequently appears to the senses. However, if it is hidden in some people, and is subtle and not shown, then it is necessary that you place your phlebotomy, by which to phlebotomize it, the incision with Alnesil, and avoid the nerve with your skill.
If the vein is between two nerves, then split the vein lengthwise.
Concerning the Cephalic vein. Phlebotomy of the Cephalic vein is helpful, because it draws blood from the head, and is beneficial for eye ailments.
And it is necessary in this vein properly, if you wish, that you phlebotomize it by puncturing with an olive-shaped lancet or with a myrtle-shaped lancet, because it is the safest of all veins, since there is no underlying artery or nerve.
However, it is appropriate for you in phlebotomy to avoid injuring the elbow with the lancet, and to seek a gentler spot; and it does no harm to it, if not found with the first stroke, for it to be repeated with the phlebotomy several times.
However, perhaps it becomes suppurated in some people when it is not phlebotomized on the first stroke, but that suppuration does not harm it in any way.
Concerning the usefulness of Phlebotomy, and the precautions to be observed in it. The quality of Phlebotomy, and its resulting effects, which you must consider in correcting.
First, you should know that phlebotomy is not performed except in the preservation of health, and its persistence, and caution against the onset of illness.
And so that phlebotomy be performed on one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the Cephalic, and the Basilic, and the Median cubital. And phlebotomy should be performed in the beginning of spring on an empty stomach, in the spring and summer, and in autumn and winter after eating, when signs of fullness appear.
And phlebotomy be done on the first or third day after three hours have passed of the day.
However, phlebotomy given in illnesses does not have a set time: but when the need and appropriateness call for it, by night, or by day, and in every hour, and in every time.
And it is not necessary that boys be bled until they reach fourteen years. Nor should a pregnant woman be bled, except from the fourth to the seventh month. Nor should old people who have already passed sixty years be bled.
Therefore, when someone wishes to be bled for whatever reason, it is necessary that they cleanse their bowels beforehand with a gentle clyster, if there is much retained stool, so that the veins during the bleeding do not draw putrid harmful superfluities from the bowels to the principal limbs.
Nor should a patient suffering from toctina, that is, nausea of satiety; nor the inebriated, nor those recovering from drunkenness, be bled until that is removed from them. Nor a sick person with dropsy.
And let phlebotomy be avoided again after colic, vomiting, and diarrhea, and an excess of sexual activity, labor, exercise, vigils, fasting, and any condition that dissolves bodily or animal strength.
Then consider the subtlety of the humors before that, if the blood is thick with foods, drinks, and medicines, if it is possible to do so.
Then let him enter a bath, if nothing prevents him; or let him be exercised by some exercise, so that the blood may be refined.
And place his phlebotomy in the chest, about the third day, as we have said; and strive that his chest be evacuated that day of all evil animal accidents: such as anxiety, anger, and fear; and of all bodily accidents, such as labor, pain, superfluity of coitus, and the like.
And let him have present at his seat things with whose use the custom of men is accustomed, from aromatics, and scents, and rejoicing, and things similar to these, to the extent possible.
Then let the phlebotomist sit on a pillow that is higher than the pillow for the phlebotomist to sit on; afterwards, let him draw his arm out, and let the phlebotomist rub it with his hand twice or thrice. Then tie the ligament with a strap, and wrap it twice.
And let the constriction be moderate, because constriction, when it is unequal, or through excess in the constriction, then it hinders the flow of blood; or in its slackening, then it also hinders the flow of blood.
Then, after the constriction, it is necessary that the phlebotomist rub his hands together until the veins swell and become apparent to the senses.
Then the phlebotomist should wipe the phlebotomized area with a little old oil.
Then he shall place his left index finger slightly over the vein itself, below the place where he intends to make the phlebotomy, so that the vein does not move and the puncture is precise. Because there are veins which, like a fleeing chord, are found during phlebotomy; and there are those that are full of airiness; and when you place the phlebotom on them, they contract beneath the phlebotom, and the phlebotomist is deceived, and the phlebotom does not open the vein; and if he opens it, it is constricted; therefore it is necessary for the phlebotomist to be firm and careful in all these matters.
Then he should place the phlebotom down. If therefore the vein is opened at the first attempt, then it is good. And if not, then do it again a second time a little below or above that place with haste, if the place is not ulcerated.
If, however, it becomes ulcerated, or the patient becomes fearful, then leave him for a day or two days: and do not tighten the ligature. For perhaps it will draw a hot abscess. Nor let him enter a bath.
Then again he should practice phlebotomy, if he wishes.
If the opening is small, or the blood flows subtly, and you fear that not enough of the desired amount will escape from the blood, then again perform phlebotomy at the very opening in a straight line, and add a little in the operation.
And do this with haste, before the place becomes abscessed, for an abscessed place occurs in many people with small incisions.
If therefore you see it already abscessed, then do not repeat on it at all, for it does not excuse anything to you. And place something from the dregs of oil upon it, because it alleviates the flow of blood. And it is better in this place than the oil itself, and than other oils.
And similarly administer the dregs of oil in all your phlebotomies, by which you lance veins to prohibit the flow of blood: and the Tyrians and alfarach and sagzenea do it, when placed from one of them on the place.
For blood is thinned and resolved when it is thick.
If a large abscess should occur at the site of the phlebotomy, for it often happens, especially to one who is not a phlebotomist except on that occasion, or if the opening of the vein is small, then hurry and place upon the site a sponge soaked in water and salt, warmed for a short time; and bind it for one hour; because it is resolved.
And it is necessary that you do this after the exit of blood from the vein itself, with the completion of it, or from another vein.
If some blackness or greenness remains at the site after a few days, it does not harm; and if you wish, place some dissolved aloe or myrrh upon it; or something from the juice of mint, that is, balm or something similar to it.
And very often indeed, abscesses and swellings occur near phlebotomy in the Basilica.
Therefore, place your hand upon it; then if you find softness upon pressing it, then that swelling is bad. Therefore be careful to place upon it something of what we have said. For perhaps blood from an artery will flow from it.
But make a plaster on it with that which has astringency, so that the place is hardened. Then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
And it is necessary to extract from every man's blood according to the quantity of its virtue. And that which appears from the color prevailing over the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it go out until it becomes red; and similarly if you see it thick, then let it go out until it becomes thin; and similarly if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for one who is full, strong, and in need of bloodletting at once, that the phlebotomy of the vein be widened and the phlebotomist be broad; and in one who is weak, be the opposite of that.
And it is necessary to extract it in stages, and that the opening be narrow; and better that is administered in phlebotomy of a vein is that it be a transverse section, not a puncture.
And this method of phlebotomy is safe from a flow of blood, and from an incision of the nerve; and it is more praiseworthy and safer than phlebotomy by width and length.
And to whom it is accustomed to arrive at phlebotomy syncopes, it is necessary that he eat before the phlebotomy with moderate bread soaked in water of pomegranate juice; or with secaniabin, if it is heated; and extract from him from the blood in three times, or four.
And if he is of a cold complexion, then let him take before the phlebotomy bread soaked in the syrup of miel, or in syrup of honey aromatized with spices, or in an aromatic, fragrant syrup.
If fainting occurs during phlebotomy, and the cause of it is the exit of much blood, then it is necessary that he take in drink water of meat, and a subtle fragrant syrup; and that he use aromatics with algalia, and that his chest be lined with it; and that he use the rest of what we have said in the division in the chapter on syncopes from evacuation.
Whoever wishes to evacuate their arm, and to release their blood a second time, it is necessary for him whose phlebotomy is for much evacuation, and whose strength is weak, that they release the blood gradually by a quantity of strength in continuous days.
But whoever wishes to evacuate their arm and release their blood a second time, and their body is strong, then let them do it after seven hours, or nine, from their first phlebotomy.
But whoever wishes to draw blood from their body to the opposite part to which it inclines, it is necessary that it be evacuated for them on the second, or on the third day.
In whose body, however, there is much blood already heated, and sharpens, and causes fever to occur: it is necessary that blood depart from it in one instance, and a great quantity be drawn from it; and the opening be amplified until fainting occurs; after you have observed all the conditions of phlebotomy; and that you place your hand upon his pulse during the flow of blood, so that death does not occur in place of fainting. For often that happens, when the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligence falls.
And it is not proper, when one wishes to release the arm, and release blood a second time, when the opening of the vein is already closed, and the exit of blood becomes difficult, to press upon it with violence, and quickly bend or extend it. For that is very bad; indeed, leave it, until it is phlebotomized a second time.
Or remove with sharpness the clotted blood from the opening of the vein, and place some salt dissolved in water upon it. And place something of al-frokh tyriaca, or sagzenea, upon it, and apply a gentle pressure until the blood emerges.
If however the vein is already suppurated, then leave it, and do not cover it, until the abscess is resolved.
If, however, necessity compels you to draw blood a second time, and there is no excuse, then either phlebotomize it above that place, or in another arm in another vein.
However, the arm's vein is phlebotomized at the location of the Fusca and Basilica veins, when they are not found, and are hidden, because it is composed of them.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that the infirm person should immerse their hand in warm water until the hand becomes red, and the vein becomes clearly visible.
Then tie it above it with a ligature of medium constriction. Then phlebotomize it transversely, gradually, not laterally, nor lengthwise; and let the opening be wide, and let your phlebotomy be such as to phlebotomize above the joint of the hand for a short time.
If the flow of blood is prevented, then immerse the hand again in warm water, and allow the blood to flow into the water until you achieve your desired result.
However, if you are in the days of summer, then you will excuse yourself from repeating the immersion of the hand in warm water. And indeed the flow of blood is placed most abundantly in warm water in the time of winter.
And phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all veins, because there is no pulsating vein nor nerve beneath it.
Phlebotomy of the right hand's Saluatella vein is beneficial for liver ailments.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that you should bind the mahason of the hand with a bandage, or with your hand, after you introduce it into warm water, until the vein swells and is clearly visible. Then, incising the vein transversely. And if you cut it completely, it will not harm him in any way.
And keep your hand away from the phlebotomist. For beneath it are the nerves of the fingers bare of flesh.
Then return the hand to warm water, and allow the blood to flow into it. For if you do not return it to warm water, the blood clots in the opening of the vein, and is prevented from flowing.
When therefore you have drawn out the necessary quantity of blood, then place oil and salt upon the vein: so that it does not quickly consolidate.
And similarly it is necessary that you do with every tight branch.
The advantage of phlebotomy from the left hand, however, is that it is helpful for ailments of the spleen; and similarly do in its phlebotomy as you did in the second equally.
The advantage of saphenous phlebotomy is for ailments in the lower part of the body, such as ailments of the womb, and retention of menses, and infirmities of the kidneys, and old ulcers of the hips and legs, and similar ailments.
And the method of its phlebotomy is that the sick person should immerse their foot in warm water and make friction upon it until the veins swell; and tie above the joint of the foot with a bandage. And indeed the vein's location is near the heel, appearing towards the big toe; and branches from it into many branches on the foot's face.
Therefore, phlebotomize it in its larger branch, or near the heel in its confluence, because it is better and safer.
If you phlebotomize it on the foot's surface, then beware of the nerves that are under it on the foot's surface.
And place your phlebotomy so that you phlebotomize it across, as if you wish to cut it: and let the phlebotomist be skilled.
If however the exit of the blood is hindered, then again place his foot in warm water; and let the blood run into it, until it is evacuated.
If, however, the phlebotomist errs in the first instance, then he should repeat the phlebotomy a little higher up, for the place is safe, and you need not fear harm from it, when you beware of the nerves, as we said.
And similarly with the Saphena of the other foot equally.
The Sciatic vein's location, as we said, is near the heel on the wild side.
