And the aid of phlebotomy is for the pain of the hip, when it is due to hot blood.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is that the sick person should enter a bath and hurry; and that they should bind his leg from the side of the hip up to the heel by four fingers, with a long thin bandage: for it does not appear except for that.
When it therefore appears, then let the phlebotomist cut it according to whatever skill is possible to you, or according to the transverse method; and it is better either to cut it with an incision or to divide it with a section. For its location is safer, and it is in many people very hidden.
For its location is safer, and it is in many people very hidden. If you do not find it, and it does not appear to the senses at all, then let the phlebotomist cut some branch of it, and that is the one which appears on the back of the foot and towards the ear; and beware of the nerves, and make the blood run in whatever quantity you wish.
Then loosen the binding, and place a skin over the place of phlebotomy, and bind the place. For it is quickly healed.
Concerning cupping, and the quality of its use.
Cupping instruments are made from horns, and from metal, and from glass.
And cupping indeed is done in two ways. With scarification, and the extraction of blood; and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping which is without scarification, is in two ways. For it is either with fire. Or it is without fire.
And the cuppings which are administered with scarification and the extraction of blood are fourteen places of the body. Of which one is, the cupping Alnocrati, and is at the back of the head; and Alkehel, and is the middle of the neck; and the cuppings Alhagdahim, and are two lamina of the neck on both sides together; and the cuppings menti, and is under the jaw in the lower part of the mouth; and the cuppings of two shoulder-blades; and the cuppings Alachosos above the end of the tail; and the cuppings of two armpits, and are in the middle of the two arms; and the cuppings of two thighs; and the cuppings of two calves.
And indeed cupping does not draw blood from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and because of that no virtue is lost from them, as is the case with phlebotomy. Nor should cupping, whether with or without fire, be administered in any illness arising from repletion until the whole body is emptied.
If necessity prompts cupping from illness, or for habit, we administer it at any hour at the beginning of the month, at its end, and in the middle of it, and at any time.
This is because there are people who, when blood multiplies in them to the point where they need its extraction by cupping, find heaviness and headache in their heads.
And some of them find fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. And some of them find itching in their face and forehead, and darkness and dullness in their eyes.
And some of them scratch the place of their cupping. And some of them have their laughter multiplied. And some of them find the taste of blood in their mouth: or the tongue is suppurated, and they spit out blood. And some of them have their sleep multiplied. And some of them see in their dreams blood and redness, and slain men, and wounds, and things similar to these.
Therefore, when we see something like that, and especially if it is in the third middle of the month, we then order that venesection be performed after two or three hours have passed of the day.
The benefit of venesection of the nape of the neck is that it alleviates heaviness in the head and that which flows to the eyes. However, it is necessary that it be after the evacuation of the whole body.
And this venesection is in the place of a cephalic phlebotomy.
And it is not permitted to use it for one of cold disposition or in whom there is a cold. For it brings great harm to him.
And on account of that, it is not appropriate that the elderly use it, nor those with cold ailments in their head.
If one is constantly over it, forgetfulness is generated in him.
And therefore it is necessary to command the wind-doctor to put his hand for a short while lower with the windpipe, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
Ventosation, or Alkehel, is in place of the phlebotomy of the Fusca and the phlebotomy of the Basilica, and therefore it benefits asthma, and constriction of breathing, and rupture of the breathing instrument, and coughing, and repletion.
And it is necessary that the Alkehel cupping be impelled for a short time, because if it is placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
Ventosation of the alhagdahim benefits accidental pains in the head, and ophthalmia, and hemicrania, and perforation, and pain in the roots of the teeth: and it is in place of phlebotomy of the Basilica.
And it is necessary that you command the wind-doctor not to press his hand with the scarification, so that he does not cut the artery and cause a flow of blood.
Ventosation under the chin benefits sores in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar to them from illnesses that are in the mouth: and it stands in place of the phlebotomy of alieherich, which are on the two lips.
Ventosation of the shoulder blades benefits alchafacham, which is produced by inflammation and heat.
Ventosation of the inner parts of the two armpits benefits the relief of phlebotomy of the three veins, Basilica, and Fusca, and Cephalica; because it draws blood from all those small veins that are in the flesh; and those small veins draw from other larger veins than them, until the drawing reaches the three veins.
And it is necessary that you command the wind-doctor not to proceed too much in the scarification, because the place is deprived of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
A cupping that is applied over the alchausos benefits hemorrhoids of the anus and lower ulcers.
And it is necessary that you command the cupper to use a large, air-filled cupping, because the area needs strong suction, and perhaps the small cupping devices will break; and let it be scarified with a large scarification.
Cupping of the thighs reduces swelling noticeably, because they draw blood from the entire body, and they relieve long-standing pain in the kidneys, and the womb, and the bladder; they induce menstruation, and they alleviate bother and discomfort; and they take the place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins and alhorcobeni.
However, they weaken the body greatly, and cause fainting in many people.
And the cupping of alhorcobeni is nearly as helpful as the cupping of both thighs.
The quality of applying cupping is to place the cupping initially empty, and to draw with moderate suction; and do not prolong the application of the cupping, but place them quickly, and remove them quickly, so that the humors may come to the area with equal arrival; and do not cease to repeat this and continue until you see the area already reddening and swelling, and the redness of blood appears; then scarify, and repeat the suction gradually. Then consider the disposition of the bodies.
For those people of tender flesh, with rare pores, it is necessary to scarify them once, no more, so that the area does not become ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to enlarge the scarification a little, and deepen it slightly; and to moderate the suction with ease, speed, and gentle movement.
If there is thickness in the blood, then it is necessary to scarify him twice. In the first instance, a pathway is opened for the thinning and liquefaction of the blood; and in the second, for the meticulous examination of the extraction of thick blood.
If the blood is very foul, then let the scarification be done a third time, so that the final result may be achieved.
And finally, when we wish to extract blood gradually, or a little at a time, one scarification suffices for us. If we wish for a large extraction of blood, we scarify many times.
And if we measure that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that it be scarified with deep incisions; and the equal measure in scarification is the depth of the skin.
What is it that must be administered from oils at the application of cupping glasses, and what should the cupper beware of?
In that person whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are tight, it is necessary to anoint the cupping locations with oils that are opening, soothing, and dissolving: if it is in the summer, like the oil of alkekengi, or bruised oil, or oil of sweet almonds, or oil of pumpkin seed.
And if it is in the winter, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or camomile oil, or savin oil; and similar to them.
If however the excess is thick, cold: then the oil should be amaranth oil, or balsam oil, or white oil, or dill oil; and similar to them.
If however the cupper is of wide pores, tender flesh: then it is necessary that he abstain from oil, and that these wash the cupping locations after cupping with rosewater, or with cold water, or with soothing water, or with pumpkin water, or with purslane water; and similar to them.
That person whose blood is of much moisture, should wash the cuppings with vinegar, or with myrtle water, and sumac; and similar to them.
However, whoever has thick excesses, should wash their cuppings with old wine, or with samsuca water, or with dill decoction, or chamomile; and similar to them.
And it is necessary that cupping be avoided in a bath, or in the vicinity of a bath; indeed, it is necessary that it be administered after exiting the bath by an hour, or by two hours.
And it is not appropriate for someone to sleep after cupping.
What is it by which the cupping, or bloodletting, should be governed before cupping?
And after it, it is necessary, first of all, to consider: then if the cupper or bloodletter is choleric, and sharpness and inflammation prevail over his blood, then it is necessary that he receive cooling things, such as pomegranate juice, and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and secaniabin, and iuleb, and similar to them.
And place his food chickens, and the meats of sheep sekebiget, and foods seasoned with agrimony, and similar to them.
And whose complexion is cold, it is necessary that he take in drink syrup of honey, or of almiua, or alsecaniabin made from seeds; and let him take moderate fragrant wine, which is between old and new; and let him be instructed to use little food. And place his food chickens, and canebir, and sparrows, that is, small birds, and doves' young.
And it is necessary that the drink on the day of cupping and bloodletting be more than the food; and it is necessary that there be given in the drink to some people at the hours tyriaca alfaroch, or medicine of musk, that is, muscat, or alesselithe before cupping; and before bloodletting, or after: so that the principal limbs may be strengthened, and the blood refined. And it is not necessary that they take it in heated drink.
Concerning cupping without scarification. However, cupping without scarification is cupping that is placed on the liver, and spleen, and gout, and on the stomach, navel, and kidney areas, and the pouch of the loins, because these limbs do not tolerate scarification upon themselves.
And they are not sought after unless there is either an attachment of blood from a limb to a limb; just as we place a cupping vessel on the breasts in a sickness of nasal blood flow. And we use them to release cold, viscous airiness from a limb; such as placing a cupping vessel on the stomach and navel. For it itself thins the limb and warms it, and removes the pain because it has resolved that airiness.
It is also placed on the kidneys, when obstruction or stone occurs in them. For by its power of attraction perhaps it opens the obstruction or extracts the stone from its place.
And similarly it does when placed on the liver and spleen, when there is viscous airiness to be treated in either of them.
And these cupping glasses are administered empty with only incision. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of lukewarm water in diseases of pleurisy: and that is, so that the cupping glass be filled, and be large, with just warm water, or water in which herbs suitable to it have been boiled.
Form of cupping glass administered with fire.
Let its opening be the width of two open fingers, according to what we have shaped it: and its depth be half a palm: and let there be a small hole in its side, approximately in the middle of it, to accommodate a needle: let it be made from bronze, or brass, with thick, smooth, equal, polished edges, so that it does not injure the limb at its placement upon it: and let there be a crossbar of bronze or iron in its middle, where the candle with the fire is placed.
And now this cupping glass is made large, larger than the one we have described, and somewhat smaller than that, according to the diseases and age of the one who uses it.
For the cupping glasses of children, and of those who are thin, are different from the cupping glasses of men and those with thick bodies.
The method of placing this cupping glass with fire upon the limb is this: light a wick with fire from linen properly, or a small candle of wax, and place it over the middle of the crossbar that is in the middle of the cupping glass; so that the fire is burning approximately at the lower part of the cupping glass, so that the sick person's body is not burned.
Then it should be placed on the limb, and a finger should be placed over the hole that I mentioned, until when we hold the cupping glass; while we need it, let us remove the finger, and vapor will come out through that hole, and the cupping glass will be released immediately.
Then light the wick according to its manner, and repeat it, if you need it.
However, in a cupping glass administered in the illness of pleurisy with water, there is no crossbar, nor hole; and it is not administered unless it is filled with water, and is placed upon the limb only.
And this is its form.
And this cupping glass, the larger it is, so that it can hold much water, is better.
Concerning the suspension of Bloodsuckers.
Bloodsuckers are not applied in numerous places, except in those limbs where the application of cupping is not possible due to their small size, such as the lip, and the gums, and similar parts. Or on a limb stripped of flesh, such as the finger and the nose, and similar parts.
And the quality of using them is: that you obtain bloodsuckers that are in fresh, clean waters, free from putrefaction.
Then they are to be left in fresh water by day and by night, until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their belly. Then the body is to be emptied first by bloodletting and by cupping. Then wash the limb of the sick person until it turns red. Then place them upon it.
When therefore they are filled, and fall, and suction of the place is possible with the cupping vessel, then it is more useful in the treatment; and if not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then with much water, and it is rubbed, and squeezed out.
If the flow of blood after the fall of the Sanguisugae is prolonged, and it is oozing: then immerse a linen cloth in cold water, and place it above, until the oozing is stopped.
If the blood is multiplied, then pulverize on it ground zegi, or gallnuts, and similar astringents, until the bleeding is stopped.
Or place the halves of shelled beans on the area: and leave them, until the beans adhere to the place, for the blood is stopped.
And if the use of leeches is necessary, when possible, change them.
If however the leeches are prevented from being suspended, then the area is to be wiped with fresh blood. Or fix a needle in the areas, until something comes out of the blood. Then place them, for when they sense something of the blood, they immediately adhere.
Therefore, when you want them to fall off, sprinkle something of crushed aloe, or salt, or ash on them, and they will fall off immediately.
This chapter is also very necessary in the art of medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations of accidents in bones.
Know, O children, that the foolish among physicians and the common people boast about this chapter, and those who have never examined the book of the ancients, nor read a letter from it.
And for this reason, this method is private among the sciences in our land. And I never encountered any good in it at all, and I did not obtain what I have obtained from it unless because of the length of reading the ancient books, and the study on the understanding of them, until I extracted the knowledge of it from them.
Then I adhered to experimentation and study for the length of my life.
And now I have described to you from it in this chapter all that my knowledge contains: and upon which my experience passed, after I abbreviated it for you, and freed it from the occupation of elongation, and abbreviated it by the end of abbreviation, and showed it by the end of declination: and I formed in it many forms from the forms of instruments that are administered in it, since it is from the addition of declination, as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter II.
It is necessary, as we say in the prologue of this chapter, to summarize the discourse and chapters, the understanding of which the craftsman is first required to consider regarding their truth: so that you, and those who are eager, may know, or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when a fracture, or dislocation, or twisting, or fall, occurs to someone, then it is necessary to hasten first to bleed him, and to loosen him, or both at the same time: if nothing prevents it, such as weakness of strength; or if the one to whom something has happened from among those is a child or a decrepit old man; or if it is a time of great heat or great cold.
Then let him be content in his food, with cold vegetables, and the flesh of birds and kids; and prohibit wine, and fatty meats, and overfilling from foods and all food that fills the veins with blood. Until when you are certain about the hot abscess and do not fear the effusion of matter to the limb: then let the sick man return to his first regimen with which his custom ran.
When, therefore, the fractured bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the sick person be fed with nutritious foods, with much, thick, heavy nourishment, in which there is viscosity: such as foods made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish: and thick wine, and similar to them. For with this regimen the coagulation of the fracture happens more quickly and better.
And know that fractured bones, when they are in men who are in early old age and decrepit, are not possible to be joined and consolidated according to their nature always, because of the drying out of their limbs; and those from them are consolidated and joined in the end of their softness, like the bones of small children.
However, nature connects something similar to glue, in which there is thickness, to the fractured bone from every side, to which it adheres and binds it, until they are glued together and bound together, until it reaches the end of strength and firmness, as it was at first, until nothing prevents it from its functions; and for this reason it is necessary that you provide the sick person with foods that have heaviness, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of the limbs; because a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the head bone; and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the spine; and likewise all the other limbs are different from each other; and I have come with a recollection of every type of fracture, displayed in its own chapter, distinct from another. And the types of bone fractures are diversified even within themselves.
Because a fracture of it can be curved, besides which splinters occur in it. And sometimes its fracture is along the length of the bone; and its fractures are splintery, that is, splinters and proceeding additions, like those in a reed when a knife is fitted to it for writing. And not proceeding; and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption at the head; and there is a fracture with a small fissure.
And every type has an appropriate nature, or instrument, in its restoration, according to which its recollection will come, distinct in its locations.
And from that by which the fracture of the bone is known, is its twisting and its prominence; and its appearance to the senses, and its sound when you compress it with your hand.
Therefore, when there is no apparent twisting in place, nor sound upon your touch when you touch the bone with agitation, nor does the infirm person find great pain there, then there is no fracture there, rather it is possible that there is a twist or a small fissure; therefore you should not move it with your hand and compression entirely, but rather place upon it from the medicines, of which its recollection comes, after that moment which is suitable to the place; then a subtle bandage should be applied.
And know that when a place is bowed and split into two, besides the struggles that happen in it, nevertheless, every part is removed from its counterpart; then it is necessary for you to begin at your hour for its rectification, equalizing it before a hot abscess happens to it.
If however a hot abscess happens to it, then leave it for a few days, until the abscess rests. Then equalize it according to whatever method is possible from gentleness and skill.
And know that its restoration and equalization are easier than that of a bone in which struggles occur.
Then bind it, according to how its memory will come to you.
If however there are struggles in the bone, then necessary is the extension of this fractured limb from two parts, if it is a hand or a foot: or with your hand, if the limb is small: or with two ropes, or with a rope and a hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb over an equal place, according to its natural form, until when the parts of the fractured limb are extended: then strive to restore those additions to their places with all your skill, and according to any method you can: and be careful according to your strength, lest pain or injury befall the weak through your operation: and strive according to your strength to apply one of the two bones with its partner, according to the best forms.
And it is necessary at that time to try and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see there anything different, correct it, and equalize it according to the measure of your ability.
And beware of violent extension and strong compression, as many fools do. For often they cause a hot abscess or contraction in the limb through that operation of theirs, as we have already seen it from their operations many times.
Then bind after the equalization and proper operation, and the tightening of that limb, and rest, and tranquility; and admonish the sick person about it. And make him beware lest he move it in the hours of his waking and sleeping, at his turning and stirring, and at his excretion, and all his movements with his utmost power. And that he beware lest the position of the limb be one that will be followed by pain. For when he feels pain or injury in the disposition of the limb's position, then it is necessary to change it to another disposition in which he does not feel pain.
And be careful with him that his position be thus equal, straight, so that tortuosity does not befall the limb when it is restored.
And the quality of the fracture of the limb is, according to what I narrate.
Know that broken limbs are diversified in their smallness, and their size, and their shape.
Therefore, those of them that are small, like an arm, and a finger, and a toe, and similar to them, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of soft, fine cloth. But those of them that are large, like the thigh and the leg, it is necessary that there be a wide, hard wrapping on them.
Because a wide ligament connects a large limb and binds it from every side with equal constriction, so that no blemish enters it.
Then, after your evacuation, begin to apply a suitable liniment to it, on soft linen over the place of its fracture. Then begin to wrap the bandage over the place of its fracture three or four times, according to what the limb deserves; and hold your hand for a while on the bandage.
Then proceed with it to the upper part from the place of the fracture, and bind less tightly than your constriction with which you bound the broken place. Then move away the bandage from the fracture a little, and loosen the constriction gradually, until you take hold of a large part of the sound place.
Then take another strip, and wrap it again over the broken place in turns. Then proceed with the bandage to the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation on the tightening of the bandage, and its loosening, be according to what we said in the first upper bandage.
Then place between the bandages soft linen, or cloth, with which the twist of the fracture is equalized, if there is twisting in it: and if not, do not place anything in it.
Then wrap another strip over it. Afterwards, place over these bandages properly made stars immediately, if there is no swelling or hot abscess in the place.
If however there is swelling or abscess in it: then place over it that which will settle that abscess and remove the swelling; and leave it for several days. Then tie over it the stars.
And let the stars be from the halves of broad reeds, shaved, cleverly shaped; or let them be from the wood from which sieves are made, which are made from pine, or gerard, that is, palm branches, or calingi; and let the breadth of each star be according to this form and this same figure.
However, it is necessary that the star placed over the wound itself be a little thicker and wider than the other stars; and the length of the star should be according to the size and smallness of the limb.
Then tie over the stars with another bandage, according to the same first binding. Then tie over it carefully with threads, according to what we said about the binding; and it is that your binding should be tighter over the place of the fracture; and the further it is from the fracture, the looser the binding.
And it is necessary that the middle threads be of medium thickness and fineness, and be of soft flax.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the operation of foolish men who place their threads from twisted flax cords, it is a great error, since the binding falls outside of its proper quality with them.
And again, thin threads do not suit, for you will not achieve with them through the binding that which you wish.
And it is not necessary that there be less than a finger between the splint and the splint.
If the sick person is injured by the ends of the splints after the binding in healthy places, then place under them soft wool, or dyed wool, until nothing is injured from it.
However, when there is a fracture with a wound, and a break occurs in the skin, I will come with a recollection of it in the second chapter, in accordance with its particularity.
And know that not every broken limb needs to be bound with splints from the first day. That is because, when a limb is large, you should not place splints upon it unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your safety from the event of a hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters, by which the ancients restored those which are placed upon a fracture and dislocation.
A method of a common abbreviated plaster, restoring a fracture, suitable for many complexions, especially women and children, since it is from it that neither heat nor cold prevails.
And it is, that you take from the mill's dust, and it is the flour-meal that hangs on the mill's walls at the grinding of the millstone: and you make it as it is, except that you sieve it with egg-white: and place its preparation not thick, nor thin. Then use it.
A different method of plaster, restoring fracture, dislocation, and torsion. Take aloes, and laudanum, and acacia, and enula, and mugath; and juice of all, ana 10 drams; myrrh, and aloes, both ana 5 drams; and alethil 20 drams.  Grind all together, and sieve them, and mix with alethil water; or with egg white, if the patient's complexion is heated. Then use this plaster. For it is a good composition, restoring broken bones quickly, and benefiting many people.
Another method of plaster, which also benefits fracture and torsion. Take mugath, and mess, and white mallow, of all ana 10 drams; myrrh, and aloes, both ana 5 drams; acacia 6 drams; Armenian bole 20 drams. Grind all together with much trituration, and sieve them, and make into a poultice with water, or egg white: and administer.
A method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places; and it relieves pain that occurs to them, and bone fissure, and fracture. Take wool, and soak it in vinegar and decocted oil, and place it over the affected area.
And in this plaster there is no power of restoration, but it is good in quieting a hot abscess; and it expels pains properly.
A method of plaster that restores a broken bone. Take leaves of the fig tree, and leaves of wild poppy: and grind them all, and make a plaster from both.
A method of a shortened plaster, administered in the restoration of a fractured bone; and when you want resolution of the remaining abscess. Take root of marsh mallow, and camomile, and violet flowers, and herb flour, of each one part: let all be ground, and be made into a paste with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
If however it is inflamed, then make it with water of moist coriander, or with water, and use it.
A method of another stronger plaster for resolution, than this first one, when a hard abscess occurs in the restoration of a limb. Take root of marsh mallow, and flax seed, and pomegranate seeds, and melilot, and marsh mallow, and violet flowers, and camomile, of each one part; let all be ground, and be made into a paste with willow water, or with fresh water, or with wine. All of that according to the warmth of the limb and the rest of its heat.
Hippocrates however did not say in his book that a plaster should be placed on a fractured limb during its restoration, except for one made from wax and oil: nothing else; and he related that it should be intermediate in thickness and fineness.
Galen, however, believed that things which dry out heat should be placed on a fractured limb during its restoration, such as myrrh, and aloes and frankincense, and similar ones to those we have mentioned.
Method of the plaster that helps against injury and pain. Take equal parts of barley, chickpeas, cut human hair (or bird feathers), marshmallow root, and salt: grind them, sieve them, and mix them: and make a plaster from it.
However, the amount of time that the bandage should remain on, and then be removed, is that you should then consider if the patient experiences no pain, nor itching, nor if the fractured bone is moved from its place; then do not remove it for many days. But if intense itching or disturbing pain or swelling occurs at the location, then quickly remove it in an hour, and take the plaster off: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, wet: and immerse it in lukewarm water, and wash the place with it, until the itching subsides, and the pain is calmed.
But if intense itching or disturbing pain or swelling occurs at the location, then quickly remove it in an hour, and take the plaster off: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, wet: and immerse it in lukewarm water, and wash the place with it, until the itching subsides, and the pain is calmed. Then leave the limb at rest for one hour. Then place upon it shredded wool, soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil, and bind it over it for one night, until you are sure of being free from a hot abscess, and the swelling of the limb is calmed, and its pain is removed.
Then apply it again to a fine bandage and a small plaster; and do not tighten it with your initial constriction, and use fine bandages with it, until it is healed.
If you see that the abscess, redness, pain, swelling, and all the accompanying conditions have been completely removed, and you no longer need a plaster and bandage, then apply it again as you did at first, evenly.
If none of the things we have mentioned happen to the limb, then do not loosen it, unless after three, or four, or five, or seven days. And when twenty days have passed, as it appears to you from the limb's loosening, as we said, until when the fracture adheres and the flesh's coagulation approximates over it: then also add more to your initial bandage, and add also to the thickening of the weak food, as its prior application recalled.
If, however, you see the fracture site already drying out and wasting away more than it should, then know that nourishment is being prevented from reaching it. Therefore, anoint it with warm water every time you loosen it, and let it be so for all three days, and slightly ease the bandage.
By this operation, then, nourishment flows to the limb, and it is healed quickly.
However, what foolish restorers do with a fractured limb a second time, when it is not restored at first as it should be, and is restored in a crooked fashion, is an error of their operation and a great fear.  And if it were correct, the ancients would have said so in their books, and would have operated with it.
And I have seen no trace of it anywhere at all, and it is better that it not be done.
Concerning the accidental fracture of the head.
The kinds of fracture are diverse, and its shapes are many: and its causes are distinct.
For there is a fracture that occurs from striking with a sword: and the blow removes the bone completely until it reaches the siphac, which is beneath the bone, just as an axe does in wood: and because of this, this type of fracture is called Axial.
Or the sword cuts a part of the bone, and cuts its surface, and the incision does not penetrate to its utmost extent: and this type of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, Lifting absolutely.
And there is a wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture, which is a crushing, or attrition: and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall on a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which is now approaching the tissue that is beneath the bone, or it is on the surface of the bone; and the wound of these two fractures is also either small or large.
And there is a fracture, which is hidden in the bone in the subtlety of a hair: and it is a small fissure, and because of that this type of fracture is called Capillaris.
And from those is a fracture, which occurs from a fall, or a blow of a stone, and things similar to them, which cause the lamina of the bone to enter the interior, and a hollow space is made; as happens to bronze kettles, when a blow strikes them, and part of them enters the interior.
And this happens most often in the heads of moist bones, such as the heads of children.
And all these types of fractures are made separately, and I will come with a recollection of that entire thing in its place.
And we recognize all these types of fractures by probing over them, and investigating them with probes, and removing the putrefied flesh that is over them.
However, the capillary species is recognized by this, that it is uncovered above the bone, and it is wiped and anointed on it with ink: for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, first consider the patient's symptoms: then, if you see from the symptoms that it signifies clearly an impending danger, such as vomiting, cholera, extension, loss of intellect, loss of voice, fainting, acute fever, protrusion of the eyes, redness of the eyes, and similar symptoms in various conditions, undoubtedly it will not be healed. And if you see symptoms not alarming you, and you hope for recovery, then begin its treatment.
If a wounded person comes to you immediately after being wounded, and it is in the days of winter, then it is necessary that you strive in the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all conditions.
And if it is in the days of summer, then it is necessary that you hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before that which is under the bone from the membrane is corrupted, and those accidents occur that we have described.
If the fracture of the bone has now reached the membrane touching the brain, and is with fragments and crushing, then it is necessary that you cut away the broken and crushed part, as I describe to you.
And it is necessary that you shave the head of the wounded person, and uncover the bone in whatever way is possible for you, and according to the shape of the wound and what is mild on the sick person.
If, during the uncovering of the bone, a flow of blood or a hot abscess occurs, then apply to it what is appropriate. And it is necessary that you fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil, and make the wound rest until the abscess subsides and you are secure from the flow of blood. Then begin the treatment of the bone and its removal.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
One method is to cut the bone with a scalpel of a sharp point; and this is its form.
Then apply another scalpel, slightly wider than the first, and this is its form.
Then apply yet another scalpel, wider still than the second.
And the second method is that you should have a number of different scalpels, some wider than others, and some shorter than others; and let their edges be sharp, and let them be made of iron, or steel; and use ease in striking upon the scalpel so that it does not strike the head and injure it.
If the bone is strong, hard: then it is necessary that you pierce around it, before you apply the scalpels, with drills, which are called non-penetrating drills, and are not so called except because they do not pass through the limit of the bony flesh to that which is behind it; because a drill has a round end on the part below its sharp head, like a margin, and a small circle prevents sinking, and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is suitable for you to take from these drills a multitude, each appropriate to the thickness of the bone, until you have a drill for every skull; whose sharp end's size in length and shortness should be in proportion to the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three kinds of drills, or trepans.
The method of piercing around the fractured bone is: that you place the drill on the bone, and revolve it with your fingers, until you know that the bone has been pierced. Then change the drill to another place, and place what is between every hole the thickness of a radius, or approximately that.
Then cut with the chisels what is between every hole from the bone; and make it thinner than you can above it from the end of lightness, as we said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with some other instruments that you have prepared for it: such as pliers and fine forceps.
And it is necessary that you guard against every precaution, so that the drill, or chisel, touches nothing of the flesh.
When therefore you have encircled the bone by piercing, and it has been separated from the flesh, if it has been continuous with it, and you have pulled it out, it is necessary that you scrape and make even the extremity of that which remains in the bone with another similar instrument to the chisel. However, it ought to be thinner and finer than other chisels.
If however anything of small bones, and splinters, remains, then take them away with ease, with what is with you of instruments. Then treat the wound after that with the bandages and ointments that we have said.
And another method of operation is easy, completely far from fear, which Galen said, and praised it with great praise: and this is his statement. It is necessary for you first to begin to uncover the part of the bone from the place where the fracture is more severe and more obvious until when you have uncovered that part and have placed under it the extremity of this lenticular chisel, which has this form.
And the lenticular part, smooth, does not cut anything: and its sharp part, running in length on its two sides, as you see: so that the lenticular part rests against the siphac, its sharp face resting on the bone.
Then strike the chisel from one side with a small mallet, until you cut the entire bone with ease, as it turns; and you be secure from the cloth, that no accident happen to it, which you fear entirely.
And if the surgeon were not more foolish than men, and more ignorant of it, and were not asleep, then if anything remained attached to the bone from the cloth in some places, he would cut it from it with the extremity of the lenticular chisel itself, and free it from it with ease. It is freed from it without harm, and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the cloth, and that which is cut away from the bone is its surface, and there remain on it roughness and subtle irregularities, then it is necessary to scrape away that roughness, and eradicate those irregularities with the fine razors of which you have prepared a number of different sizes, so that it may be possible for you to administer from them whatever is more appropriate and straighter in every place, according to that to which the operation leads you, and the fractured bone, and its shape. And it is necessary that you administer in your reasoning, with which in the first place you scrape the bone, more widely those razors. Then use after it a finer one.
And it is necessary that you administer in your reasoning, with which in the first place you scrape the bone, more widely those razors. Then use after it a finer one. And do not cease to do this according to continuity, until you arrive at administering the thinnest and finest of them all.
In every one of the remaining small fissures, and subtle fracture, it is necessary to administer care, according to that which leads to its rectification.
And it is a matter not hidden over every one in this art having any study, and stood upon that which we have written and shown in great fractures.
If, however, the flap is retained when you excavate the bone, then it is necessary to take a linen cloth, according to the quantity of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the wound opening. Then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth.  And do this as easily as you can over it, so that it is not weighed down by this.
Then apply a wide bandage over it; and do not tighten it, unless by a quantity which holds the wound only.  And leave it with this treatment for one day or two days, until you are secure from a hot abscess. Then loosen it, and apply after it medicines with which superfluous dryness is removed: such as lily roots, and herb flour, and frankincense powder, and aristolochia, and similar to them; and it is that you should make a powder from these medicines, which is powdered over the wound as if dry.
And universally apply to it any medicine of whose property it is that it cleanses and does not bite. And take care, according to your skill, that the wound in your treatment is clean: neither be there filth of ointment, nor any dirt.
And take care, according to your skill, that the wound in your treatment is clean: neither be there filth of ointment, nor any dirt. Nor allow the virus to gather on it at all: for when the virus gathers on the brain membrane, it corrupts it and rots it, and great harm comes upon the sick person afterward; and this happens in the heads cavities, when the bone is cleansed from it, especially when its care is neglected, a blackness in its surface.
Therefore, if the sick person experiences the ailments we have described, know that it is undoubtedly harmful.
And if blackness does not occur, except from the medicine placed upon it, and it is in the power of that medicine to cause that blackness: then it is necessary that you take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and linen with them. Then place it over the cavity: afterward, treat it with the kinds of remedies that are suitable until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the Nose when it is broken.
Know that only the two upper parts of the Nose are broken simultaneously, or one of them: because they are two bones; since its lower part is cartilaginous, it does not break, and it is not afflicted with anything except abrasion, and twisting, and crookedness.
If one of the two parts is broken, then it is necessary to insert the little finger into the nostril and straighten the fracture with your index finger and thumb on the outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape: and do this artfully, with ease. And beware that your operation upon the afflicted does not cause pain.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it is necessary to straighten it with the end of a rod, of which the thickness is small.
If, however, the fracture is in two parts, then do likewise: and hasten its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, by means of rest of a hot abscess.
Then insert into the end of the nose a bandage made of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part; or insert two bandages if the fracture is in two parts; and let the bandage have a thickness of such a quantity as to fill the nostril.
And some of the ancient restorers said that the bandages should be soaked in butter, and changed every day. However, that does not seem to me to be so; rather, the bandage ought to be soaked in the white of an egg, mixed with mill dust. Then leave the bandage until the bone is strengthened and the cartilage is hardened.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nose in place of a bandage, after a soft cloth has been wrapped around it; and this holds the nasal fracture more firmly, and so that the weak person is not prevented from breathing.
And this is not necessary. If you wish, do it; and if you wish, make bandages.
If, while treating a hot abscess, the nose happens to be injured again, plaster the nose with caeroto, or with cot infused in rose oil, or with a small amount of diaquilum.
If, however, a hot abscess does not occur, then it is necessary that you plaster it with sesame flour and powdered frankincense mixed with egg white. Then place soft lint over it, and do not bind the nose with anything at all.
If, however, the bones of the nose are fractured or broken into small pieces, then it is necessary to cut over them, and remove them with instruments appropriate to them. Then sew up the wound, and treat it with that which heals and consolidates it with appropriate plasters or ointments.
If a wound occurs between the nostrils, then it is necessary to treat it with bandages, and administer a lead cannula, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the lower jaw, when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken, and its fracture is not accompanied by a wound, then examine if its fracture is only external, and it is not broken in two, and curves inward: then its recognition is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on its right side, you should insert the index finger of your left hand into the patient's mouth; similarly, if the fracture is on the left side of the jaw, then insert the index finger of your right hand; and with it push the curvature of the fracture from inside outward with your other hand from the inside of the bone properly, and with it straighten it.
If the jaw fracture is already separated into two parts, then it is necessary to administer an external force from both sides, according to the straightness, until its alignment is possible.
If however, there is already movement or breakage in the teeth, then tie those which you hope will remain with gold, silver, or silk thread. Then place a poultice on the fractured jaw. Then place a doubled cloth over it, and place a large properly made bandage, or a piece of leather sole of equal length to the jaw, over the cloth.
Then tie it above, according to how its bandage is prepared for it and its application fits, until it is not destroyed; and instruct the sick person to use tranquility and quiet, and give him/her soft drinks as food.
If, however, you estimate that something of its form has already been altered in some way, then hasten and loosen it on the third day. Then straighten what has been altered, and plaster it with mill dust, with egg white, or with sesame flour, after you have removed the poultice; and place soft wool over the plaster.
While, therefore, that plaster remains attached to it, and the bone's position is not altered, leave it, so that you do not loosen it, until it is healed; or bind the fracture. For this fracture is often bound for three weeks.
If, meanwhile, a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times for its reduction, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider it. For if a fragment has already separated from the bone, or from the surrounding tissue, then be delicate in this, so that you remove those fragments of the surrounding tissue with what is suitable to you in removing them from the instruments.
If truly the opening of the wound is constricted, then widen it with a phlebotom knife according to your necessary amount.
Then, when you have removed that surrounding tissue, and nothing remains of it, suture then the opening of the wound, if it is wide; and if not, place over it from plasters that are suitable for it, and cause it to heal, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the [surgical] fork, when it breaks.
Indeed, the surgical fork breaks most often at the anterior humeral portion, and its fracture occurs according to one of three ways.
For it either breaks and separates into two, apart from what happens to the surrounding tissue: and it is easier to restore it.
Or so, that there are injuries to the bone: and it is more difficult to restore it.
Or so, that there is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation for it when the fracture is without a wound is that you should have two attendants present, so that one of them holds the support which follows the broken fork, and the other extends the neck towards the part.
Then adjust the fracture with your fingers, until its shape is as it should be: and there is neither a prominence nor a concavity in it.
If you need more extension, then it is necessary to place under the patient's armpit a ball made of cloth or wool: and its size should be according to your need: and extend and raise the fork, and compress the ball with your hand, until you make the fracture straight, as it should be.
If you cannot pull the end of the fork outward, because it has gone deep, then it is necessary that the patient lie on their back, and place a pillow of medium size under their shoulder, and let an attendant press the patient's shoulder downward, until the bone of the fork that is deep is lifted upward; and then straighten the fracture, and make it even with your fingers.
If, however, you feel a splinter from the fork, and it moves, then it is necessary to cut above it, and extract the splinter with ease.
If, however, it is held in the bone, then make an incision with one of the incisions you have prepared for it, after you have placed an instrument that holds a sifach under the fork; and this instrument is of wood or iron. And this is its form.
It resembles a spoon that has no concavity.
And let its width be according to what you need of it in terms of the size of the bone, and its smallness. Its length, however, also according to what is possible for you for the operation.
And it should have two ends, as you see: and one end should be wide, and the other narrow.
If however the rupture of the wound that you made when you extracted the splinter of bone is wide, and you are certain of avoiding a hot abscess: then bring together the two edges of the wound with sutures.
And if the rupture is small, or you fear a hot abscess: then fill the wound with cloths and bandages according to the size of the wound's tear.
If a hot abscess should occur, then soak a cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and place it upon it.
A constriction of the bone, however, when it is without a wound and without incision, is to apply a plaster made from mill flour with egg white to the bone. And place soft lint over it. Then place a sphere under its projection, if you need it. Then take a himematem, that is, a wheel which the Saracens hold, which is very long, and its width is one palm, or approximately that. Then place a doubled pad over the lint and plaster. Then take a sliver from a thin board, whose width is three fingers, and similarly in length. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the sliver in the place of the alhimemati that falls over the fracture and the place. Then place the alhimemati over the fracture as it revolves, and wrap it around its neck, and under its healthy and under its infirm titillico: and reduce it over the entire part, and as you see that the constriction holds the fracture with decent constriction: and that is it, which is not hidden from you, and all of it, so that the sliver is not removed from the fractured bone. And for this it is necessary that you observe the ill person every day. Therefore, whenever the ligament softens, and you see the sliver already being removed, then straighten it and tighten the ligament.
Then place the patient's head on his back, and place a small pillow under his head at night, so that with it his support may be raised from his side. Therefore, the fracture of the collarbone is raised by raising the shoulder.
Or tie his arm to his neck; and do not loosen the bandage if it does not happen in a place where it occurs from itching or an abscess, up to twelve days.
Then renew the plaster if you see his face towards it. And add to the constriction, and leave him until it is restored and congealed.
And indeed a fracture of the collarbone is bound and strengthened, in most cases, within twenty-eight days; and sometimes in certain people it occurs within a shorter time.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the shoulder blade.
The shoulder blade is rarely fractured, and at a wide place on it; and only its edges are broken from it.
Therefore, when a part of it is fractured, and it is fractured in its middle, and this is not recognized except by touch, then according to the shape of the fracture, attempt its preparation. And restore it to its natural shape by every means of ingenuity that is possible to you.
Then place on the area ground flour with egg white, and soft linen, and place a cushion made of doubled cloth on top. Then place over it a wide splint of thin wood, according to the size of the entire shoulder blade, or a little larger.
If, however, there is a hollow under the splint in any part of the shoulder blade, then fill that hollow with soft linen until the splint rests evenly.
Then bind it tightly with a long bandage, with fitting constriction, and firmness of the splint, until it is not removed from its place.
And consider the bandage every day. Whenever, therefore, the bandage softens, tighten it, and adjust the splint if it is removed from its place: and let the sick person lie on his healthy side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When, therefore, this number is completed, then loosen the bandage: and be you in safety. For it is from bones whose joining is not feared, nor their separation.
If, however, a fragment of bone protrudes and pierces under the skin, then split it over it and remove it. And do what we said in the fracture of the fork from the subsidence of a hot abscess, if anything happens from it.
On the restoration of a fractured breast.
The breast sometimes breaks in the middle of itself, and that rarely happens. Its extremities, however, are broken and crushed very often due to its accidents.
When its middle breaks, inclining downwards, it is accompanied by intense pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing; and perhaps vomiting blood, and a hollow in the fractured bone is revealed, and not hidden from sight.  Its restoration is that the sick person be laid supine on their back, and a pillow be placed between their shoulder blades.
Then press on their shoulders, and gather the ribs from both sides with your hands, and make them even, in every way that is possible and suitable, until the shape of the bone returns to as it should be.
Then place a plaster and linen bandage over it, and put a thin wooden slat, or a splint, or something similar in lightness, over that, after you have wrapped it in cloth. Then carefully bind it over the broken bone so that it does not move: and go around the back with the bandage several times, and tighten it properly.
Then check the bandage every hour, and whenever it loosens, tighten it. And if its need prompts its loosening due to itching or corrosion occurring in the area, or pain, or an abscess: then hurry, and untie it, and remove the plaster, and correct what has happened with the method of its treatment.
Then replace the plaster, if you see it fitting, and apply a bandage to it until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of ribs, when they are broken.
Know that a rib fracture does not occur except in the thick places which follow the back, and from their extremities on the anterior side. And attrition does not happen to them unless because they are cartilaginous. And its knowledge is not hidden from the senses when examined with the fingers.
And its restoration is done thus, that one aligns the fracture with the fingers to the extent possible, until the shape is made equal, as is proper. Then apply plasters, and bind the fractured bone with splints, if it needs it.
And if the rib fracture is sloping inwards, then intense pain and a stabbing sensation occur to the patient, like the stabbing sensation that occurs to one who has pleurisy, because the bone pierces the membrane, and also difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting of much blood occur, and this is a difficult condition to treat.
And the ancients were ingenious in this, with many methods, and among them are those who say that one should give the patient foods which produce inflammation and windiness, so that the abdomen is inflamed and distended, and the fracture is driven outwards.
We, however, abhor this, lest it become a swift cause for producing a hot abscess, if it does not already exist. If however it has already occurred, then add to it.
And some of them said: let a wind-inducing substance be placed upon the affected area, then suction should be applied with force, and this is a similar line of reasoning. However, it is feared that wind-inducing substances will draw superfluous fluids to the area, because of the weakened condition.
And some of them said: the area should be covered with wool that has already been immersed in hot oil; and cushions should be placed between the ribs, until it is filled, so that the bandage is even when you wrap it around the curvature.
Then the sick person should be treated with the treatment for pleurisy, through food and medicine.
If a strong substance, which the patient cannot tolerate, constricts the patient, and the mouth is pierced or injured by a sharp object, and we fear for the patient, then it is necessary to cut over the area and expose the fractured rib. Then we shall place upon it the instrument which preserves the sinews, the description of which preceded: and we shall cut the bone with ease, and cut it.
Then we shall bring together the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures; and we shall treat it with plasters until it heals.
If, meanwhile, a hot abscess occurs, then hasten, and pour powders in rose oil, and place it upon the area; and let the patient be treated with what is suitable for the abscess.
Meanwhile, let him lie on the side on which he sleeps, until he heals.
On the restoration of a fractured spine of the back and neck.
A fracture of the bones of the neck sometimes occurs, and attrition rarely happens to them; and similarly with the vertebrae of the back. Therefore, when this happens to someone, and you wish to know whether it will heal or not, then consider. For if you see his hands already softened, and numb, and dead; and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor contract them; and when you contract them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then know that it will not heal, and in most cases is mortal.
If he moves them, and feels the contraction with his fingers and the prick, then know that the neck bone is sound, and that the sick person will be healed with treatment.
If a similar condition affects the vertebrae of the back, and you wish to know whether it will heal or not, then consider his feet.  Then, if you see that they are softened, and that the same condition as in the hands occurs in them; and when he lies on his back, wind and waste discharge involuntarily; and when he lies on his stomach, waste discharges involuntarily; and when he lies on his back, and wants to urinate, and cannot, then know that it is fatal. Therefore, do not labor in his treatment.
If, however, nothing of that happens to him, the condition is less severe.
And the treatment for what occurs from that is to try to relieve the hot abscess: so that you place on the vertebra a crushed rose oil alone, or with the yolks of a roasted egg.
Place that on it several times a day until the hot abscess subsides.  Then place one of the strengthening, drying plasters over the area and bind it with a bandage.  And order the sick person to use rest and quiet. And do not let him sleep on the part where he finds pain until it is healed.
If, however, it happens in the rubbing of the bone, a suppuration, or something that has already separated from it: then it is necessary to incise the skin and remove it. Then bring together the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures. Then treat it with some kind of healing ointment until it is healed.
If, however, the last bone of the tail breaks, and it is the end of the tail: then it is necessary to introduce the thumb of your left hand into the anus, and straighten the broken bone with the other hand, as much as possible, and with a second straightening.
Then place it on top of it, and remove it: and treat the wound with the treatment that preceded, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the hip.
Chapter XI.
The bones of the hips rarely break. If they do break, then their fracture is not, unless it is a shattering at their extremities: and they are split lengthwise, and are bent inwards: and pain and a prickling sensation occur in the injured area, and the leg that breaks is numb.
And its restoration indeed is, that you extend your hand over it, until you stand over the fracture in its way.
If the fracture is only at its extremities, then align that fracture, according to what is prepared for you from the alignment, until it resembles its natural shape.
If however the fracture is lengthwise, or has already bent inwards, then make the sick person lie on their stomach, until its fracture's restoration is prepared for you. When therefore you have aligned it, place a plaster over it; then place over it a splint of wood, or of leather; and bind it tightly, so that you need not fear the fracture's shifting, nor the splint's removal.
And align the concavity of them with that which fills it, until the binding takes it according to equality; and command the sick person to sleep on their back, or on their healthy side.
If however a hot abscess occurs, then cease from extending it, and its restoration, until the hot abscess subsides; and place over it that which settles it, according to what went before, then return to its restoration; and bind it as is appropriate.
If however it occurs in the bone of the shin, or it is shattered, extract it.
On the restoration of the fracture of the Adiutor.
The Adiutor is that which is between the elbow and the head of the Scapula.
If therefore it is broken, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: that you should take a piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form, which follows.
And two bandages are tied at the two ends.
Then it is suspended from a raised place, and the patient sits upon a seat. Then the patient should place his broken arm over the wood, until it reaches the point of the wood's curvature, which is in the middle.
Then suspend something heavy over it, so that the attendant extends it downwards. Then the physician should bring the fracture together with his hands, until the fracture returns to its proper alignment.
And another method is that the patient lies on their back, and suspend their arm from their neck with a bandage. Then instruct two attendants, that one of them hold that which is above the fracture with their hands, and that the other hold below it, and that each of them extend it to their side.
And if you wish the extension to be stronger, then bind below the fracture with one bandage, and above it with another bandage, and let each of the two attendants extend them to their own sides.
If therefore the fracture is near the extremity of the humerus, then it is necessary to place one bandage under the armpit, and another under the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary to place a bandage above that place, and above the elbow itself.
Then equalize the fracture with ease without effort, until when the fracture is equalized as it should be, and a good application is applied; then bind it, if a hot abscess does not occur.
If however a hot abscess occurs, then leave the binding until the seventh day. And place over it cut wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until when the abscess rests; then bind it; and the way of binding it is, to place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster. Then support the arm on the aid itself, and place his hand open on his shoulder.
And place cloth, and bind the arm over the aid, so that the arm stands in place as stars: if nothing hinders you from it, nor is anything of the broken bone disturbed upon you.
If you fear that anything of the broken bone may be disturbed upon you, then use splints: and it is that you place upon the fracture itself a splint which is broader and stronger than the other splints. And place between each splint the width of a finger: and the length of the splints should be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on every side.
Then bind over the splints the binding which I said at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your binding should be tighter over the place of the fracture. And the more the fracture is lengthened, the smaller the binding should be.
If therefore you see the positioning of the splints and the binding, as we have said, at the hour in which you restore the limb immediately, then do so; and if you fear a hot abscess, then leave the binding and the splints until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then consider the ligament for all three days, lest itching or swelling occur in the place, or lest food be prevented from reaching the limb, due to the disposition of superfluity of the binding. Therefore rectify it all, according to what we have said.
If however you are confident that nothing will happen from it, then do not loosen the binding unless after three days, or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on his back, and his hands on his stomach; and place under his assistance a mattress full of wool equally.
And consider at every hour of the night or day that the shape of the fractured limb is not loosened or the bandage softened. Therefore, rectify all of that with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we have previously described, so that the food should be subtle at first, until the mouth begins to become strong; then it is necessary to increase the thickness of his food.
For it is the custom of the assistance and leg that it should become strong in forty days; then, therefore, it is necessary that it be loosened, and a bath administered, and it be medicated with ointments which rectify it.
If the fracture is foul, bruised: then do not loosen the bandage and splints from it until fifty days, and until two months.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called two forearms.
One of which is small, and it is the one that follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is the one that is placed below the small one at the lower part.
Perhaps therefore only the larger forearm is broken, or the smaller one, or perhaps they are broken at the same time.
When therefore the smaller forearm is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the forearm is broken below, its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when the two bones are broken at the same time.
If therefore the bone that is broken is the smaller forearm above, then it is appropriate for the physician, in its restoration, to place a small extension with ease, until he equalizes it.
If however the large forearm is that which is broken: then it is necessary that he place a very strong bandage.
And it is necessary that he place the shape of the hand in its restoration, and its extended extension upon a bolster. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers; and let the lower joint be lower than all the fingers, or the rest; and let the sick person be seated upright, and let the bolster be before him, or directly in front of him, at a height so that the sick person does not incur labor.
Then let the attendant extend the arm from the lower part, either with his hand or with a bandage. And another attendant extend from above similarly.
Then the physician should align the bone until he restores it to the best shape possible.
If there are splinters in the fractured bone, then try to restore each fragment to its place with your skill.
However, if a separate splinter is visible, and it pierces the skin, and you have no hope of restoring it: then cut over it and remove it according to the method we discussed in the preceding matters.
If however the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a special chapter about that, therefore take its treatment from there.
If, however, a hot abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then line a cloth with a cerate made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be of medium thickness and thinness: and bind the cloth over it with fine binding, until the abscess subsides; then remove the cerate, and apply a plaster prepared from mill dust with egg white. Then place splints, and let the splint placed over the fracture itself be a little wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most arrangements, whether the fracture be in one or two hollows simultaneously.
Then apply your binding to the fracture site more strongly and forcefully, and somewhat more binding above or below the fracture; or apply a binding somewhat softer than its previous binding, according to what was previously mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths wrapped around the fracture be soft, moist cloths; and let them not be hard, rough. And let the thread with which it is tied be of linen, properly, medium between thin and thick, as we have described.
And consider the limb and the ligament after days. If anything requiring correction should occur, such as itching which afflicts the limb, then it is necessary to bathe the limb with warm water, until that itching ceases, and to release the limb without binding until it rests; then reapply the binding.
If the binding has now softened, and the bone has now been removed, and similar things to these: then rectify all of it with your skill, and consider again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, because of the excessive tightness of the binding, then it is necessary to soften it a little, and to release it for a few days, until nourishment flows to it; then bind it again.
However, if nothing of what we have said happens to the sick person, then it is not necessary to loosen it, unless after twenty days, or thereabouts.
Then let the sick person's hand be suspended from his neck. And let his neck be level, and be guarded by his own skill from violent movements. And have him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All that is according to the dispositions of the complexions, and the dispositions of the strength.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of the Hand.
Know that fracture of the hand's pectines and the nodes of the fingers rarely occurs. And it does not occur to it unless by frequent attrition.
Therefore, when a fracture or attrition of the sole occurs, then it is necessary that the infirm person sit square; and in front of him there be a seat according to equality. Then place his hand upon it outstretched.
Then let the attendant extend the broken bones, and let the physician align them until they are joined with a good union.
Then it is necessary that he apply a plaster and lint, if a hot abscess does not occur. Then place a bandage over it according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped it in linen.
If however the fracture is towards the lower part of the sole of the foot, then make a sphere out of cloth, and instruct the sick person to hold it with his fractured foot upon it, then tie it with a long cloth.
And let the bandage be of leather, in which there is softness, so that the leather is applied to the sole of the foot; and tie it as is necessary.
If however the fracture is outwards, then it is necessary that you place a bandage over it, and another bandage from below on the sole; and let the hand be open and straight. Then apply a constriction, as the hand is revolved; and extend between the fingers with a bandage.
If a fracture happens to any of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be equalized as necessary. Then it should be bound to the sole; and if you wish, place a small, straight bandage on it so that the fracture is elevated and does not move.
If however the fracture is in the remaining fingers, such as the middle, and index, or ring, and little finger, then it should be equalized and bound to the healthy finger that follows it; or all should be bound according to their continuity; and this is better. Or place a small bandage upon it, as we said in the case of the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the disposition of the hot abscess itself: and apply with it what is necessary, when something from it happens, according to the repetition of its narrative.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured hip.
The hip bone is frequently fractured, and it is evident to the senses, because it is turned towards the interior and posterior part.
And its restoration is done thus, that a ligament is tightened above the fracture, and another ligament below the fracture. And the sick person should lie on their face.
Then the attendant should extend each ligament to its side according to equality; and this, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
However, if the fracture is near the root of the hip, then let one ligament be on the side of the crest, so that the extension falls upwards; and another ligament below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, then let the ligament be near the knee, so that the extension is downwards.
Then the physician should align the bone with both their hands, until they restore it according to the likeness of its natural shape; and the bone is joined by a good union.
Then it is necessary that you apply a plaster and a bandage, if no abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess does occur in it: then leave it for a few days, until the hot abscess subsides, then return to your treatment.
However, its bandage should be like this. It is necessary that you wrap a hard bandage, that is, a sling, over the fracture: and double it twice, or thrice: and let there be excess from it. Then bandage the leg until the heel reaches the root of the buttocks.
And introduce a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee to the lower parts, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of the two parts. Then wrap around the leg and thigh what remains over from the bandage.
Then place over the thigh, at the site of the fracture itself, splints, and place one splint over the shin bone.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the constriction is equal.
Then begin with a strong constriction in the middle, over the fractured area, with three or four wrappings. And the farther you move away from the fracture site with the bandage, the smaller, softer, and gentler your constriction should be.
Then pull on the two ends of the thread, which you inserted between the thigh and leg. Tie then with both ends the three splints that are above. Then proceed with the two ends of the thread downwards, until you reach the ankle. And with both ends tighten also the ends of the splints on the other side, so that the bandage does not move from its place.
Then release the tightening over it, while it remains, so that no corrosion, nor abscess, nor swelling, and things similar to them, occur to the limb. If anything of that sort happens. Therefore hurry with its removal, and rectify whatever happened from it entirely, according to what we have said many times.
If truly there is a piece of bone from the bone that pierces, then it is necessary, that you equalize it, if it is possible for you. And if not, split over it, and remove it: and treat it with that which preceded it is recalled, until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is tightened, besides which are added splints, as we said in the aid and arm.
However, in the restoration of it, thus fractured, lameness does not happen to the sick person. And if it is restored alone, besides which the other leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that he whose it is limp always.
And know that the hip is tightened in fifty days, or a little is added, or a little is diminished: all of that is according to the diversity of complexions and other dispositions.
Concerning the restoration of the fractured kneecap.
Know that the kneecap rarely suffers fracture, but it often suffers attrition.
If however a fracture occurs in it, then it is only either a fissure or a hole in its parts. And it is both with a wound and without a wound. And one stands over the whole thing with feeling.
And its restoration is such that the parts of it which are broken are equalized with the fingers until they are brought together and joined as much as is possible by adjustment, ease, and skill.
Then place a plaster of sesame flour, or mill flour, with egg white: and place a round pad on top of it, if you need it: and bind it with a suitable bandage over it.
Then consider all the treatments which we have described for other fractures: such as a hot abscess, and similar things: so that you oppose every accident with what is suitable to it, until it is healed.
