Concerning the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick, and is called the Cruris, and the other thin, and is called the Focile.
And the same kinds of fractures occur in both of them as occur in the two bones of the arm: and because of this, its restoration is carried out, just as the restoration of the arm is equally, and the operation is one.
If the two bones are fractured together, the leg turns in all directions.
And if the thinner bone is fractured, the leg turns toward the front.
And if the thicker bone is fractured, you find it lower: and it is among those that are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, and alignment, and the ligature of splints according to that equally.
However, if the fracture of the leg is a foul fracture, having much bleeding, let the extension be less, and the easing of your treatment gentler.
And in the leg there is something more about the operation than the arm: and it is, that when you have aligned the splints, and completed all your operation, then take two strips of pine wood, such as is used in the construction of the roofs that are placed under the boards; and they are made from palm leaves; and choose those of small thickness, and let them not be small; and let their length be according to the length of the leg, from the knee down.
Then wrap each one in a cloth in two folds along its length. And place one part of the leg on one side, and the other on the other side: and let them be from the knee down to the foot.
Then tie the two strips in three places from the two ends and the middle. For when the constriction is forbidden, the leg declines to the right and left, and stands in good posture.
And now indeed let a cradle of wood be administered according to the length of the leg, and let the leg be placed in it, so that it preserves it from movement.
And very much indeed it is necessary that you do that in a fracture when there is a wound with it properly.
Then consider the leg every day, and strive with that final effort against the abscess, or swelling, or any other remaining ones. If therefore anything from those happens, then oppose it with that which is necessary, until it is healed, if God wills.
Of the fracture of the bones of the Foot and Toes.
Indeed, a fracture of the heel bone does not happen completely.
However, fractures of the bones of the foot sometimes occur, and fractures also rarely occur in the toes; and they do not occur to them except for attrition in many dispositions.
If a fracture of the bones of the foot occurs, and you see those bones raised up above one another, then let the infirm person place their foot on prepared ground, as if walking.
Then stand you, and place your foot on that which is raised from those bones; afterwards press it, or tread on it; and straighten it, until it returns to its place.
Then place a plaster, and linen over it; and place a small board under the sole of the foot, with two flat ends.
Then bind it with a fitting bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths, and other things it needs.
When therefore three or four days have passed, then loosen the bandage. For you will find the bones straightened, whether they are broken or dislocated. Therefore, the operation on them is as you see.
If, however, some fingers are broken, then restore them and straighten them according to what I have told you concerning the restoration of fingers of the hand. Then place a splint along the length of the finger, and let it be slightly wider than it; then place beneath the foot this board that I have told you about, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
If, however, what is broken of the bones is two, or three, or more, then place a splint of its appropriate size over each finger, wrapped in linen cloth; and bind the board to the lower foot; and let it stand upon one head of the board outside the foot's belly, so that it holds with good retention.
And it befits you, so that you do not forget, to consider all that I have said to you in the rest of the fractures from accidents that we have mentioned; and oppose every accident with what is appropriate.
Concerning the fracture of the woman's vulva, and the rib bone, and the man's penis.
When the woman's vulva is broken, then make her sit square. Then incline her towards the part of her back a little, and let it be held back.
Then let the midwife fill her vulva with wool, until it fills it: and let it become in the vulva like a sphere.
Then let the woman be shaken, and raise her back gradually. For that wool comes out, until it becomes before the door of the vulva like a sphere, and the broken opening returns.
Then place a cushion on her back. Therefore, when she wishes to urinate, remove the wool with ease, then let her urinate: and return it according to the manner in which you first introduced it: then let her return to her first cushion.
Let her do that for seven days, or thereabouts, for the opening itself is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and tie a reed cannula over its opening, and introduce the whole bladder into her vulva. Then blow into the cannula with force, until the bladder inflates within the vulva, for the rupture is thereby restored.
Then let it be filled with wool, and let it stand for several days, according to what we have narrated, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the penis of a man, or a woman, is fractured, then administer in its restoration, and its equalization, that which we have narrated in the case of the coccygeal bone.
And the rectum is not concealed in these nearby fractures, which rarely occur, especially in someone with some skill or experience.
And understand well my book, for the fracture itself signifies to you in many situations the correct way to restore it and to constrict it: understand therefore.
However, when the penis of a man is fractured, take the throat of a goose, and insert the penis into it. Then place over it a bandage of cloth, and let it be wrapped and left for three days, or thereabouts, and it will be healed, if God wills.
Concerning the restoration of fractured bones when there is a wound.
It is fitting for one who has a fracture with a wound, especially if it is large, like a fracture of the hip bone, or of the arm bone, or similar to it, that you hurry and bleed him immediately; if the conditions for bloodletting are as we have previously stated.
If the wound is flowing blood, then it is necessary that you hurry to cauterize it, so that you powder the crushed zegi over it, if you do not have another remedy available.
Then begin restoring the fracture on the same day, and do not postpone it if an abscess has not developed.
If however a hot abscess has developed, then postpone its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. And do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you would cause him to suffer harm.
If however the broken bone is protruding above the skin, then it is necessary that you try to reduce it, and align it with your hands with ease and a small extension.
If however its extension and alignment with your hands is not prepared, then reduce it with this instrument. And it is an instrument made of iron, whose length is the amount of seven or eight fingers, and its width is according to the size of the wound. And because of that, it is necessary for the physician to take from them three or four, according to the quantity needed for that treatment in every kind of fracture.
And let it be round, and let it have a small thickness so that it is not doubled upon compression on it during the operation; and let it be sharp at the ends, having a margin on its extremities. And let its upper part be of a thickness from its midpoint down to the lower part.
And this is its shape.
It is necessary in this operation that its sharp curved end be placed on the projecting end of the bone, and to drive it with it in one turn, until when the bone returns and is equalized with some equalization.
Therefore, try to equalize the ends of the fracture with each other.
If its fractured ends are slender, and the instrument does not grasp them well, then it is necessary to saw the end of that bone until the instrument has a hold on it.
If however you cannot reduce them with what we have described at all, then cut it with whatever of the cutting instruments we mentioned is suitable for it, or saw it with one of the saws in any way possible for you.
Then smooth that which remains on the bone from roughness and small splinters.
Therefore, when the bone is reduced, and the infirm person finds intense pain hurting him after its reduction, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Do therefore that. For you confer great aid to the infirm person when your restoration of the bone is completed.
Then immerse a cloth in astringent, black wine. And properly if it is in summer. And do not place a cauterized piece of cloth, nor anything containing oil, on the wound: so that you do not cause putrefaction and corruption in it.
Then administer the splints at the hour of completion of your restoration of the bone. And leave the wound exposed, so that you cut a hole with scissors in the bandages according to the size of the wound.
And be careful with every precaution not to bind the wound with the fracture. For many times foolish physicians have done this, and have caused their patients either death, or corruption, or zoche, that is, pus. And let your constriction be soft, gentle, different from the constriction of the other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or is a large wound, and you fear some bad accidents that we have mentioned, and find pain in a suspended place, then you should not place splints on it. And make wrappings for them from hard cloths in the place of splints, and bind it with them.
When, however, it is after one day or two days, and you see the wound beginning to generate pus in it, then remove the cloth that you placed upon it with wine. Then administer bandages and ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds, such as the tetrapharmacon ointment and similar ones.
And it is fitting for you to loosen the bandage. And consider the wound every day morning and evening, until it is consolidated and healed.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared so that pus runs from it downwards with ease.
If many days pass over the wound, and it does not consolidate, nor is pus separated from it: then know that there are small bones' afflictions there. Therefore it is necessary that you examine the wound with probes. However, remove those of those afflictions that are separated, and extract them. And those of them that are not separated, and pierce the limb, and cause pain to occur, try to cut them out, and remove them by every means possible to you.
If a wound experiences putrefaction, or festering, that is, an ulceration, or some other species of corruption and putrefaction: then it is necessary to apply to each of them with that which suits it from the treatment, of which the description precedes in its chapter.
And in that on which my discourse should stand, and to which you should direct your mind when a bone is fractured and protrudes from the limb, such as the hip bone and the kneecap, and similar large bones, you should not attempt to pull it back, for often death results from that; rather, leave it until it becomes putrid.
For perhaps it will fall out by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then, therefore, treat the wound if it seems to you; and if not, leave it.
Concerning the treatment of a swelling at the site of a fracture.
This swelling frequently occurs at the site of healing fracture. Especially if it is near the joints: and the shape of the limb is disfigured by it. And perhaps it prevents the limb from its natural function.
Therefore, if the swelling is recent, administer to it medicines that have astringent properties, such as aloe, and frankincense, and myrrh, and sarcocolla, and acacia, and similar things to them.
So, take some of these, or all of them: and mix them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar: and place them on the swelling in linen, and bind them tightly over it.
And leave the bandage, and do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and apply another, until the swelling is removed.
Or bind over it a lead plate properly. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from the limbs.
However, if the swelling has already turned to stone, and it is tight, and necessity urgently demands its removal: then split over it from above, and cut away the protruding excess; or scrape it with certain scrapers, until it is removed; and treat it until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment when it is restored, and a limb remains after it in a delicate state, not according to its nature.
When a fractured bone is restored, and a limb remains after it delicate, weak: then it is not so unless due to many causes. One of which is, the multitude of loosening of the ligaments and their ligation not according to what is proper; or because of an excess of the tightening of the ligaments, until the nourishment is prevented from flowing to the limb; or because of an excess of embrocation at an inappropriate time; or because of a lack of blood in the body of the sick person and his weakness.
And the treatment of that is, to nourish the sick person and to make his body gentle, until the blood multiplies within him. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and gladness upon him.
Then place pitch upon the limb, so that the pitch attracts much nourishment to it. And assiduously embrocate it with warm water, until the nourishment flows, and it returns to its natural form.
Concerning the treatment of fractured bones when they are restored crookedly.
When, in a limb already restored after its healing, crookedness and prominence of the fractured bone, or a knot, occur; and the form of the limb is marred thereby, yet the limb is not hindered from its natural function; then you should not accept the opinion of one who thinks that the limb should be broken again.
And many foolish physicians and surgeons have already done that in our land.
And this operation is indeed highly reprehensible, leading to death or great fear, so that it may be deadly.
But if the lameness and knotting are recent, then it is necessary to soak it in lukewarm water in which softening herbs have been boiled, such as marshmallow leaves and its root, and melilot, and similar things. And a plaster should be made with a softening plaster, such as a well-made diachylon.
Or take the mucilage of marshmallow root, and mix it with hen's fat or sesame oil, and plaster it with that.
Or take fat figs and grind them with pigeon dung, and similar medicinal substances that are said to lessen consolidation.
And sometimes the knotting is loosened with diligent, long-standing, gentle rubbing, which is done with wet hands. And movement of the limb should be administered to every part at all hours.
If, however, twisting has already occurred, and it has become strong, and turned into stone, and the necessity for its treatment with iron has arisen: then it is necessary that it be cut above it, and the continuity of the bone be loosened, and that which is superfluous from the knotting or bone be cut away with fine incisions; and that ease be administered to it with care and diligence.
Concerning speech on Dislocation.
Dislocation is the departure of any joints from their place. Therefore it is prohibited from its movement, and the shape of the limb is disfigured. And it causes intense pains and severe injuries to the afflicted.
Therefore, when any dislocation occurs, one must hasten to restore it; nor should one delay it at all.
For if delayed, the place becomes suppurated, and perhaps the reduction of the dislocation as well.
Therefore, it should not be moved or extended during the time of its suppuration. Because it often causes spasms and painful injuries.
But when that happens, then one should hasten to perform phlebotomy on the patient. Then let it be left alone until the abscess subsides.
Then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil. Then it should be reduced gently.
And every limb should be treated in such a way that it returns to its proper place.
I have indeed already arranged the chapters on dislocations, even as preceded by those on fractures, from the upper part of the body down to its lower part.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws are rarely dislocated, except rarely.
And the dislocation of both occurs according to one of two methods. For either they are moved from their places by a small displacement, and are softened a little. Or they are dislocated completely, until they are softened to the chest area, until the sick person's saliva flows, and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is involved with the speech.
However, when its dislocation is small, then it returns in many dispositions by itself with little difficulty.
But if it is a complete, full dislocation, then it is necessary that its reduction be administered quickly, and not delayed at all; and it is that the minister should hold the sick person's head; and the physician should insert the thumb of one of his hands into the root of the dislocated jaw between his mouth, if the dislocation is on one side. Or he should insert two of his thumbs simultaneously, if the dislocation is on two sides. And the remaining fingers of his hands should be outside, with which he should steady it.
And instruct the sick person to clean their jaws and allow them to chew on every side. And the physician should steady the jaw and push the jaw until it returns to its place.
If its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated simultaneously, then apply vaporization with hot water and oil until the reduction of both becomes easy:  nor delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
When they return and are steadied, and the sick person's mouth is closed and does not soften, then place pads of cloth, with a cerate made of wax and rose oil, upon them.
Then bind it with ease with soft bandages.
And let the sick person's sleep be on their back, and let their head be supported between two pillows, so that it does not move to the right and left, nor try or be forced to chew anything; rather, let their food be a gentle drink, until when the pains are removed, and the jaw is tied: then let them eat what they wish, and let it be administered with ease.
Let them not labor over the operation of their mouth at eating and drinking and yawning until the jaw is set and healed.
If the reduction of the two jaws becomes difficult, when they are dislocated at any time and do not return to their places, then fevers and persistent sweating often result, and perhaps the sick person's bowels loosen, and perhaps they vomit pure bile.
Therefore, when you see that, then know that it is destruction, and often the one who suffers this dies within ten days.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the humerus.
The collarbone indeed does not dislocate from the inner side, because of its connection with the chest. And it sometimes dislocates outward, and that is evident to the senses.
And its restoration is that the sick person lies on their back, and extends their arms. Then grasp the place with a strong squeezing of your palm, for it will return.
Then place a plaster and cushions over it, and bind it.
However, its extremities that follow the elbow and are continuous with it do not dislocate, except rarely. If however it dislocates on any day, then it is necessary that it be reduced and put in place according to what we have said, and as is prepared for you.
Then place a plaster and cushions over it, and a bandage. And command the sick person to adhere to tranquility and rest until healed.
And with this same treatment, the humerus extremity is reduced when it is also separated from its place.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the humerus.
Know that the humerus is not dislocated except in three ways.
One of which is that it is dislocated to the side of the armpit inferiorly. The second is that it is dislocated to the side of the chest. And perhaps it is dislocated to the upper part of the Humerus; and that happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated posteriorly, because of the scapula. And it is not dislocated anteriorly, because of the nerves.
And very often it is dislocated and emerges downwards towards the pubic region, especially in those whose flesh is scant. Because it emerges quickly in them, and enters quickly.
However, in those whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite; namely, because it emerges with difficulty, and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps in some men, a blow or a fall occurs, and the humerus becomes inflamed with a hot abscess, and it is thought, because of this, that it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, you must test it, until you stand on the certainty of it, and then proceed in its treatment.
And dislocation is recognized when it is turned towards the armpit: and distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a healthy humerus. For you find a clear difference among them, and you find a concavity in the head of the humerus, and below the armpit, when touching, the head of the humerus seems like an egg; and the sick person cannot bring their hand to their ear, nor move it in all ways.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest, or upwards: then you find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easy when it is recent, or the patient is a child.
And its reduction is for the attendant to lift their hand upwards. Then place your thumbs under their armpit, and lift the joint with force upwards to its place; and let the attendant lift their hand and extend it upwards. Then let him place it below, for it will return quickly.
However, if it does not return by what we have said, and there have been many days since the dislocation occurred, then it is necessary for the patient to bathe in warm water, and an embrocation to soften and soothe should be administered, such as if the roots of marshmallow, fenugreek, and melilot are boiled in water, and administered.
Then let the patient lie on their back; and let a sphere and wool be placed under their armpit, which should be of a degree between softness and tightness.
Then let the physician place their heel on the sphere, and lift the head of the humerus with force, and pull the patient's hand downwards.
And if you wish, reduce it in this way: and let a man taller than the patient be present, standing on the side, and let him insert his arm under the patient's armpit, and lift their armpit upwards until the patient hangs suspended in the air. And another attendant should pull the patient's hand downwards towards their abdomen. However, if the patient is light, then it is necessary to suspend something else with him to weigh him down; for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also reduced by another method: and it is that a long piece of wood, whose head is round in shape, like the sleeve of a mortar, should be fixed to the ground; which should not be thick, nor thin. Then, after soft cloths are placed on the head of the wood, the patient is placed beneath it, standing along the length of the wood.
Then his hand is to be extended downwards on one side, and his body extended on the other side with strength; for the joint returns to its place quickly.
If his reduction is difficult by all that we have said, then use this treatment: and it is that you should take wood whose length is the size of two arms, and its width the size of four fingers, and its thickness the size of two fingers; to which there should be a round head, so that its entry into the depth of the tendon may be facilitated, according to this form.
Then soft cloths are tied over the round head, so that the wood does not injure the patient.
Then place it under the patient's tendon, so that the hand or arm is extended along the wood downwards; and tie the wood to the support and arm and hand.
Then place the arm on a step of a ladder across its width, and extend the hand downwards, and let the rest of the body hang from the other side. The joint immediately enters.
When, therefore, its entry is completed in whatever way is possible, then it is necessary to place a sphere of equal size made of wool under the tendon. Then place the prepared plaster, made of mill dust with frankincense and egg white, over the entire shoulder, as it revolves from above.
Then bind tightly under the tendon with a suitable bandage; and let the ligament be wrapped around the plaster from above; and bind his hand to his neck, and let it hang, and let his hand not be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary that little food be given to the sick person, until the limb is strengthened; because this is quicker for its healing.
Then, after seven or five days, a larger one should be put on, and exercised with movement. If therefore it is strengthened and does not soften, then it is now healed.
If however the joint is dislocated many times, because of the moisture that happens to it, or because of another cause, then it is necessary that a cautery with three holes be administered to it, according to what has been stated in the chapter on cautery.
If all this is done, and the ligament loosens after seven days, and the joint does not strengthen, and the plaster and constriction are applied to it repeatedly, and it does not strengthen, and it falls, and cannot lift itself upward: then know that its nerves that are to the head of the humerus are already cut, or stretched, or softened: and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
The reduction of a dislocation towards the breast and nipple, or to the rear, is done with pressing and stretching with the hands until it returns, and the remaining constriction and treatment are administered to it until it is healed.
If, after healing, heaviness occurs in the limb, and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use the bath many times, until that heaviness is lessened, and it returns to its first natural state.
Concerning the treatment of elbow dislocation.
Know that the elbow joint dislocates with difficulty, and similarly returns with difficulty; and indeed it dislocates to all parts, and especially to the front, or to the back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you, because it is evident to the eye and to the touch, according to whatever form it is dislocated. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, it is clearly evident to you. For the joint is hollowed out, and cannot double the arm, nor touch its shoulder with it.
And it is necessary that you hasten and reduce the dislocation promptly, before a hot abscess occurs in it.
For if a hot abscess occurs in it, its reduction becomes difficult, and perhaps it will not be completely healed, especially when it is toward the back. For it is worse than all the types of dislocation, and causes them more severe pain: and often death descends with it.
And its restoration, when it is one of those that can be reduced, is that his hand should be extended with two hands, and his arm be stretched out, and the physician's hands be above and below the elbow, and he should push the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the root of his palms, until it returns to its place.
Or truly the dislocation is to the front: then it returns in such a way that he doubles his hand with his hand, until he strikes with the root of his palm the elbow that is dislocated.
If the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer a vigorous, very strong extension; and it is that two assistants should extend the arm, and two assistants should also hold it so that it is not moved during the extension.
Then the arm is to be turned in every direction after a long doubled cloth, or a broad bandage, is wrapped around his hand.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, let him anoint it with oil, so that it may be one of those things that assist in the lubrication of the joint with ease. Then he should drive the joint with a strong impulse until it returns.
And after its reduction, it is necessary to place a plaster upon it, containing astringency and desiccation with egg white; and it should be bound with a suitable bandage. And let the arm be suspended from the patient's neck, and leave it for a few days.
Then it should be released. If therefore the joint is strengthened in its place, then loosen the bandage from it, and leave it.
And if you see the joint not being properly bound, then reapply the plaster and bandage, and leave it for a few days, until it is properly bound. Then release it.
However, if after the reduction of the joint, heaviness occurs to it, and its disposition is in motion: then administer moistening in bathing, and gentle friction, and compression, until it is lined, or place upon the joint the iliac, that is, the fat rump of a ram. Then it should be bandaged, and leave it on it day and night. Then remove it, and immerse it in a bath. When therefore it sweats, then rub the joint with moderate friction. Then repeat upon it the application of aleliati once, and a second time, and a third time with each bath entry, until it is eased.
And if you wish, place upon it cow dung, moist, warmed with butter, and bind it over it.
Do it several times. For it is thus eased, and returns to its condition.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of Almahassem.
The joint between the arm and hand, Mahassem, is frequently dislocated.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. On the contrary to the other joints. However, it is necessary that speed be used in reducing its dislocation in the hour in which it is dislocated, before the place becomes suppurated: or a hot abscess occurs there.
And the reduction of the dislocation in it is that you place the weakened hand of Mahassem upon a table, and let the attendant extend his hand; and let the physician place his palm upon the eminence of the joint, and push it until it returns.
However, it is necessary to consider if the palm has already receded to the back of the hand; then let the weak person place the back of his hand upon the table at the extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding to the back of the hand, then let the position of his hand be inwardly upon the table, that is, the inner part of his hand be placed upon the table, so that the hand of the physician falls upon the very eminence of the joint.
If therefore it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then bind it with a plaster that quiets the abscess, and leave it, and do not repeat it.
For if it does not tolerate it, nor can you accomplish its reduction, after the days have passed, nevertheless the joint remains according to its twist; and it does not harm the weak person anything, except that the hand is softened and cannot grasp anything; then know that the nerves have been severed or bruised.
Therefore there is no skill in it, unless it is bound with a cautery near its location. And perhaps it does not contribute anything.
When therefore the Almahassem does not return, then place upon it the plaster which we have narrated. Then it should be bound and left for five days; afterwards it should be loosened and left, and the hand exercised.
If, however, its movement is deprived, and something serious happens to it: then soothe it with heated water and friction several times, until it is soothed.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the fingers.
When a finger is dislocated from any part, or a finger is dislocated towards the back of the palm, or its belly: then straighten the finger, and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it towards the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend it, and straighten it, until it is made straight on that day of its own.
Therefore, when it is night, bind it according to the narration itself: and do not cease to loosen it by day: and exercise it with movement: and bind it at night.
Do that for several days, until it is strengthened.
And similarly do this with it, if it is dislocated to the palm of the hand: and bind it to the part itself, and do your first operation with it, until it is healed.
And similarly do this with it when it is dislocated to the other parts.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the spinal column.
When a dislocation of the spinal column, or of the neck, occurs completely, or when many vertebrae are removed from their places, then there is no cure for it, since death hastens for the infirm.
And a sign of this is that the infirm's excretion comes out involuntarily, nor can he hold it: and many times certain members of his, or his two legs, or his two arms, or one of them, soften, or a vertebra is removed from its place.
For often it is removed, for often their removal occurs in four directions.
Therefore, that which is removed to the back is called a hump.
However, the treatment for it is that you should consider then, if the hump occurs from childhood: then there is no treatment for it, nor is there any healing from it at all.
However, in that which happens from a fall, or blow, or similar things to these, the ancients have already spoken of methods of treatment at length. Of which very little returns with profit.
I, however, have now abbreviated it, of which little excuses the much of its proximity to my intention: and by my exposition I have exposed it, and formed its instrument in a different manner from that in which they showed it and explained it.
I say therefore, that in a hump that occurs on the anterior part, there is no remedy, nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which is made on both sides also.
And it is not cured from them, unless that which occurs on the back properly, with what I narrate: and it is, that the infirm person be stretched out on their face on a level platform near a wall; and moist straw be spread under it, so that his chest is not injured.
Then let a straight piece of wood be fixed in a hole in the ground, at the end of the platform, approximately at his head; and another piece of wood, approximately at his feet, at the other end of the platform; and let an attendant hold the wood, and it not be firmly fixed in the hole. And another attendant hold the other.
Then wrap around the head of the infirm person, and beneath his armpits, a soft, firm bandage: and extend the end of the bandage to the wood which is at his head; and tie it to it.
Then tighten another bandage over his armpits, and over his knees, and at his ankles. Then gather all the bandages, and tie them to the other piece of wood which is at his feet.
Then let every attendant extend the bandage towards the piece of wood, and do not remove the two pieces of wood from their places where they are fixed; however, do not let them be fastened, as we said.
And let the physician place his hands upon the vertebra with strength, until it returns to its place; or let him place a board upon it. Then he ties his feet to the board until it returns.
If however it does not return by this treatment, then take a board whose length is approximately three arms. And hollow out a wall, of which we said is near the sick person, a place into which the end of the board may enter. Then place the middle of the board upon the vertebra. And let the physician place his feet upon the other end. And pull tightly until the vertebra is constricted, and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, operate with the lathe that is wrapped with your hand: and it is, that you fix in the earth, near the head of the sick person, on the back of the footstool, two pieces of wood, whose length is equal to one arm's length: and near his head on the back of the footstool two pieces of wood.
And let the length between each piece of wood be one palm: and now a hole has been made in every place, through which the lathe runs: and let all the wood be firmly fixed in the earth, so that it does not move at all.
And let a round piece of wood be inserted: and the lathe revolves the bandage in the two holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its end is a hole in which a piece of wood is fixed, whose length is a palm's width along with that which revolves: and in the other two pieces of wood is likewise the same.
Then tighten the bandages that you have tied around the chest of the sick person with the lathe that is near his head; and the bandages that you have tied around his legs with the lathe that is near his feet.
Then let a servant stand near each lathe, who shall turn his hand with the cord with which the lathe revolves: and the physician shall adjust the Spondyle, as we have said.
This is the form of the lathe, which follows.
Then, after the Spondyle has returned and the place is adjusted, you should apply a drying plaster with egg white. Then, linen. Then place a strip of wood on top of the plaster, whose width is three fingers, or thereabouts. And its length should be the amount that covers the place of the hump and over the healthy part of the Spondyle.
Then bind it with the bandage we mentioned. And let the sick person use mild nourishment until they are healed.
If something of a protrusion remains in the place at the end of healing, then it is necessary that medicines that soften and soothe be administered, along with the administration of the board that we described for a long time. And now a lead plate is administered on it.
And sometimes a protrusion occurs in the final part of the spine of the back, and it is thought to be a dislocation, and that is the bone which now protrudes. Therefore, it is not fitting to use this medicine on it: for it might, perhaps, cause death.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated shoulder.
Know that dislocation of the shoulder joint, and the joint of the humerus, happens only as a complete dislocation; and what happens to the other joints as a result of a small displacement and concavity does not happen to them.
And indeed the shoulder joint is dislocated in four directions. Which is, because it is dislocated forward and backward.
And it is most often dislocated inward; and it is little dislocated forward and backward.
And the treatment for its inward dislocation is as follows: when you join the sick leg with the healthy one, it is longer; and the knee of the sick person is more prominent than the healthy one, and the sick person cannot bend their foot at the groin; and the place following the groin is suppurated with a hot abscess, because the head of the thigh has now reached there.
And the sign of someone who has experienced an outward dislocation is that their symptoms are the opposite of these symptoms.
And the sign of someone who has experienced a forward dislocation is that they extend the healthy leg to its full extent; however, they do not bend it without pain in the knee; and if they try to walk, they cannot move forward on it; and their urine is retained, and their groin is suppurated, and in walking, their heel strikes the ground.
And the sign of someone who has experienced a backward dislocation is that they cannot extend the knee, nor can they bend it before bending the groin; and their leg is also shorter than the other; and the groin is softened, and the head of the thigh is at the location of the ilium.
The reduction of this type of dislocation, however, is to consider if the dislocation is old, already established in the person with it. Then, if you attempt its reduction, and it remains in its position, there is no cure for it at all. Therefore, it is not necessary to attempt a complete presumption for him.
However, in the case of one whose dislocation is recent, and is of one of the four types of dislocation, then hasten to attempt to move the joint, and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left; for perhaps it will return, and not require further treatment.
If, however, it does not return in this way, then it is necessary to prepare a strong assistant, and let him extend his leg from below; either with his hands, or with a bandage that is tied above his leg above the knee; and another assistant should extend his hand from above, so that he inserts his hands under his kneecaps. Then, he should tighten with a gentle bandage over the root of the thigh, and hold the other end of the bandage.
And let his extension be either from the anterior part of the groin to the part of the crotch, or from the posterior to the part of the back.
And let the extension of all of them be simultaneous, until the sick person is lifted with his body from the ground, and remains hanging.
And this type of extension is a common type of the four types.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for every type that I speak of the proper cure.
In the proper reduction of it, when the dislocation is towards the interior, it is necessary that the sick person be laid on his healthy side. Then he shall place a bandage over the root of the hip, in that which is between the head of the hip and the place that is under the groin. Then extend the bandage upwards from the groin towards the higher part of the body, towards the part of the crotch.
Then let another strong attendant take his arms, and lessen the bulky place of the diseased hip, and extend it outward with forceful extension. For now it returns to its place.
And this type is easier than the other types of treatment, by which this limb is reduced.
However, if it escapes from under you, and does not obey you in entering with this type of treatment at all: then it is necessary that two feet of the diseased limb be tied together with a strong, gentle bandage, over the two heels, and over the two knees: and the length of each should be of the measure of four fingers from its partner: and the diseased leg should be extended by two fingers more than the other.
Then let the diseased person be suspended over a wooden beam in the house, and its length from the ground should be the measure of two arms.
Then command a strong youth to secure the head of the hip; and let another youth be suspended with the diseased person; and let another youth, securing the hip with strength, propel it, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
However, in its own return, when the dislocation is to the outside, it is necessary that the sick person lie on a raised platform, according to what we have narrated in the case of the one with a hump. And let a bandage be tightened over his diseased leg, and over his chest.
Then let two wooden beams be placed, one at his feet, and the other at his head. Then let a further wooden beam be placed in the middle of the platform, firmly fixed; upon which let damp cloths be wrapped, so that it does not harm the sick person; and let the beam be between his hips, so that it is not pulled downwards during extension.
Then let each attendant pull on their respective side, and let the physician adjust the reduction with his hand.
If therefore it yields to the return, it will be good.
And if not, place it upon the board itself, and press it, just as we have said in the case of curvature, evenly. However, it is necessary that the sick person lie on their healthy side.
And when the dislocation is towards the front, then it is necessary that the sick person's leg be pulled quickly; and it is according to this same account on the plinth; and let the physician place the palm of his right hand on the sick person's groin. Then grasp it with the other hand, and with that grasp put it extended downwards to the part of the knee.
And if the dislocation is backwards, then it is not necessary that the sick person be extended downwards and raised from the ground; rather, it is necessary that he be placed upon a hard object, as is appropriate, as also happens if his ankle is dislocated outwards, according to what we said about his lying on his two ankles; and it is on his face, and the ligaments extended, according to what we said recently.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a board also on the place to which the joint emerges, when the reduction is completed as it should be.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you: and it is that you extend the two legs of the sick person; and when you see them equal, and the sick person tightens his leg, and extends it without excuse, then know that the limb has now returned as it should; then join the two thighs, and put on a plaster, and bind with a bandage, that is, a roller: so that the ankle does not move to any part: and let the sick person adhere to rest for three or four days.
Then loosen the bandage and plaster, and compare the leg with the other; then if you see them equal in condition, then know that the dislocation is now firm; therefore dismiss the sick person to walk.
And if you see any softening, then return, and plaster it, and bind it according to your first binding: and leave it also for three days.
Then loosen it and make him slow in walking on both legs for a few days, until he is well strengthened.
Concerning the dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outwards, inwards, and downwards, that is, backwards.
And it is not dislocated forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you should instruct the sick person to apply his leg to his thigh. If however it does not adhere to the thigh: then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all the ways of its dislocation is that you should make the sick person sit upright, so that he may if possible extend his leg over it, and adhere your back to his face: and place his foot between your feet.
Then make his knee adhere to your palms, and place them straight between your fingers over his knee. Then apply with your palm the two sides of his knee with strength. And another attendant should extend his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh with ease, not with effort.
Then plaster it, and make the leg adhere to the thigh. Then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days. Then unbind them.
And let the sick person not use walking for several days, until he is healed.
If, however, its reduction is refused by you, then administer strong extension with bandages, of which the description preceded in the treatment of a sprain, until it returns.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated heel.
The heel sometimes is moved by a small displacement, and sometimes is dislocated completely. And its dislocation occurs either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen, protruding towards the side to which it is dislocated.
The treatment of its displacement is easy to restore: it is to gently extend it with the hands, and straighten it, until it returns to place.
In treating it when it is completely dislocated, it is necessary that you make the patient sit upright, and a strong assistant hold him firmly from the back of his spine, in the middle of it.
Then, hold his foot with your right hand at its upper part, and with your left hand at the lower part of his foot, near the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with the left hand twice.
Then extend with the left, and push the foot's sole with the right towards the leg without effort.
Do that twice, as we have described.
Then push the sole of the foot towards the leg a third time, and you pull the heel.
If therefore it returns in one or two times according to this method, and you see the foot straightened, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation upon it, because it returns.
If, however, its reduction is forbidden to you with what we have described, then place the sick person on his back on the ground, and fix a firmly planted stake in the ground, so that it falls between his thighs; and you have now wrapped a cloth around it, so that it does not harm the sick person.
Then let the attendant take his thigh; afterwards let another attendant extend the foot, either with their hands or with a bandage that ties above the ankle. Then let every attendant pull against the pull of their counterpart; and the stake should stand between the sick person's two thighs, restraining him so that his body is not pulled downwards during the extension.
Then the physician should straighten the dislocation with his hands, and another attendant should hold the healthy leg downwards. For the dislocation returns with speed.
Therefore, when the dislocation returns, and its return to health is clear to you, then place a plaster and a bandage, and firmly bind it with bandages, and bind the foot to the leg with a bandage.
And it is necessary that you beware or fear for the nerves that are above the posterior heel, so that the bandage is not too forceful on them and harms them.
Then leave it for two or three days. If the bandage softens, tighten it; then loosen it on the third or fourth day; and prohibit the sick person from walking for forty days. If, however, he attempts to walk before that period, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not loosen again, and be corrupted, and not receive subsequent healing.
If a hot abscess should occur to him, then it is necessary that you administer to it, in its allaying, that which our previous account in another place has described in the treatment, and a fomentation, until it is removed.
Concerning the dislocation of the toes of the foot, and its treatment.
It is necessary that what is dislocated from them be brought into its proper place by a slight extension without any effort; and indeed this is not difficult, rather it is easy.
If however the dislocation is in some of the dorsal knots of the foot, then it is necessary that the sick person place his foot on level ground, or on a board; and he should stand as if walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot upon that which projects from those joints. Then press it with your foot with force until it returns, and you see it now in its proper place, and no protuberance appears in the location.
Then place under the sole of his foot a board that will support the entire foot, having two ends. Then bind it with a suitable bandage for three days. Then loosen it, and have him rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and secured from reduction.
Concerning the types of dislocation with wound or fracture.
When something like that happens, and you try to cure it and restore it, death often results.  Therefore, it is not appropriate for anyone to presume to treat such a case unless they are skillful through extensive experience, compassionate, pious, slow, not bold, nor presumptuous. And they should initially use only medications that soothe hot abscesses, and leave the patient to divine judgment. Unless you see, or hope for, salvation from destruction with a gentle lessening of the illness, and in which something of hope appears. Therefore, attempt its reduction immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot abscess occurs.
If, therefore, the limb returns as desired, then administer a regimen to soothe the abscess, and treat the wound with whatever appropriate drying ointments are indicated.
If the dislocation is with a fracture, and separated bone fragments occur, then attempt their removal, and do in it what we have said in simple illnesses from what preceded in their locations.
And beware according to your ability, and guard yourself from entering the path of fear, as my previous teaching to you has prescribed.
For that is better for you before your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and more salutary to your body among those in your time.
